THE RED PAGE.
Let us Irrigate.
I “Bob R.” : You say “In defiance of the proof
it China, India, and Americ'a; and in the teeth of the
Irought losses in Australia, there are still Australian
dockheads who deride the profit of irrigation.” What
ve do deride is the practicability of irrigation, unless on
i small scale— e.g., Jemalong. China, India, and America
ire specially favoured in what Australia lacks—rivers.
Che presence of numerous large and inexhaustible
itreams makes irrigation a matter of comparative ease
md small expense. A chain of waterholes, such as the
darling, Lachlan and Murrumbidgee are in drought-
lime, renders irrigation on a national scale a matter of
mpossibility or else of such enormous expense as to
rutweigh the advantages. Only t’ other day a township
rear the junction of the Lachlan and Murrumbidgee
•ose in wrath to protest against the use of the waters of
;he Lachlan for irrigation, when it could not ■get.suffi-
jient for domestic purposes ! Yet there are only three
small pumping stations on that alleged river. Irrigation
is a magnificent thing, provided there is plenty of water ;
without that, it is only a dream of theorists and a
“catch-vote” for politicians.
[lt is precisely because Australia (compara-
tively speaking, and in some places; lacks rainfall and
rivers, that irrigation is forced upqn her. She may irri-
gate from rain-catchment or flood-catchment where rivers
are “ a chain of waterholes ” ; but she must irrigate to
conquer. J
W.H.C. t For those who misunderstand irriga-
tion the case of the Rio Pecos may be cited. This
stream, rising in the eastern slopes of the Rockies, flows
through parts of New Mexico and Texas that comprise a
desert almost as desolate and barren as the Sahara. Like
the other rivers of the south-western portion of U.S.A. —
■where the rainfall is less than in our back country—the
Pecos is a capricious stream, being one week so dry that
its bed can be crossed without wetting the feet, and'a
fortnight later a muddy torrent almost to thg tops of the
banks. The construction of a big weir has resulted in
the establishment of a prosperous irrigation colony.
People came over and secured the sand patches that the
Govt, called land. Two hundred thousand acres are now
supporting in comfort a community of 100,000 people, who
have 500,000 cattle, almost .as many sheep, maintain one
of the biggest sugar-beet factories in America, grow cotton,
fruit and other produce in sufficient quantities to excite
the liveliest competition of railroad companies. That
river, compared with our never-failing, snow-fed Murrum-
bidgee, is a storm-sewer.
THAT WE MAY BREED MEN
In contrast on the lower Murray are two tracts o f
250,000 acres each—one a sheep station (Chowilla); the
other an irrigation colony (Renmark). At the present time
the 2&0j000 acres of Chowilla carry 5000 (live thousand)
sheep, and there is the owner and his family, and the
staff necessary 1 to attend to 5000 sheep: On Renmark
there are 3000 acres under irrigation, maintaining in
comfort and under fairly agreeable conditions a com-
munity of 1000 pepple.
When the pastoralists of N.S.W. were confronted with
the problem of hand-feeding the remnants of their flocks
they were net long in discovering that under the most
economical system that could possibly bp devised—-and
some of them, it is said, started with ofie straw a head—it
took at least (id. worth of purchased fodder Jier week to
provide tfie barest starvation ration. The points
N. A. Gatenby’s Work demonstrates are that—(l), by
having a small patch under irrigation (it’s a poor
50,000-acre station on which one cannot find one
60-acre patch of soil of the quality and situa-
tion essential for intensive irrigation) the pastoralist
can always provide for the preservation of his “stud.”
(2) While it will cost £250 a week to keep the merest
spark of life glimmering in 10,000 rams and ewes with
makeshift stuff like Argentine lucerne, maize, straw,
and molasses, a man, by taking advantage of even so
limited a water supply as the Lachlan unfortunately is,
can provide the very best of fresh food and keep the
flock in fair condition at a twelfth of the monetary out-
lay.
Irrigation is certainty not “a magnificent thing"
where there is plenty of water. Among all the famine-
stricken creatures on the face of the earth the inhabi-
tants of irrigated India and China, with their great
rivers, probably are at the tail of the procession. On the
other hand, when the irrigator has to pay through the
nose for every gill of water that is measured out to him
as carefully as wine, and that is in Riverside, California
and in Arizona, and many parts of irrigated
irrigation is a prosperous enterprise, which enables the
farmer to live in comparative luxury.
THUS, FOR EXAMPLE
The only thing that is Worse than aridity for farm
and fruit crops is excessive moisture. A watering of 22,000
gallons per acre(= 1 inch rainfall) will make lucerne
grow nearly a foot in a week. A watering of 100,000
gallons will drown it. The “ chains of ponds ” referred
to by “ Bob R.” send into Lake Alexandrina in a dry
year some 424,000,000,000 cubic feet,'and in a normal year
three times '-as much. A cubic foot equals 50 miners
inches, and a miner’s inch of water flowing into 5 acres
of land equals a rainfall of 3 inches per month or 36
inches per annum—more than any crop, bar watercress,
requires. j ,
At Mount M‘Donald, on the head of Lachlan River, a
weir costing (roughly speaking) A 1 ,250,000 will impound
12,500,000,000 cubic feet of water. This quantity, allow-.
ing for an annual evaporation of, say 4ft., and even in a
dry season, when the local rainfall does not exceed 10
inches, will provide sufficient water to irrigate 250,000 or
more acres with ,12 inches per acre, and thus provide
moisture equal to a rainfall of 22 inches per annum—
which, for the Lachlan country, would mean an abso-
lutely good season for wheat, wool, and fruit. Against the
cost of that weir take the outlay of one, pastoraiist on the
Lachlan for last year’s for purchased
fodder to maintain about 100,000 sheep on about 100,000
acres. On 250,000 acres of evenly though lightly watered
lands on the Lachlan 250 farmers could grow wheat and
sheep and live in prosperity.
♦
There have been several interesting decisions
lately in Eng. Court of appeal. In Parry v. Moreing,
plaintiff’s right was upheld to bis work in an edition of
some British Museum MS. Defendant altered and im-
proved plaintiff’s work, instead of going to the originals ;
and the Court decided that, even in the matter of print-
ing national MS,, copyright held good to the extent of
the original authorship—in deciphering and annotating,
for example. (This decision seems to controvert that
of N.S.W. Equity Court, in 1898, regarding school-
books Wm. Brooks & Co. published and owned a Second
Reader. Upon that a commentary was found desirable ;
and Angus & Robertson and Brooks & Co. published
independently “ pupils’ guides ” based and dependent on
the Second Header. Brooks & Co. sued for infringement,
but their case was dismissed).
A King’s Bench decision was reversed by the Master
of the Roils on May U, in McQuire v. The Western
Morning News. Defendant had published a scathing
criticism of the acting of plaintiff’s dramatic company,
and a jury in the lower court gave him £lOO damages.
The higher court upheld the night of honest criticism.
On May 16 the same court decided the law as to copy-
right in photographs—thus. If you go to»a photographer
in the ordinary way, and pay him to make a picture of
vou you own the picture (t.e., the negative and all posi-
tives that, may he made), and he has no right to publish
reproductions of your picture without your consent.
But if vou are a celebrity, for example, and the photo-
grapher romes to you and desires to take your picture,
and vou permit him, then it will be inferred that copy-
right in the picture passes to the photographer unless
you make a definite agreement to the contrary.
BUGLE AGRA!
IV. .T.D.: The Celtic Renascence is in full flower
in Sydney. Eugene Ryan is teaching Irish at Redfern, and
Dr. O’Donnell delivered an impassioned speech in the
old tongue a little time ago. Also, the present writer
has been pegged to publish the English text of that
oldest and most famous of Jacobite songs, “ Shule Agra.”
The name of the author is not known for certain, but it
is suspected that he belonged to the noble Celtic clan of
O’Daley. The “ wild geese” referred to were the Irish
exiles who fled to France and Spain after the last defeat
of Sheamus Righ (James the Ring). I give the words in
A. P. Graves’s version, and the tune from the Gaelic
League’s journal —Au Claideamh Soluis.
SHULE AGRA.
IHs hair was black, his eye ivas blue,
His arm was stout, his word was true,
I wish in my heart I was with you,
Go-d’e-thu, mavourneen slaun.
Shule, shule, shule agra,*
Only death can ease my woe,
Since the lad of my heart from me did go,
God'e-thu, mavourneen slaun A
’T is oft I sat at my true love’s knee,
Many a fond story he told to me,
He told me things that ne’er shall be...
I sold my rock, I sold my reel;
When my flax was spun I sold my wheel,
To buy my love a sword of steel ...
But when King James was forced to flee,
The Wild Geese spread their wings to sea,
And bore ma bouchal far from me. ..
I saw them sail from' Brandon Hill,
Then down I sat and cried my fill,
That every tear would turn a mi 1 1.,.
I wish the King would return to reign,
And bring my true love back again ;
1 wish, and wish, but I wish in vain., ,
I ’ll dye my petticoat, I ’ll dye it red,
And round the world I ’ll beg my bread,
Till t find my love, alive or dead ...
Shule, shule, shule agra,
Only death can ease my woe,
Since the lad of my heart from me did go,
Go-d’e-thu, mavourneen slaun.
* Come, come, come, heart’s dearest 1
t Good-day, darling, and good luck (health).
POPULAR CONTROL OF THE PUBLIC-
HOUSE
Is the title of a pamphlet by Rowntree and
Sherwell (Edinburgh : Macniven and Wallace ; 3d.). R.
and S. are the most sensible, the most lucid, and the
most authoritative Eng. writers on the liquor question.
Here they summarise practical recommendations for the
U.K.
1. That the right of prohibiting the [liquor] traffic
shall be placed within the power of every locality.
2. That localities, where the right of prohibition is
not exercised, shall be at liberty to organise and control
the traffic, preferably through a company, as in Nor-
way, but always under the direct supervision of the
central government and the local licensing authority,
and only within clearly defined statutory limits.
3. That tbe -whole of the profits shall, in the first
instance, be handed over to a central State authority.
4. That the sole benefit that a locality in which the
trade is placed under public control shall receive from
the profits shall be an annual grant from the State
authority for the establishment and maintenance of
recreative agencies, the primary object of which shall
be to counteract the influence of the public-house.
Such grant to be in ratio to population, and not in
I ratio to profits earned.
s.—That similar grants shall be made to prohibition
areas, all inducement to continue the traffic for the
sake of the grants being thus effectually destroyed.
These conclusions might be accepted for Austra-
lasia—with the difference that municipal or other
public local authority should be preferred to a company.
Because : there would be greater publicity, less risk of
dishonest dealing ; and because individual and local cor-
porate responsibility would be more clearly seen, more
keenly felt, and the apparatus of direction would be sim-
plified. Continue R. and S.—■
The houses carried on under public control might
reasonably be subject to the following among other
conditions of management:—
fa)—Sales on credit to be prohibited.
(b)—ilo female bar-tenders to be employed.
(c)— No adventitious attractions, such as music, etc.,
to be associated with the sale of intoxicants.
(d)— Accommodation for clubs, sick benefit societies,
etc., no longer to be provided at the public-housdS ;
ample provision for these being made elsewhere (e.g.,
in buildings devoted to the counteracting agencies).
(e) —Every public-house to be open to full inspection
from the highway ; no screens or partitions to be used.
It is noteworthy that this reform was advocated by a
Select Committee of the House of Commons as far back
as 1834.
(f) —Back-door and side-door entrances (i.e., deors
opening into passages or courts not used as thorough-
fares) to be prohibited.
Agreed to every one of those recommendations.
I Drink is a blessing. Drink is a curse. It is impracticable
to abolish its U3e ; it is injudicious to attempt to abolish.
But to minimise its potential evils, to destroy actual
temptations to abuse it, every citizen should aid.
If only that chuckleheaded Canon Boyce with his
fictitious drink bill—(pardon this language: the clear
stupidity of “educated” people, people who should have
brains and brains to use their brains, is a revolting thing)
—if only the tribe of Maoriland women with fanatical
propaganda—would assimilate the conclusions of Rown-
tree and Sherwell, doubly proven bv tile facts and figures
of experience, we might hope to reform the drink traffic
in a single generation.
THE WIDOW.
Listen, that I may tell you what happened to a widow:
She lost her petticoat, and said that I took it.
Chorus—The ill-fated widow!
1 did not take it.
If perchance I took it, may I die the death of a thief t
May they take and hang me—on the tap of a cask.
Chorus —The ill-fated widow!
I did not take it.
If perchance I took it, may I die the death of a thief!
May Ibe hung—on cabbages ; may 1 fall into—a lettuce-
bed.
Chorus—The ill-fated widow !
I did not take it.
If perchance I took it, may I dw the death of a thief!
May they chain me with—a string of figs.
Chorus—The ill-fated widow !
I did not take it.
If perchance I took it, may I die the death of a thief l
May they pelt me with—shelled eggs ,
Chorus—The ill-fated widow !
I did not take it.
If perchance I took itmay I die the death of a thief l
May tny teeth break—in a fresh cheese-cake.
Chorus—The ill-fated widow !
I did not take. it.
—From Songs of Modern Grekcb.
THE SIN OF COMPROMISE
I ....The great mass of a generation of children
bom into a country, all those who have no more than
average intelligence and average moral qualities, will
accept the ostensible institutions of that country at
their face value, and will be almost entirely shaped and
determined by that acceptance. Only a sustained, under-
tone of revolutionary protest can prevent that happen-
ing. They will believe that precedence represents real
superiority, and they will, honour what they see honoured,
and ignore what they see treated as of no account.
Pious sentiment about Equality and Freedom will enter
into the reality of their minds as little as a drop of water
into the gredsy plate it has fallen upon.
There must be concessions; there must be conformities,
just as thbre must be some impurity in the'water we drink
and flaws in the beauty we give our hearts to, and that no
doubt is true. It is no reason why we should drink
sewage and kneel to grossness and base Stupidity. To
endure the worst because we cannot have the best is
surely the last word of folly. Our business as New Re-
publicans is not to waste our lives in the pursuit of an un-
attainable chemical purity, bqt to clear the air as much as
possible. Practicalethicsis.afterall, a quantitative science.
... It would be a small concession, I admit, a mere
personal self-sacrifice, to pretend loyalty, kneel and kiss
hands, assist at Coronation mummeries and all the rest of a
it, in order, let us say, to accomplish some great improve-
men of the schools in the country, were it hot for the fact
that all things must be done in the sight of the young,
that you cannot kneel to the King without presenting' a
kneeling example to the people, vyitbout becoming as
good a teacher of servility as though rod were servile to
the marrow. There lies the trouble. By virtue of this
reaction it is that the shams and ceremonies we may
fancy mere curious survivals, mere kinks and tortuosities,
cloaks and accessories to-day will, if we are silent and
acquiescent, be half-way to_ reality again ip the course of
a generation. To our children they are not evidently
shams ; they are powerful working suggestions. Human
institutions are things of life, and whatever lies still
rooted in the ground has the promise and potenev of
growth. It WiH tend perpetually to grow again according
to its nature, to it 3 old influence over the imagination,
the thoughts and acts f>l QUr children.—H. G. Wells, in
The Cosmopolitan.

Frank S. : Note also that The Century Dic-
tionary includes among its “typical extracts ” a definition
of boomerang. The “ Boomerang... can be made to
ricochet almost at will. It can be thrown in a curved
path, somewhat as a bowl can be ‘ screwed’ or ‘twisted,’
and it can be made to return to the thrower and strike
the ground behind him.” The notion of a boomerang
“ ricocheting ” along the ground, taken with the ineffec-
tive reference to its aerial performances, is misleading.

They were re-telling this story for Herbert
Spencer’s eighty-third birthday on April 27. Spencer used
to play billiards, and in his middle-age at bond. Athemeum
Club he seemed rather “ to fancy himself ” with the cue.
Once he proposed a game to a fellow member whom he
did not know, offering to give him points. The offer was
not accepted, and the game proceeded. Spencer gave a
miss in baulk; the stranger gave a miss under the
cushion ; Spencer played, but failed to score ; thestranger
ran out without giving Spencer another chance B, mw
his skill His revenge took this Johnsonian shape : sir,
said the philosopher, “ a certain proficiency in games of
skill is an indication of a well-balanced mind. Adroit-
ness such as you have just displayed is strong presump-
tive evidence of a mis-spent youth. ’

THE GIN GIN ESTATE FARMS
11,000 ACRES.
Will be cold by PUBLIC AUCTION, by order ef the Trmtaes in the settlement! of
SIB THOMAS McILWBAITH, Decerned.
THE GIN GIN ESTATE is situated on the Mt. Perry Railway Line about 23 miles
from Bundaberg, Queensland, about 12£ hours train from Brisbane, with a frontage of
over 3 miles to the railway line and about 3j miles to the fresh waters of the Burnett
River.
THE GIN GIN ESTATE which has long been coveted by Agriculturists has
now been SUBDIVIDED Into 103 FARMS
of various areas, well watered and drained and containing SOME cf the BEST
AGRICULTURAL LAND in this already famous District.
THE SOIL CONSISTS OF LIGHT AND EASILY WORKED BLACK AND
CHOCOLATE VOLCANIC LOAMS and by analysis has been PROVED EQUAL
TO, AND IN MANY CASES SUPERIOR TO THAT OF THE CELEBRATED
DARLING DOWNS with the additional attraction of A BETTER RAINFALL
due to its proximity to the sea coast.
CLEARING WILL COST PRACTICALLY NOTHING as the estate has nearly
all been ringbarked for 14 years and you can start ploughing right awav.
CROPS OF ALL CEREALS, SUGAR, POTATOES-and ONIONS grow in
profusion and for general farming and dairying the land is unsurpassed.
MAROONDAN RAILWAY STATION IS RIGHT ON THE LAND and
allotments in the TOWNSHIP OF MAROONDAN will be sold immediately after
the farms.
The Gin Gin Central Sugar Mill (which cost £55,000) is close to the Farms, also
a Creamery and a Butter Factory.

Instalments with interest at 4 par cant.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Exasperated.
Bang into the Lawyer’s office thro’ the private
door he plodded
With a rusticated reoklessness and blucher-booted
tread.
Uninvited seized an office chair, sprawled into it
and nodded
As he wiped his corrugated brow of honest sweat
and said—
Mornin’, Lawyer! I’ve just steered around to
interview yer for
To shake up some bloomin’ bounders for a-smashin’
up the law.
Say I want ter buy a horse that doesn’t kiok
and ain’t a shier ;
Say I doesn’t want a goer that’ll eat a lot of
feed,
But the quietest I oan pick up, and say I
becomes a buyer
Of a chestnut from a dealer and I pays him what’s
agreed;
Say I take his word for granted cause I’m layin’
sick in bed,
Can 11 summons him for sellin’ me a darn moke
that was dead ?
Say I pegs out a selection next to someone else
that poses
As a friend, and seems the kindest human shape
I ever knew ;
Say he gives me half a ton of seeds he sez are
creepin’ roses,
And I plant ’em all around me hut to make it
picture-skew.
Can’t I lump that crocodile to court if—spare me
old red hairs !
I can’t find me darn selection now for weeds and
prickly pears ?
‘ Say again I buys a sheep-dog from a cove and
tracks home with her,
And she just sniffs ’round my larder and ske-
daddles back agen :
Say I go and buy another and she does the same
darned slither,
Then another, and another, till I’ve shovelled out
for ten;
Can’t I get that blazin’ wrigglin’ adder strangled
up for crime
If I’ve bought the same dog dyed a different
color every time ? ’’
vio.
ALFERD E. BRIDGES.
M'Callum.
I’ve followed up a shearer’s work
For five and thirty seasons now ;
Fra’ Camooweal way down to Bourke
They ken M'Callum’s auld grey pow.
Aweel, each tyke must have his day—
The proverb’s worn, but verra true—
An’ men an’ sheds have changed their way,
But I’m too auld to learn anew.
They’ve passed awa’ beyond my ken,
The men I knew in bygone years,
I’m left—the cobbler o’ the pen—
To wait the end that slowly nears.
Poor lads ! their souls are nane the worse
For that they were a reckless crew,
If over prone to drink and curse,
I found their hearts ring leal and true.
’Tis fearsome, now, the sheds to see
A’ filled wi’ whirlin’ belts an’ wheels,
The dinnin’ o’ machinery
Is chorused by the whistle’s screels.
In place o’ honest, ringin’ shears,
New-fangled combs an’ cutters click
Wi’ clatterin’, complicated gears.
Good Lor-rd 1 it mak’s a body sick !
I’ve seen the auld give way to new,
For time and changes will not stay;
The sheds are growin’ verra few
Where sheep are shorn the good auld way.
An’ when the Lor-rd—the time draws near—
Shall please to give auld Mac the route,
Maybe the lads will grieve to hear
M'Callum’s had his last cut out.
JASON.
Wattle Bloom.
The magpie’s constant, joyous chime
Falls from the blue-gums, far and near;
The wattle is in golden prime;
It is the glad time of the year
When music of the spring we hear;
There is no thought beneath the sky
Of fading leaf and winds austere,
When wattle, like us all, must die.
The soft, sweet Spring of Austral clime,
Its murmurs fall upon the ear
In liquid cadences sublime ;
It is the glad time of the year—
The voices of the bush rise clear,
And bid the winter glad “good-bye !”
The winter —dull-clad, cold-swept, drear—
When wattle, like us all, must die.
Spring turns life’s prose to rippling rhyme ;
In its enchanted atmosphere
A lack of lovers is a crime ;
It is the glad time of the year.
Alas ! that, though love knows no fear,
Both Spring and lovers, too, must lie
On their inevitable bier,
When wattle, like us all, must die.
N.S.W.
S. ELLIOTT NAPIER.

mistake, and the Bible was hastily reinstated in the
schools.


The Miseries Caused
by a Torpid Liver.
The Only Safe Remedy Effects
a Perfect Cure.
The Cate of Mrs. M. HICKEY,
(BV a special reporter.)
There is much profound and important
wisdom in that old proverb where it is said
—“ The heart knoweth its own bitterness.”
It forms part of a truth still more compre-
hensive—that every man knows his own
peculiar feelings, and difficulties, and trials
far better than he can get any of his
neighbours to perceive them, although in an
effort to convey such impressions to other
minds lately made by Mrs. Marian Hickey,
of No. 3 West-street, Paddington, the
subject dealt with was so clearly described
that no trouble should be experienced in
understanding her story.
“For several weeks,” said Mrs. Hickey,
“ I had been feeling Tather unwell, but
there was no particular cause for alarm
until one day I took a bad turn whilst doing
my work. Such a horrible feeling of faint-
ness came over me that I said I would see a
doctor, and so I did.”
“Did the doctor tell you what was
wrong ? ”
“ He said it was my liver and kidneys
which were causing the trouble, and that
the medicine which he prescribed was the
best thing 1 could have for my complaint,
but my sufferings, instead of getting less,
became fifty times worse than ever ; so after
taking that medicine a while I thought I
had better hasten to another doctor and see
if he could bring me any relief. For a day
or two I was slightly easier, then the medi-
cine had no effect upon me, and I drifted
to the lowest depths of wretchedness.
Even then I did not lose heart altogether,
for I consulted another physician, and then
another, and so on till I could see it was no
use going to any more. My courage failed
me after all those bitter experiences, and I
thought my only chance w ould be simply a
matter of luck. If I could but get a good
physic amongst all those that were
advertised I might yet be relieved, I
thought; but after trying almost everything
procurable I w r as in a hopeless predica-
ment,. ”
“ Are you sure you did not receive some
benefit from all those medicines ?”
“ None whatever. They made me worse,
for I became as helpless as a child. Yes,
twelve years ago I lost the use of my limbs.
Why, I could not put my hands to-my head
to do ray hair, and for five months I could
not w'alk except by the aid of sticks and
with some one to help me along. Oh ! it
was terrible ; and to think that I was not
going to see my children grow up was a
source of constant anguish. I had given up
all hope of recovering, and felt as sure as I am
that I am sitting here that I would never
get better, and if you had seen me you
would have thought the same. You never
saw such a sight as I was—not a morsel of
flesh on my bones (I was as thin as a wafer),
and my cheeks quite sunken. With my
darkened complexion as well, I am sure I
looked the picture of death. Iw as awfully
nervous, too, for I used to jump up in the
middle of the night and fancy that some-
body was killing me, and if I had been able
to walk I would not have crossed the street,
as if anybody came near me my limbs shook
like the branches of a tree There was no
peace in life for me, and I was so melancholy
with the that I was dying that I
resigned myself to my fate. Heavy pains
in the chest gave me such a horrible choking
sensation that I was afraid to lie down, and
I had to~be propped up in my chair because
of the excruciating pains in my side and
back, between my shoulders the pain was
so severe that I used to have a lotion rubbed
in ; but that did not ease them, and the
piercing pains at the top and back of my
head nearly drove ms frantic. My eyes felt
M if they were not properly opened, the
sight also being very bad, and if I washed
my mouth out a dozen times a day, it made
no difference to the bitter taste that was
there. I had no appetite for food, and
what little I was obliged to take gave me
violent pains in the stomach, which seemed
to be tied in a knot. When those terrible
cramps came on I used to think, ‘ Oh, dear, I
am going to expire S’but even death would not
come to my relief. Soon afterwards, how-
ever, the food came up again, and it was
that constant vomiting which made me as
weak as a kitten. Giddiness and palpita-
tion came with the least movement, and I
felt so feeble that I knew the last "bregs of
life would soon be exhausted.”
“ Unless you received relief you mean ? ”
“I did not look for help, because I
thought I was past all worldly aid ; but, to
please a friend who came to see me, I
consented to try a bottle of Clements Tonic.
When that was emptied she insisted on me
taking more, and in a fortnight I really felt
the better for it. Something seemed to
whisper that I should persevere with
Clements Tonic, and, helpless and crippled
as I was, a new hope sprang up, because
Clements Tonic was stopping the fits of
vomiting, and I was sleeping Well already.
Restlessness at nights bad been one of my
greatest trials, but under Clements Tonic
I could soon sleep peacefully, without
dreams occurring to upset me. Then the
dreadful chest and stomach pains started to
decrease, and my headaches were less
frequent. For a time I could hardly realise
that I was being rescued, but when a good
appetite appeared and my food stayed down
regularly, 1 almost jumped with joy at the
knowledge that I was saved. For twelve
months I had suffered unspeakable torture
and misery, and those who had seen me
tottering on the brink of the grave were
amazed at the wonderful power of Clements
Tonic, which cleared away the pains in my
side and shoulders, and drove the aching
sensations from my limbs, the use of which
I acquired by degrees. To be able to mix
with the outside world after despairing of
ever seeing it again is what I have to thank
Clements Tonic for, and I am sure I would
have been dead long ago only for that medi-
cine. The strengthening effects of Clements
Tonic were shown by the way I could go
about my work, as in a few weeks I was as
brisk and active as ever, and with as much
happiness in life as I enjoyed before being
afflicted with that terrible sense of nervous
depression which nothing but Clements
Tonic could eradicate. The symptoms of
kidney disease were banished simultaneously
with my liver disorders, and they were all
removed so effectively that for nearly eleven
years I have been blessed with the very best
of health. That is why I advise all my
friends to take Clements Tonic when they
are ill, and nothing pleases me more than
this interview, every word of which you
can publish in any form you like.”

Something for Mothers to Think About
EVERY CHILD born into the world with an inherited
or early developed tendency to distressing, disfiguring
humours of the skin, scalp, and blood, becomes an object
of the most tender solicitude, not only because of its suf-
fering but because of the dreadful fear that the disfigura-
tion is to be lifelong and mar its future happiness and
prosperity. Hence it becomes the duty of mothers of
such afflicted children to acquaint themselves with the
best, the purest, and most effective treatment available,
viz., THE CUTICURA TREATMENT.
Warm baths with Cuticura Soap, to cleanse the skin of crusts and scales
and soften the thickened cuticle, gentle anointings with Cuticura Oint-
ment, to instantly allay itching, irritation, and inflammation, and soothe and
heal, to be followed in the severer cases by mild doses of Cuticura Resol-
vent Pills (see below), to cool and cleanse the blood, are all that can be
desired for the alleviation of the suffering of skin-tortured infants and
children and the comfort of worn-out, worried mothers. A single set is
often sufficient to cure when the best physicians fail.
Millions of Women Use Cuticura Soap
Assisted by Coticura Ointment, for preserving, purifying, and beautifying the skin, for
cleansing the scab) of crusts, scales, and dandruff, and the stopping of falling hair, for
softening, whitening, and soothing red, rough, and sore hands, for baby rashes, itchings,
and chaflngs, in the form of baths for annoying irritations and inflammations, or too free
or offensive perspiration, in the form of washes for ulcerative weaknesses, and for many
sanative, antiseptic purposes which readily suggest themselves to women, especially
mothers, and for all the purposes of the toilet, bath, and nursery.
Cuticura Resolvent Pills (Chocolate Coated) are prepared to meet the wants of

SATURDAY, JUNE 27, 1903.
The “Great Natural Resources" of this
Country.
Most of these States are just now, or very shortly
will be, floating the usual loan to develop the
magnificent natural resources of this country,
and the present is as good a time as any to
consider the nature and reasons and alleged
advantages of the much-advertised borrowing pro-
cess. For the development of our vast natural
resources with loans has as much right to be put
on its defence, and called upon to show reasonable
cause for its existence, as any other policy. The
mere fact that it is a round, sonorous term, a
mouth-filling mass of syllables, a loud and
handy remark for the use of mentally-barren
politicians, a platitude that has become sacred
to the Herald and Telegraph, and to See
and Carruthers, and poor old Pa Brunker,
a large, fat bye-word or shibboleth that is sup-
posed to be in itself the explanation and defence
of every political foolishness—all these things are
nothing. The only possible excuse for the system
of borrowing immense sums for the development
of our unbounded natural resources is that by
that process the people of Australia become richer
and happier. If building railways with loan
money has only left the people poorer and more
miserable, then Australia might almost as well
have built pyramids; if borrowing money to make
roads and harbors has only resulted in making
the people more wretched, and the struggle
for life harder, then Australia might almost
as well have made a statue of Baal or
some other useless ornament. It is better
to have ox-waggons and be comfortable and
happy, than to have mortgaged railways and
be steeped in poverty. Unless the policy of
developing the huge natural resources of the
country with loan funds can show that it has at
least led to no diminution in the average citizen’s
share of happiness, then it hasn’t even a negative
virtue to put to its credit, and it would have been
better that no loans had been floated, and that
the development process had gone on as best it
could with our own means.
* * * *
Public finance is, in a general way, as good a
test as any of the public prosperity. It isn t an
infallible test, but it comes reasonably close to
being so. Taking New South Wales as an example
—one State is as good an example as another, and
New South Wales is the oldest State, the largest
borrower, and the one whicli publishes the most
complete statistics—it is worth while to consider
the evidence which its financial condition affords.
If the review commences about the year 1860 the
loan policy will be allowed 42 years in which to
vindicate itself, and a policy which can’t do some-
thing to vindicate itself in 42 years is too slow to
be worth serious consideration.
* ' * »-* *
In 1860 New South Wales had a debt of barely
£4 000,000— equal to less than £ll per inhabitant,
or’not much more -than'one-sixth of the present
figure. There was no accumulated deficit in those
days, and revenue covered expenditure quit®
easily. There was a very mild revenue tariff in.
1860; there was no land-tax and no income-tax,
nor was there any excise; the stamp duties
were much lower than they are now, and probate
duties had not begun to be thought of. And m
1860 the land revenue, in proportion to popula-
tion, was far less than it is now, and the headlong
disposal of the public estate had hardly com-
menced. Yet in that far-off period New South
Wales, as already mentioned, paid its way. It
had barely begun its great loan policy, and blatant
politicians did not then run to and fro with the
fury of later days, talking about the development
of our utterly flabbergasting natural resources.
That blighting phrase was hardly born 42 years
ago, and though New South Wales was steadily
developing, it was doing so, to a great extent, with
its own money. It had scarcely cut the first leaf
in the book of the great Gospel of Debt, or begun
to absorb the disastrous theory that the way to
grow rich is to owe more than any other country
on earth, and that the most beautiful thing a
country can import is a heap of embarrassments,
i * • * *
But the belief in the policy of making the people
rich by getting into debt was growing, and by
1870 the liabilities of New South Wales were close
on .£10,000,000. The process didn’t pan out well,
though there were few developments so far.
Instead of growing rich the people seemed to grow
a shade poorer, and the old sources of revenue
dried up to some extent. In 1871 it was found
necessary to greatly increase the customs tariff
and to sell land more vigorously in order to keep
the State afloat under the blessed and enriching
policy of developing its immense natural resources
out of loan funds. In theory the development of
these resources should have yielded enough to pay
interest on the loans and something over by way
of profit, for unless there was a profit the loan
system lacked vindication ; and that profit should
have enabled the State to reduce taxation instead
of increasing it. But despite the fact that there
was a woeful discrepancy somewhere the doctrine
of developing our splendid natural resources with
loans was preached with renewed vigor.
* * * *
So New South Wales proceeded to develop
its unlimited natural resources with more loans,
and the debt which had been less than £10,000,000
in 1870 touched £15,000,000 in 1880. A lot
of things began to happen about this
time. The land sales were multiplied threefold
in 1880 as compared with 1870, and in 1881 they
were multiplied fivefold as compared with 1870,
in order to raise money to keep New South Wales
out of the workhouse, where the process of
developing its great natural resources out of loans
threatened to send it. The customs duties were
increased in 1879 with the same end in view, and
stamp duties were imposed in 1880. Yet with all
this tremendous sacrifice of the public estate, and
these increased taxes, deficits began to appear
with considerable regularity. But the State still
kept its faith in the doctrine that it would neces-
sarily grow rich by raising money at the British
pawnshop to develop its incredible natural
resources, and the debt, which had been
£15,000,000 in 1880, became £30,000,000 in
1884. By this time it might have been reason-
ably hoped that the wealth which was to arise
through the development of the country’s brilliant
natural resources, on the mortgage principle,
would have begun to visibly assert itself, for the
public debt represented <£33 per inhabitant. And
the most reasonable and convincing way by which
the good results of the development process could
assert themselves was by making new taxes super-
fluous, by making it possible to remove some of
the old taxes, and by enabling the finances to keep
on an even keel.
... * *
And, instead of this, the year 1884 found the
developed New South Wales with the big debt
so much more embarrassed than an undeveloped
New South Wales with a small debt was in 1860
that it was found necessary to increase the customs
duties again, to impose new excise duties, to raise
.£1,750,000 out of the public estate, mostly by land
sales (as against £312,000 thus raised in I 860,) and
with all this there was a monstrous deficit of
£1,300,000. And even then the public faith in
the policy of developing the unparalleled natural
resources of the country out of foreign loans didn’t
diminish by one iota. In fact, it rather increased.
* * * *
By 1891 the loan doctrine had had another seven
years’ trial, and more things had happened to the
sudden and distressful Cornstalk State. The debt
had risen to £53,000,000, and if there was any
possible ground for the belief that the money thus
expended in the development of the dazzling
natural resources already alluded to not
only, directly or indirectly, paid the inter-
est on the debt, but also left a direct or
indirect margin for the general enrichment of the
country, it might be fairly assumed that that en-
richment would begin to become undeniably
apparent. Instead of that, however, things were
so much worse that a few more duties had been
put on in the struggle to make ends meet; a
deficit 0f£2,770,000 had been accumulated ; the
habit of charging .£500,000 or so of ordinary
expenditure to loans in order to keep up a pre-
tence of solvency had fairly set in ; and even with
the aid of this swindle, revenue had practically
given up all pretence of meeting expenditure.
By 1896 the process of enriching the country by
developing its resources on the loan principle had
progressed so much further that the income-tax be-
came a necessity, and the first beginnings of that
impost were collected. In 1897 the land-tax was
first collected. In 1899 the probate duties were
enormously increased, and in 1902 the new cus-
toms tariff fairly set in. By this time the debt,
which was £53,000,000 in 1891, was somewhere
about £78,000,000. There had been £25,000,000
more development, out of loans, of the practically
boundless natural resources of the country. The
“ judicious expenditure ” of that sum was not only
supposed to justify itself by earning its own
interest but also to leave something over to make
New South Wales a better, happier, and more
prosperous land than it had been before. And
somehow it was merely the old experience over
again. More taxes had been put on in the
struggle to keep the country’s head, weighed
down by the ghastly process of borrowing to
develop its immeasurable natural resources, above
water. The accumulated deficit was larger than
before. The swindle of charging ordinary expen-
diture to loans in order to make things look semi-
solvent, had grown to greater dimensions. And
the new loan to develop the utterly incalculable
natural resources of New South Wales is to-day
being floated as gaily as ever. Apparently the
delusion is incurabie.
* • * *
The record of the loan madness in its worst
form covers a period, so far as New South Wales
is concerned, of about 42 years. It isn’t long in
the lifetime of a State, but many things have hap-
pened in that period. New South Wales is really,
in proportion to its size, a desperately poor coun-
try, though richer than some of its neighbors. It
began by incurring £1 worth of debt to develop
resources worth 155., and as it used up or I ° w f < J
to drift into the hands of id>. e monopolists ts
patch of good country, and carried the opening up
process further and further out, it h 8 develop
reached a point where it borrows £1 to develop
something worth perhaps half-a orown,
something worth half-a-crown can t st f a •
under the debt. Every few years of borrowing
and developing have made the poople a little
poorer, made tl.e old sources of revenue a little
less profitable, and made new taxes • >
swindles necessary. In 42 years °f g
loan policy, N.S. Wales has sold all its best lands
and spent the proceeds; it has accumulated a
huge deficit and a huge army of unem-
ployed ; it has put on successively P
duties, excise duties, probate duties, increased
customs duties, land-tax, income-tax- an
years of developing the wonderful natural
resources of New South Wales with orl '
money have swallowed them all. The struggle to
make the public finances meet is ten times harder
now than it was in 1860. The undevelope
country that used to attract immigrants has
been, in its developed state, for the last
dozen years, a good country to get out oi.
And there is no visible reason to suppose that
another 42 years of borrowing, of developing two-
and-sixpence worth of desert with a pounds
worth of loan, of settling people on the
western dust, and giving them a mort-
gaged railway to take them there, and gratis seed
wheat to keep them there, and of empty
babbling about the necessity of raising more
money to develop our astonishing natural re-
sources, will produce any result save more taxes,
more deficits, and more disappointment. New
South Wales isn’t a heavily-taxed country even
yet, but it only needs to keep on “developing
and it will be. And then it will not have the
satisfaction of knowing that the extra taxes are
used to build remunerative public works, the
revenue from which will make it possible to
reduce taxes later on; nor that they are working
off an old dead horse; nor that they are prevent-
ing the dead horse becoming any deader. There
will still, if the loan policy continues, be nothing
ahead but more taxes. The loan delusion has
had a long enough trial to enable it to show any
possibilities of good that are in it, and it is due
for a long, long rest.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Flyspeck’s Labor Movement.
It is an astonishing thing that Tasmania has a
Labor party at all; but the toilers of the Wet
Spot, having wakened up sufficiently to return
four Labor members (very nearly five) at last
State elections, now seem desirous of staying
awake permanently, in order to astonish the
Commonwealth some more. The Labor League
Convention, held recently in Hobart, decided to
run three Labor candidates for the three Senate
seats shortly to become vacant. In reply to those
who opine that the Labor men are biting off
more than they can conveniently chew in
laying claim to all the seats, the Labor
people urge that, as they have to vote for
three candidates, they might as well vote
for their own three as assist candidates of the
Dobson breed with their “thrown-away ” votes
In thus figuring out the situation the Labor men
are perfectly correct; when a man must give three
votes it is not good enough to give the third one
to Dobson and Co. But with this enlarging of
the Labor horizon a very large question that has
long been urgent becomes more urgent than ever.
In three States—Queensland, Westralia, and Tas'
mania—the Labor party has already decided to
contest all three Senatorial seats. It is almost
certain that the labor unions are not strong enough
in Tasmania to win even one seat, and they are
probably not strong enough in any State to win
three. They need to attract votes from outside,
and to do that they require a wider platform than
they possess. It isn’t that they have too much
Labor legislation on their programme, but that
they haven’t enough of other kinds. A Labor
party that went bald - headed for high Pro-
tection, for a heavy graduated Federal land-
tax on large estates, for a Federal absentee-
tax, for the absolute refusal to borrow except for
the purpose of renewing State loans as they fall
due, for the taking over and consolidation of the
State debts, for Federal railway control and a
Federal public works policy supported solely out
of revenue, would attract a lot of friends to whom
a Labor programme by itself appeals but very
slightly. It should attract among others the
reasonable section of the manufacturers (not the
low-wage section), the oppressed tenants of the big
land monopolists, the people who want land, and
the increasing crowd which is scared at the'huge
accumulation of public debt. So far the Federal
Labor party has done good service as the oppon-
ent of Commonwealth borrowing, but it has
gained little credit for it because it has not de-
clared that financial independence is a permanent
feature of its policy ; it has only opposed, up to
now, in an accidental kind of way. The unions,
by themselves, are not strong enough to get con-
trol of Parliament —which is, presumably, their
object ; a political party that doesn’t aim at
getting control of Parliament had better go out of
politics. The question to be considered is: What
principles can the party honestly add to its plat-
form in order to bring in a wider circle of voters
—what principles which don’t conflict with its
existing policy, which are not already annexed,
and which are for the good of the country?
The permanent anti-borrowing policy, the policy
of constructing Federal public works out of
revenue, and the principle of Federal direct
taxation and Federal bursting up of big estates
are all waiting for an advocate. They are all
clean, democratic, and progressive ideas, and
they would all bring votes. The Labor party,
in addition to going solidly for Protection,
should annex those principles while they are
lying around loose. If it doesn’t, some time-
server of the Reid variety will take them up for
mere advertising purposes, and use them to attract
votes that the Labor party should get.
Keeping* our own Money in the Country
or Borrowing 1 Other People’s Money.
Few theories have evoked more tremendous scorn
from the haughty and superior Freetrade person
than the Protectionist statement that his aim is
to “Keep the money in the country.” “You
can’t eat money,” says the blatant importer. “It
is mere barbarism to hoard it up. The true use
of money is to send it abroad for the purchase of
useful and necessary articles.” And the same
person says that this country wants, above all
things, an influx of capital for investment so as to
“develop our magnificent natural resources,”
and his paper complains bitterly that the conduct
of the Labor party is “ preventing the investment
of outside capital in the country.” Therefore the
Freetrade man holds that it is no earthly use
keeping our own money in the country in
order to “develop our great natural resources ”
for our own benefit, and at the same time if we
don’t get other people’s money to come here at
heavy interest to “develop our great natural
resources” for the benefit of the foreign investor
we are lost. The Freetrader says that having our
own money in the country is an unenlightened
and miserly hoarding up of useless sovereigns, but
that having somebody else’s money here and con-
sequently being in debt, is an enlightened policy
of development. The Protectionist view is that
if we manufactured more we wouldn’t need to
import so much ; if we imported less we wouldn t
need to send away so much money to pay for im-
ports ; consequently there would be money
of our own in the country available for
“development.” When N.S.W. wanted to
start the Sydney Harbor Colliery it went
to England for the money, because so much
money goes out to pay for imports that the
necessary funds weren’t available here—conse-
quently the colliery is owned in England. Ihe
other day N.S.W. rejoiced when it heard that its
biggest gold mine had been sold to an English
company—there being no money available locally
because it had gone to pay. for the states
£17,000,000 a year of foreign imports. rv.S. n.
felt that a load had been lifted off its mind a very
little while ago when the See Government man-
aged to borrow £1,000,000 in London at 4iper
cent., though if we increased our local manufac-
tures to the extent of £1,000,000 a year, and sen
away £1,000,000 less to pay for foreign shoddy, tne
money would have been available locally, and the
£42,500 interest would have been spent locally)
and there would be £1,000,000 worth more worx
and wages for our own people. The Freetra
says that the policy of “Keeping the Money i
the Country ” is a barbarism. Isn t the money
wanted in the country ? Likewise isn t
country always borrowing publicly and pnva my
just because money is wanted in the coun ry •
Isn’t the Freetrade press always weeping becau
foreign capital (in other words, foreign debt] l
coming into the country fast enough, and all
because we want money in the country,
the Labor party always being told by the r
trade papers that its policy is driving out tore g
capital (i.e., foreign debt), and that the ®y
we so badly want kept in the country is g
away ? If the Freetrade party made one-
as much row when <£10,000,000 of our own 111
goes out of the country to pay for foreign. g_
that we could manufacture ourselves, as 1
when £lOO,OOO of somebody else’s coin that t
invested here, and cost us 5 per cent, m
annually, is withdrawn, Australia would
better and brighter than it is.

The Land Without a Fast.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
««Go where you may, except in Australia,” says Jos.
Symes, “ you find the face of the earth covered with
ruined buildings, or you find the remains of ancient cities
covered up with the accumulated dust of centuries."
No civilisation has been buried here. Has one been born.?
— Bulletin Red Page, May 30, 1903.
Not a story of our glory—not a single Pyramid
Tells a tale of heroes parted and the wondrous
deeds they did;
Luckless land without banditti, robber barons,
pirate city—
What the mischief were you doing ’—where the
dickens were you hid ?
Not a temple, not an altar, not a monument of
old,
Not a fiction writ on vellum, not a pillar on the
wold,
Not a token there behind us, not a single thing to
bind us
To an ancient golden period in this barren land
of Gold.
We’ve the sand —but where’s the Mecca? Who
has stole our Bethel stone ?
And our old Australian monarchs—did they never
build a throne?
Were their temples only bunya—was their palace
but a gunyah ?
Did they leave us naught to rev’rence but a
dubious bit of bone?
Happy Moujik ! He can revel in the mem’ry of
a Czar
Who could harness olden Moujiks just like cattle
to a car;
Lucky Celt and lucky Cossacks—lucky Tyrol,
lucky Trosachs !
Only lands without a story know what happy
lands you are.
And we ponder on the problem till we query all
aghast—
Is it possible our country never had a blessed past ?
While the Hindu, young and hoary, boasts a
splendid age of story —
By the shade of previous Chaos all our day is
overcast.
Oh !to toast a single despot-who—a thousand
years ago—
Razed the city, howled a ditty, filled the land
with tears and woe,
Spilt the blood of brother Abel, built an idiot
tower of Babel,
Left a line of queer descendants with an intellect
of dough.
Think how Egypt, glorious Egypt, in her pleni-
tude of fat,
Mid a thousand tow’ring temples, as a queen
exalted, sat,
How at hundred gated Thebes, Coptic youths and
Coptic hebes
Thronged to pay their lowly homage to the
Crocodile and Cat!
So our outlook in the Future—it must needs be
grim and bad,
For a folk with no ancestors is a solemn folk and
sad ;
And we feel the days before us wave a sable pinion
o’er us,
While the Present standeth weeping for the Past
it never had !

Bulletin, for one, however much it may recognise

to remember that the [jower to levy unlimited
taxation on his land, his income, his jobs, on
the accumulated pile he will leave at his death,
rests with a Legislature in which the toiler he
insults, and the civil servant he disfranchises, and
the railway worker he bullies, and the old age
pensioner he wrongs, have as much power as he
has more, in fact, because they are more numer-
ous. So far no one has attempted to organise a
direct taxation party in the Australian Parlia-
ment ; no one, for instance, has preached to the
tenants of the Clarkes and Manifolds the
advisability of voting to tax their landlords
through the Commonwealth Legislature until they
are compelled to sell their big estates and give
their tenants a chance to become freeholders on
moderate terms. But it only requires that the
Tory party should become sufficiently exasperat-
ing, and that will come. The Tory party is insult-
ing its masters. They don’t realise' yet that,
through the medium of the Australian Parliament,
they are its masters, but the more they are insulted
the sooner they will realise it.

About Some Letters.
The very latest alarm, as published by a Sydney
morning paper
HOW CAPITAL IS SOARED.
Commonwealth Infringing State Rights.
Brisbane, Saturday.—Letters which have been received
from England from persons who are in a position to
closely judge on such matters give doleful accounts of
the effects of the tendency of the Commonwealth legisla-
tion, so far as the investment of capital in Australia and
emigration from England to these States are concerned.
It is said that until the Federal Restriction Act is abol-
ished or amended there is no chance of either capital or
individuals coming to the States. Both public and
private credit are being seriously affected, and though
the policy of the Queensland Government gives encourage-
ment to allow capital to flow this way, money holders are
frightened off by the Socialistic legislation of the Com.
monwealth.
The letters advise the States to fight for their rights,
and the opinion is expressed that if they are united they
will be successful.
Again we have here the doleful alleged fact that
we are losing a chance to get further into debt;
and the gloom therein embodied rests on the
authority of alleged letters, written to some one
not specified, by some one not specified, and
coming from some part of England which is
evidently a secret, on a date which apparently
has to be kept dark for reasons of State. Prob-
ably there are no such letters; but if they do
exist, the chances are that some one in Brisbane
has written to some one in England stating that
the Federal Parliament has started a gory revolu-
tion, and some one has written back saying that
in that case he won’t send out the post-office
order he intended forwarding until the upheaval
is over. As for the “ Commonwealth infringing
State Rights,” that is such an obvious falsehood
that though the editor publishes the big heading
he doesn’t dare to allege one “right” that has been
infringed. Meanwhile The Bulletin is glad to be
able to inform the public that it is in receipt of
letters from influential persons in Greece and
Honduras, in which the writers give it as their
fixed opinion that Australia will never prosper
until the State Governments are wholly abolished,
and a Federal Labor party runs the whole show.
These letters are probably just as genuine as the
other lot.
A Petition.
The other day poor little Bill Wilks, M.H.R.,
presented to the House of Reps, a petition from a
number of persons protesting against Premier
Barton’s visit to the Pope, his acceptance from
the Pope of a medal, and his alleged assurance to
the Pope that while he (Barton) was in power
the Catholics of the Commonwealth would receive
a larger share of liberty and benefits than they
received in other parts of the Empire. The peti-
tion also prayed the House to oppose the
conferment on Barton of any position of honor
or dignity in the Commonwealth until his
action had been endorsed by the electorates.
Now the electorates can’t “endorse ’ Premier
Barton’s action in this matter at next election
unless all the urgent questions of the day —an im-
proved tariff, old age pensions, land-tax, debt
consolidation, absentee-tax, sinking fund, trans-
continental railway and everything else are
dropped and the contest is fought out on the
question of Yellow Pupism pure and simple. Tt
would be necessary, in order to get an endorsement
or condemnation of Barton’s visit to the Pope, to
elect a new Australian Parliament solely on the
question whether Barton ought to have called on
a pleasant old gentleman of 93, or not. Unless
the election was fought on that question it couldn t
decide whether the people approved Barton s
action or not. And after that the Parliament
would endure for three years, and the man who
got in solely because he was a screaming yellow
dervish would decide about what to do with
our £200,000,000 of debt, and the man whose
sole qualification was that he was a howling
green dervish would commit the country to a rail-
way or taxation policy, and so on. Premier
Barton himself has explained over and over again
that the statement on which a great part of the
petition was based is a lie, an d_ that he didn t pro-
mise Catholics “ a larger share of liberty and
benefits,” &c. And there is this much m his favor,
that he was present at the interview, while Wilks
and his friends were not, so that they cqji only
depend on their imaginations for their statement
as to what transpired. Considering all the gross
and wilful lies that the Wilks party has told
about the “exclusion” and “imprisonment of
the Six Blathers, its word is certainly not good
enough when it assures the public on a matter
that it can’t possibly know anything about. As
regards the mere visit to the Pope, if Premier
Barton should be thrown out for that then
King Edward YII. should be dethroned for
doing exactly the same thing, and the Wilks
gang hmd better include him in its condemnation.
ut Australia doesn’t really care a straw whether
•dakton visited the Pope or not. It knows that
arto n is a Protestant — though not so petty and
uncivil a Protestant as Wilks—and it wouldn’t
u knew him be aR.C. so long as it knows
tnat he isn t going to start an Inquisition, or endow
monkeries with public funds. And it certainly
f Sn .| P re P are d to entrust its whole public business
tor three years to the stupid, ignorant, narrow-
minded bigots of the Abigail type who fight elec-
tions on either green or yellow tickets. Wilks
has the ineffable shame and disgrace of being the
first man—or the first mean political rat—who has
tried to bring the dirty sectarian issue into
federal legislation. At election time the only
sectarian issue is the necessity for throwing out
every candidate who seeks to intrude either his
green or yellow sentiments, or who asks for either
a green or yellow vote, or who travels on the holy
ticket in any way whatsoever.

Settling on the Land.
“ J.E.W.” writes from Tasmania :
Oo.’s land in Tasmania. I have been all
round the N.W. Coast, lately, looking for land upon which
to settle. I was advised to look in at theV.D.L. Co.’s
office at Burnie, and have a chat with the new manager
lately out from England, “as he was anxious to settle
people on the land, and was granting easy terms.” I
saw the manager. He advised me to look at Ridgely,
Hampshire Hills, and Surrey Plains. The terms were :
Seven years’ lease at easy rental, with option of renewal
at slightly increased rate. I was to fence and clear the
land, and make it fit for cultivation. (Only those who have
seen Tasmanian bush land know what clearing really
means. The undergrowth is cut out, but the great
trees are all on the land—either standing or lying down.)
I was also to build a house and all other necessary out-
buildings. If I had not the means, it didn’t matter;
they would build the places, and I could pay the co. for
them with per cent, added. No price mentioned
until I looked at the land. I went to Ridgely,
10 miles south from Burnie, on railway-line to
Zeehan. The estate consisted of good and of poor land,
mostly broken country. The land was cut up into blocks
of from 25 to 100 acres. This in the plan only. There
were no roads marked anywhere. The Co. was going to
make roads through, but if I took one of those blocks as
marked in the plan it seemed just likely that when my
neighbor wanted a road, it would have to be cut through
my paddock. You don’t know where a road will go
in broken, hilly country. When I returned to the
office I asked the price of some of these blocks, and was
told I could have one at £1 per acre per year on the
terms afore mentioned ! or I could have a piece of poor
grazing country—mostly broken and stones—for 7s. 6d.
per acre per annum. I asked then, in the event of my
leaving after my first term of 7 years was up, would they
allow me any compensation for improvements put on the
land by me. Certainly not ! It was to my own
benefit to improve the land. I hear that the Co. is
putting up rents 100 per cent, all round where leases are
falling due (and there are a goodly number). I’m not
doing business with the Co. just now. I think it would
be cheaper and more satisfactory to do business with the
devil.

Tozer.
Queensland’s Tozer has been writing angry
letters to Australian Premier Barton. As a State
Agent-General, Tozer has authority to issue cer-
tificates _of exemption under the Immigration
Restriction Act, and he says he was waited upon
by a family which “ wanted to take to Queensland
its domestic servants of long service,” and he was
“compelled to inform the applicants that the
exemption they sought could not be granted.”
Whereupon Tozer passes some scornful remarks
about a polic) T which shuts out “valets and
ladies’maids ” —which it doesn’t do. Valets and
ladies’ maids can come in by the gross if they
want to dp so. All the law says is just what the
United States law says: They shall not come
bound by any contract. If the “domestic ser-
vants of long service” have sufficient confidence
in their master and mistress to come with them
on their mere verbal promise that they will have
employment on arrival, the law puts no difficulty
in the way. They may sign an agreement after
landing, and it will hold good. What the law seeks
is to prevent people importing contract workers—
who may possibly be engaged at rates which
looked good enough in the country from which
they came, but which wouldn’t hold skin and bone
together here ; they may contract as much as they
like, but the contract must be made here,
so that the parties have a fair chance to
know the conditions and the local values of
money. Of course, if the people Tozer has been
dealing with are such a villainously bad lot that
their servants dare not accompany them without
a written agreement, nothing can be done, but
that doesn’t alter the fact that Tozer’s statement
as to the nature of the Australian Immigration
Law is utterly untrue. Certain powers of
exemption exist, but the Australian Government
has wisely decided not to leave the exercise of
these to Tozer. Australia doesn’t know Tozer,
and didn’t appoint him Agent-General, and cer-
tainly wouldn’t appoint him if it did know him.
Everything depends upon the administration of
the Immigration Restriction Act; and the Aus-
tralian Government isn’t, therefore, likely
to hand over that administration to a per-
son over whom it has no authority and
who, for all it knows, may be in the pay
of people who want to defeat the measure. The
error that the Australian Government commits is
in making it possible for Tozer to pass these
remarks. It ought not to have anything to do
with Tozer ; but, as it hasn’t a Commissioner of
its own on the other side, it practically has to give
some power to the State Tozers, and this leads to
all the trouble. The quickest and best way out is
to appoint that High Commissioner.

Reduction of Members.
N.B.W. Premier John See is probably bitterly
sorry that he ever pledged himself to a reduction
of Parliament. Members of his own party don’t
like the idea because, coming as they do mainly
from the country districts, they see in it the
prospect of the already vast electorates “out-
back’ being further extended, to the great incon-
venience of their representatives; the Labor
party objects, partly for the same reason,
partly because the idea is associated with Irvinism
and Bentism, and it would be almost excusable
to view the Archangel < Gabriel with dark
suspicion if any of his views were adopted by
Irvine or Bent. The only persons really enthusi-
astic about the reduction are the daily newspaper
proprietors, which have an unreasonable hatred
of the politician ever since Parliament interfered
with their subsidies. The Carruthers following
professes to be eagerly with the newspapers on the
subject, but that is only because it wishes to
whoop on the same side in the hope of getting
thereby to the sweets of office. Whilst, therefore;
a reduction of the present State Parliamentary
machine is a reasonable and logical consequence of
Federation and an appropriate beginning to the
fading away of State legislatures altogether; and
is, especially if accompanied by a local govern-
ment system, most urgent and desirable,
it has few friends outside of a little Tory
gang anxious to see Irvinism enthroned in
N.S.W. Still Premier See pledged himself to
a reduction and, not having the facility of a
George Reid or a Carruthers in somersaulting,
he considers himself bound in some way to that
promise; so he has taken a middle course and
will, at the time of the Federal Senate elections,
refer the reduction question to a vote of the
electors. The Bulletin hopes that that referen-
dum will put the question of Parliamentary
curtailment fully and fairly before the voters.
There are two suggestions for reduction —one that
the members of the Assembly be reduced to 90 or
80 or 70; the other that the second House of
Parliament—the practically useless, though some-
what ornamental, Upper House —be retrenched
out of existence. The people should be asked to
vote on both these proposals —and there is no
reason why they should not accept both. At
present there are 125 members of the Assembly and
60 members of the Council —185 State politicians.
One proposal would reduce the number by, say, 30
the other by 60—both together might_ make a
reduction of 90. The cost of the Council, allow-
ing for its frill, public offices, railway passes,
rent, &c., is £21,000 a year. The cost of 30 mem-
bers of the Assembly, allowing for salaries and
railway passes (the latter reckoned as worth £lOO
a year), is £12,000 a year. The economy of abolish-
ing the Council would thus be as great as that of
reducing the Assembly by 50 members, even on a
mere surface consideration of the case. Looking
deeper and taking into consideration the saving
of time in having only one set of gentlemen to
debate measures, and the consequent curtail-
ment of Hansard, the economy shows as vastly
greater. If reduction of members is intended
as a matter of economy only, then clearly
the most effective economy is by abolition
of the useless Second Chamber. But there are
stronger reasons for urging that step. Upper
Houses were imposed upon the Australian States
in weak imitation of the British House of Lords,
partly from the desire of the Tory British Legis-
lature of a century ago to perpetuate conditions
which were then deemed to be right, but which
have long since been shown to be absurd ; partly
from the ambition of the richer classes of the
early Australians to have available for themselves
a small titulary distinction. In N.S.W. the
Upper House was most closely modelled on the
British House of Lords; and, consequently, has
proved the least obstructive of all the Austra-
lian Second Chambers, being at any time liable
to “swamping” if it opposed the popular House.
But it has never fulfilled any genuinely useful
purpose, except that of providing a pleasant club
for well-to-do elderly retired politicians. In
abolishing it, N.S.W. would only follow the
example of the majority of the Canadian States.
Of the seven States of the Canadian federation,
only two —Quebec and Nova Scotia—have second
Houses. Five—New Brunswick, Ontario, Mani-
toba, British Columbia and Prince Edward Island
—have but a single house. The North-west Terri-
tories of Canada are also administered by a single
house of Parliament. There is thus no lack
of precedent—if precedent be needed. There is
a reasonable hope of the people of N.S.W. voting
for the lopping off of the useless and expensive
Legislative Council, if given the opportunity at
the referendum, and democrats in Parliament
should see that they get the opportunity. A vote
on the suggestion to reduce the Assembly only
would be obviously incomplete, like suggesting to
a farmer, as a means to economy, the reduction
of his team of working horses, without allowing
him to choose as an alternative the retrenchment
of his ornamental, but not strictly useful, buggy
horse.

Wanted : A Victorian Liberal
Association.
A letter from “ F.S.” :
If ever Victoria needed a thoroughly live Liberal Asso-
ciation it is now. Both the Federal and State elections
are within sight—the latter should, but won’t, be held
this November —and unless some active measures are
promptly adopted, the high carnival of reactionary Tory-
ism that at present holds the State in its grip will secure
a new lease of life. The Federal elections, which will
probably take place in December, claim first attention,
particularly with regard to the Senate. When the initial
elections were held, the conditions and requirements
were so novel that the various political parties failed to
properly realise the position ; and, unluckily, most of the
“failing” fell to the lot of the Liberal party. Conse-
quently the Victorian contribution to the Senate was—
Fraser, Zeal, Sargood, Styles, Best, and Barrett. We
have since seen the Senate at work, and the Liberal
elector is beginning to discover that not only is it a great
power ; but that, owing to its franchise being the widest
on the globe, it promises to be the great Liberal strong-
hold of Australia. The late Senator Sargood saw this
when he stood for a seat in it, and complained mildly of
its “ dangerous Liberalism.”
Intelligent Liberals who have followed the Senate’s
deliberations have come to the conclusion that, prac-
tically, the whole of Victoria’s present contribution to
the Senate must go. Owing to the clause in the Con-
stitution which makes the Senate semi-continuous, only
half of the present senators come before the electors this
year ; and it is a sad feature of the business that the most
objectionable of the whole lot, Zeal, is safe for another
three years. Victoria’s piebald half-dozen includes three
prize crusted Tories— Zeal, Fraser, and the alleged
Senator Reid ; with three very ordinary Liberals— Best,
Barrett, and Styles. Of the three Tories, only Reid,
the young man from Brisbane, will be called on to face
the music this year, and the first business of the Victorian
Liberal party will be to make sure that his short and
ignoble senatorial career comes to a full stop. No stone
should be left unturned to make the re-election of this
Ir viNE-nominated, Brisbane-exposed person impossible.
The position of Styles and Barrett is somewhat
anomalous and awkward. Styles has, through his ill-
health, been practically useless ; while Barrett, whose
election was solely due to his inclusion in the Age ticket,
has been almost a nonentity. If the Liberal party is
well advised, it will endeavor to secure the candidature
of, say, Higgins, Trenwith and MacKinnon, three power-
ful democrats, whose inclusion in the Senate would go
far towards removing the well-grounded reproach that
Victoria’s contribution to the Commonwealth’s Upper
House is the weakest of the lot. At present, Higgins
represents North Melbourne in the House of Reps., but
this is a seat that the Liberals can easily hold with
another and weaker man. Trenwith still stands for Rich-
mond in the State Assembly, where MacKinnon repre-
sents Prahran. Trenwith’s election to the Senate depends
simply on his allowing his name to appear as a candi-
date. He was chosen for the ’9l Convention without
addressing a single meeting outside of Richmond, and on
a total expenditure of £3 Bs. Higgins, also, would be
fairly easy to return, but the Liberals would need to keep
awake to make his calling and election sure. MacKinnon,
though rapidly coming into public notice, would require
the most support. It is self-evident, however, that if the
Liberals could induce these three democratic stalwarts to
enter the field on their behalf, and if the party at the
same time set to work to organise its voting strength,
the election could easily be carried. A fortunate feature
of the approaching contest is the fact that the Age will
not have half the influence it possessed three years ago.
Its action in supporting the lawless Irvine Government
through thick and thin has caused it to completely lose
the confidence of the Labor and Democratic party, so
that its ticket will be, in the words of Bottom, the weaver,
“ a thing of naught.”

Extract from speech by one of the hired agitators
of the Victorian Employers’ Union :
The thing killing the country to-day was a two-legged
thing called man. If capital stood solidly, the other
must go to the wall.
This amazingly frank individual, it will be
noticed, doesn’t class his Tory employers with
“man.” Heevidently considers them to be hog. At
all events he considers them to be some animal that
is distinct from “man,'’ and if his animal or beast
gets the upper hand “ man ” is to go to the wall.
The Employers’ Union, according to this frank
exponent of things, represents the revolt of the
three-legged stool, or the four-legged monstrosity,
or the cuttle-fish or the centipede with many
legs, or the boa-constrictor which has none,
or something of that sort, against the “two-legged
thing called man.” It is just as well to have
the situation explained, and the public owes a
debt of gratitude to the Employers’Union agitator
for making it clear that his party regards itself,
without any disguise whatever, as an uprising of
the brute creation and a general upheaval of
things which have no soul.
The parochial “reform” parties, the parochial
press, the parish pump politicians are all evidently
going to fight to the bitter end against the
creation of a Federal High Court and a Federal
Inter-State Commission. The bogus cry of
“economy” is the plea, but the real object is to
exalt the importance of the States and to restrict
the Federal idea. The intention is to prevent the
Australian Government exercising the power
given it by the Constitution to suppress wars of
railway rates, to prevent it having a Judiciary
of its own, and to compel it to apply for
legal decisions to Judges appointed and paid by,
and subject to, its subordinate States. In other
words, the idea is that the boss of the establish-
ment should always have to appeal for justice to
the nominee of the office-boy. The great hope of
the Australian democracy lies in the assertion
and extension of the powers of the Australian
Legislature—the only absolutely democratic Par-
liament in Australasia—and in this matter the
democratic party is not backing up the Barton
Government with half the energy it ought to
show.
According to the Melbourne daily paper report
this astounding thing has come to pass :
Justice a’Beckett, sitting in the Second Civil Court
yesterday, delivered his reserved judgment in the suit
brought by Alfred James Smith for the nullity of his
marriage with Sarah Jane Smith, on the ground that
respondent was a niece of his deceased wife, and thus
within the prohibited degrees of marriage as defined in
the Prayer Book of the Church of England, and desertion
for three years and upwards.
In Victoria marriage with a deceased wife’s sister
is legal, and the natural inference would seem to
be that marriage with a deceased wife’s sister’s
daughter would be legal also. If the lady can’t
hand down to her daughter her legal marriage
rights along with her color and her nationality,
then the latter might almost as well be the Greek
daughter of British parents, or two white people’s
black baby. The law didn’t specify that the
deceased wife’s sister’s marriage privileges would
descend to her daughter, probably because it
never expected any one to call the matter
in question, just as it never thought of pass-
ing a Bill to declare that the offspring of a
dog should be regarded as a dog and not as a
horse, and should have all the privileges, if any,
attached to its status as a dog. Anyhow, the
question having arisen, Judge a’Beckett had a
right to settle it according to the letter of the
law, or the apparent intention of the law if he
couldn’t find any letter, or according to common
sense if any such article was lying about and if no
law was available. But the Prayer Book of the
Church of England, or the Confession of Faith of
the Church of Scotland, or the bull of the Church
of Rome, or the visions of the Church of Latter-
Day Saints are not Australian law, and Justice
a’Beckett might gum that fact in his hat for
future reference.
