“ Burglar Bill ” writes :
Let me have a snarl at present police methods of
obtaining “ identifications ”of suspects. The manner in
which His Honor stresses the point, and the way it
smothers up any evidence for the defence which may
have found a corner in the juryman’s mind, proves that
it is high time the farce was conducted with some show
of dectncy. Identification is done in the secrecy of a
prison yard by a would-be Sherlock Holmes who is trying
to weld his links together, and he staggers into court under
its immense weight, and throws it with a thud on the
prisoner’s pitiful little defence so that the court resounds.
If the witness is not “primed” by a hint dropped “acci-
dentally” his vanity is scratched so vigorously by the
“tec.” that he becomes positively dangerous. “Sure
you’re bound to pick the hangdog 1" “ Faith, you’re
smart enough to pick the hard-faced devil 1 ” are not
uncommon remarks from detectives anxious to obtain a
conviction. Have known repeated cases of wit-
nesses picking the wrong man, and, in, response
to the “tec’s.” directions, to “have another
go” they have again failed. Yet nothing is heard in
court of the witness’s two unsuccessful attempts to pick
the prisoner. All the Grown might admit would be
“ that the witness failed to identify prisoner.” Have
heard second timers say that one detective in conducting
his “ identifications” always stood close up to the.*.*. right
man ” and looked intently at him while the witness was
considering. Until an improved way is found, and a
penalty provided for the flash fool who identifies the
wrong man, his Honor and the jury should push the
“ identification ” evidence aside as being entirely unre-
liable as testimony of the prisoner’s guilt.
The Kyabramapootra “Reform” party field a
violent meeting the other day to protest against
“Federal extravagance.” Being asked by Pre-
mier Barton to supply details of any past extra-
vagance it objected to, the obscene bird met
together again and screamed incoherently, but
supplied no details. For the future, however, The
Fowl objects to the establishment of a High
Court and the appointment of a Commissioner in
London. In this matter the Kyabramapootra is
as illogical, as usual. As regards the Commis-
sioner, he will be a saving, not an expense, to
Victoria, if Victoria’s Kyabram Government will
only do its share by abolishing its separate State
representative. As for the High Court, it is
needed to put the boss ’Pootra, Irvine,
in gaol next time he tries to pass
laws authorising any Victorian J.P. to
lock up the Federal Postmaster-General for 12
months for carrying by post papers that Irvine
doesn’t approve of. Also, the Commonwealth
Government has seen so much of Irvine that it
doesn’t feel inclined, if it has a legal dispute with
Victoria, to trust the decision entirely to a Vic-
torian judge whom Irvine’s Parliament can sack
if he doesn’t decide the case as the Kyabrama-
pootra would like him to do. Supreme Court
judges are certainly supposed to occupy an inde-
pendent position—yet when Chief Justice Lillet,
of Queensland, gave a decision adverse to that
State’s ’Pootra, the Boodle Government ended his
judicial career very suddenly indeed.

America’s new Immigration Law, passed 3rd
March, 1903, is far and away the most restrictive
on earth. Among totally forbidden immigrants
are now “those who have, within one year of the
date of application for admission to the U.S., been
deported as being under offers, solicitations, pro-
mises, or agreements to perform labor or service
of some kind therein.” The U.S. law would have
not only sent back the Six Hatters peremptorily ;
it would also have made them incapable of enter-
ing the country under any circumstances at all for
a whole year. Restricted immigrants who may
have managed to enter may be deported at any
time within two years. Also, others prohibited
include “any persons whose tickets may have

“Sherlock Holmes,” the detective with
the long, pale face and high and mighty ways,
returned from a six months’ tour of Maoriland to
Sydney Royal last Saturday. Individuals of theco.
all look fatter, and their playing is no thinner
Cuyler Hastings’s red dressing-gown has become
very shabby; report has it that he took the boil-
ing mud-bath cure wearing that garment. The
roles of Mabel Lane, May Chevalier, Hamilton
Stewart and Atholwood, and all the cut-throats
and safe-breakers in the piece, though familiar, are
still as acceptable as water to those a-thirst. As
for the great central character he is just as ele-
gantly impossible as ever, and his fixed expression
hasn’t thawed, and he leaves his cigar on the
mantelpiece in the darkened room with the same
disgustingly superior expression as of old. The
infallible look in Sherlock’s eye is, in itself, suffi-
cient reason why the criminal classes should seek
his death ; and when he isn’t looking infallible he
sleeps in a superior way in his arm-chair and is
almost equally aggravating. The drama might
be improved in one way if Sherlock would
give up falling in love and contemplating mar-
riage. That episode is a shock. It is an attempt
to make Shei’lock look human when he isn’t. A
sphinx shouldn’t get married—its feet are too cold
and its head too dreary. However, the drama is
a good one with all drawbacks, and as it is only
on duty for one week it is needless to speculate on
possible alterations. “If I Were King” will
follow on Saturday.
** * *
Whether Win. Anderson’s new melodrama
at Sydney Lyceum, “The Night Birds of Lon-
don,” ends as every respectable melodrama
should, this paper doesn’t know for certain. On
its previous first night the great explosion scene,
five minutes from the finish, resulted in more fire
gn the stage than the management bargained for,
and the curtain had to come down, and this
paper missed the end that time. This time the
curtain, presumably, stayed up as long as it ought
to ; but as the proceedings were continued while
the stage fire brigade was pouring water on the
burning residence of Bill Vosper, pugilist, and
the steam was hissing, and the edifice was tum-
bling to pieces, and “the house” was emptying
itself into Pitt-street, not being quite sure
whether it was all stage-play or not, the
dialogue was lost again, and the writer
can only rely upon Anderson’s well-
known sense of Lyceum justice to see
that in the last five minutes everybody
got what he or she deserved. Even as melo-
drama “The Night Birds” is poor stuff, and,
on the whole, it isn’t well played ; but it is most
excellently staged. At different times a whole
livery stable is hired to give reality to London
street scenes—broughams, cabs, fire-reels, coster
donkey-carts, horsemen, &c. To the general
observation that the acting isn’t what it might be,
a couple of exceptions may be made in favor of
Miss Fergus, in the difficult part of a mad
woman, and Miss Gorrick, the long-suffering
heroine. Miss Gorrick collapsed on the stage
early in the show on Saturday, and the curtain
had to come down for a few minutes ; but there
wasn’t any trace of the illness in her subsequent
work. ‘ 1 The Night Birds ” will prowl a week
longer.
** * *
“The Widow from Japan,” produced at
Sydney Palace by Miss Maggie Moore’s co. on
Saturday last, does not stand above the average
of widow farces. The plot is thin and improbable,
the variety business with which it is eked out
not very appropriate, and the “humor” seems
to reach its highest point in a Frenchman
getting sick from smoking. A desperately
hard piece for a co. to make anything out of 1
Miss Moore and her colleagues undertake
the task with courage, and in some of the
more broadly farcical scenes raise the loud
laugh which betokens the happy audience, as well
as the vacant mind mentioned by the poet. In
addition to Miss Maggie Moore, in the principal
part, Miss Osgood Moore acts well as Blanche
Swift, and Miss Ethel Bashford makes a good
ingenue. Wilton Welch and Milton Moss do
best of the others. This Saturday (June 27th),
“Wav Down South” will be staged, and it is
understood that Miss Maggie Moore will blacken
her fair face and become an attractive coon for
the occasion.
** * *
Harry Rickards, the “ eminent author-
actor-manager-vocalist,” designed a treat for
Sydney Tivoli patrons by making a few farewell
“appearances” this and last week, before his
departure to Europe by specially chartered mail
steamer; and his voice did not prove to be
the mere relic that might have been expected.
If a change of fortune came to Henry and he lost
his bushels of diamonds, and his herds of car-
riage horses, and his flights of motor ears,
lie could still earn an honest crust as a
“lion comique” of some mane. Rudinoff’s humor,
his artistic skill, and his frank childish vanity
improve with acquaintance. Altogether the
Tivoli has a strong programme just now.

Something right off the worn track of
entertainments is to happen at Sydney Criterion
on this Friday (26th) —a drama and a comedy
will be acted by inmates of the N.S.W. Deaf
and Dumb Institution. At annual demonstra-
tions the mutes have shown, in semi-private
and in a small way, what they can do in the
shape of the true pantomime ; but it is rarely that
the interesting experiment is tried publicly on a
large scale.

Miss Muriel Foster, daughter of the late
Madame Foster, whose name will be remembered
in Commonwealth musical circles, will give a
concert in the Centenary Hall to-night (Thursday).
Miss Foster, who has been trained by Mr. Parkin-
son, is credited with a melodious soprano.
** # *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the Frawley co.
tried its luck with “ Arizona” on Saturday night.
This full-blooded American play promises to have
more attractions for Melbourne than the Civil
War story of “ Secret Service,” which opened the
season. It is good, reasonable melodrama, intro-
ducing touches of Yankee novelty, and new
workings of old effects, and it has a breezy little
sub-plot instead of what would be irrelevant comic
relief in a melodrama from London. “ Arizona ”
will seem all the brighter when the waits between
the acts are curtailed and the principals have im-
proved on their first-night work ; for the story,
although it moves slowly at the start, has a fine,
free action from the moment it gets fairly going.
A military station is located in the neighborhood
of Canby’s ranch ; so Canby’s daughter, likewise
his female servant and other petticoats of the
district, are deeply interested in the officers of the
11th TJ.S. Cavalry. The elder Miss Can by, who has
married the Colonel, aged 52, is an unstable Trilby-
like person, and the wicked Captain controls her
with his baneful eye. If he says, “Look at me ! ”
the lady is lost. Younger Miss Can by is a wise
virgin with side-combs in her hair, and she lends
her side-combs and gives her heart to the virtuous
young Lieutenant, whilst the servant girl hovers
around sorrowfully, suppressing the fact that her
“fall”—everybody knows she fell—was due to
the Captain afore-aid. All the sinfulness of the
drama is provided by the gallant Cap. In a
rather sudden, casual way he is about to elope
with the Colonel’s wife and her jewels when the
virtuous Lieutenant intervenes to save the young
matron’s honor (and valuables) ; after which the
Colonel returns, and the guilt of the artful Cap-
tain is visited upon the other fellow, unto the
third and fourth acts, the giddy lady being con-
tent to drop her eyelids and say nuffin. All this
time the younger Miss Canby believes in the
innocence of her hero, who has retired
from the 11th Cavalry and become overseer at
the family ranch. She becomes engaged to him
with the approval of the old man. Father’s
method of giving his consent is quaint and nasty.
He plunges his hand into his pistol pocket, as
though about to shoot the suitor, but merely pro-
duces a plug of terbaccer, chews a bit off the plug
and expectorates before ordering a bottle of cham-
pagne. A lurid Italian rouseabout, who courts
the damaged servant of the household, learns that
the Captain was his predecessor, and accordingly
bullets him from the rear, whereupon the ex-
lieutenant falls under further suspicion. The last
act puts things right by ordinary melodramatic
means, and the Colonel goes off to fight the
Spaniards, intimating that he will resume pleasant
relations with his wife when he returns. Also, it
is understood that no harm will befall the furious
Italian who laid the Captain low.
• * * •
The weakest feature of the play is the
wife. Her feeble willingness to go astray with a
man who wants her jewellery repels sympathy
from the woman, and her repeutance doesn’t look
a strong safeguard against future captains. Per-
haps a powerful actress might give Mrs. Colonel
a pathetic appearance, but Miss Van Buren has
no emotional force to speak of, nor any sort of
grip of the character. The Colonel, played by
Harrington Reynolds, is another unconvincing
sufferer. He has only an air of importance and a
pompous enunciation which makes his meaning
indistinct. Daniel Frawley, as the youthful
hero, is a heavy, somewhat elderly exponent of
the undemonstrative style of acting, in which pas-
sion has to be taken for granted unless the actor can
show it in his face. Frawley’s repose is not elo-
quent. Miss Eva Denison plays the younger
sister very winsomely in this case; Plimmer is
the same restrained, business-like villain as usual,
and Hugh Ward, as the fiery Italian, is chiefly
successful in looking the part. The acting honors
of the show (which is poorly mounted) fall to
Jeffrey Williams. In “Secret Service” and
“Sans Gene” he did nothing to suggest that he
could achieve anything so happy as his character
sketch of Canby, the ranch owner. Other mem-
bers of the co. were better placed in the previous
dramas, but they help to make “Arizona” an
interesting matter.

“As You Like It” was approaching its
close at Melbourne Princess’s when this issue went
to press, and that inefficient pedestrian, old
Adam, the Blue Ribbonite, will arrive in a broken-
up condition at the fallen duke's encampment on
Friday for the last time. The Bulletin had some
idea of devoting this final week to an endeavor to
find out where the banished duke and his party
get their clothes and hats, but it decided to give
it up as being too large an inquiry. The banished
duke apparently has no means, for he has to
camp out in a windy forest. And, though the
forest yields a certain amount of provender, ward-
robe doesn’t grow thereabouts, or if it does it
isn’t visible at the date of the story—the clothes
tree isn’t loaded with any visible fruit, and the
hat shrub is not in bloom, nor does the green, un-
ripe shirt depend from a spreading bough. Yet
the duke is understood to have been banished for
many years ; and he never seems to take a fencing
contract to raise the money for clothes—and he
has clothes and not by any means bad ones, ihk
Bulletin gives the problem up, and mentions, in
a casual way, that “ Chinese Honeymoon arises
at the Princess’s on Saturday.

The Drought Drama preserves the uneven
tenor of its way at the Melbourne Royal, and the
public in general continues to be deeply moved
when Styan restores to his old woman the
wedding ring she had “ popped.’ Also Brunton s
scenery is responsible for several bursts of
applause, and the bones of the horse that died in
the foreground are a great success, as times go.
Moreover, the murder of Miss Harrie Ireland is
well received, although that pleasing adventuress
shows no special cause or reason why she should
be put to death. Baker speaks of her as an
obstacle to his marriage witli the angel who pre-
tends to get drunk at the .Hurricane Club, and
who seems likely to razzle in real earnest if she
doesn’t take care, but Baker could easily divorce
his obstacle if he felt so disposed. Rather than
go for a decree nisi he permits, his law r ful wife to
live in peace with his best girl s brother, and
because the situation is highly improper the
dramatist thought it advisable to bring the lady
to a violent end at the hands of Albert Norman.

Frank Thornton, at the Adelaide Royal,
has finished pushing people named Smith in and
out of a great number of doors, and has taken
refuge in the familiar petticoats of “Charley’s
Aunt.” Misses Belle D’Arcy and Vera Fordyce
combine good looks and good acting in sup-
port. Business has been good but varied.
On one disastrous afternoon Thornton looked
up from counting his money (as John Smith) and
remarked, sweetly: “Yes, I ought to be rich.
I’ve been giving a matinee.” Willoughby, at the
Tivoli, ended up with one performance of “The
Wrong Mr. Wright,” and has departed Brisbane-
wards. A Melbourne co., with Beatrice Esmond
as star, is playing in the northern towns. Also
the more-or-less gifted amateur has taken posses-
sion of suburban halls of late, four distinct com-
panies of him and her giving more-or-less good
renderings of “East Lynne,” “The Octoroon,”
“As Once in May,” “In Honor Bound,” and
other things.

The affairs of Dick Golds' rough, who died in
18S6, are in the hands of ttie lawyers, not for the first
time. The Trustees, Executors and Agency to. (Melb.)
is suing the former trustees of Goldsbrough’s estate on
account of certain alleged extensive breaches of trust,
whereby Salathiel Booth, R. G. Row and T. Walsh did
during, or after, their trusteeship get unlawfully indebted
to the estate aforesaid. The case, on the face of it,
reveals an awfully tangled condition of financial deals,
and needs a lot of legal unravelling. It seems to be the
fate of big Victorian estates to feed the lawyers Herity,
Monaghan, Goldsbrough, and many other well-known
capitalists of the long ago, ought to have a joint marble
memorial erected to them bv the Vic. Lawyers’ Institute.

Hades is an ice-house compared with the “fiery
furnace” raised by Frank Cotton at the Technical
College, Sydney, on Monday last. He exhibited the
power of his Patent Liquid Fuel Burner as a means of
treating the most rebellious ores. Two hundred Minis-
ters, members, mining magnates and minnows crowded
round the smelter, which in one hour was raised from a
tomb-like coldness to a temperature of 4000 degrees. The
s nelter itself cost but £25 to erect and the nozzle of his
burner playing therein had but one-tenth of an inch aper-
ture. Such was the terrific heat of the hydro-carbon gas
generated in the 141 b. burner by the admixture of dry
steam and residual oil that in three minutes, by the
watch, copper ore was bubbling like fat in a frying-pan.
The real eye-opener was the reduction of a parcel of New
Caledonia nickel ore in two houis.

Premier Seddon, of M. L., has at last, apparently,
made up his mind as to what he is going to do in con-
nection with the Bank of N.Z. ; and he isn’t going to do
the best thing, although he is not going to do the worst.
There were three roads to take —to turn the concern into
a State Bank, to go into partnership with the lank, and
to continue to merely serve as a prop. Seddon decides
for the second. The country, he says, isn’t ripe for a
State Bank. It is ripe for State life insurance, State
accident insurance, State fire insurance, and even State
mutton shops, but it is not ripe for a State Bank. Nine
years ago, it was ripe enough to take the responsibilities
of banking ; it put £2,000,000 into the Bank of N.Z. The
next \ ear it took up £500,000 worth of preference shares,
and gave a further guarantee. For several years past it
has also had the greater pai t of the management of the
bank. The only serious difference, in fact, between the
Bank of N.Z. and a State Bank has been that the State
hasn’t had the profits ; and if a country isn’t ripe enough
to take the profits after taking the risks and providing
most of the management, it must be a very gteen
country indeed.
The Bank of N.Z.’s. position is something like
this One morning in ’94 the Government was told that
if it didn’t hand over £2,000,000 promptly there would be
a mighty smash. It handed over the two millions as
meekly as anybody who ever looked down a highway-
man’s pistol, and the mighty smash was averted. Yet,
so utterly and hopelessly insolvent was the concern, and
so successful had the first expedition been, that next year
the Government received another visit; this time with a
demand for a heavy guarantee and more cash. It was a
case of back-up or forfeit, and the State backed-up Using
State money, and supported by State guarantees, and
with State management, the bank soon began to nuke
money, and to buy back the preference shares. It has
every year increased in prosperity ; yet into such an
appalling mess had private enterprise got it that after
nine years there is still probably nearly £1,000,000 of
deficiency to wipe out in the Assets Realisation depart-
ment —or there was at the end of the hank’s last year.
From being a wild cat bank, which could only have kept
going by paying its creditors very little in the£, the
State has turned it into a bank which is making a profit
of some £200,000 a year, which expects to pay off all its
old deficits inside seven years (probably much sooner),
and which has actually begun to pay dividends
to its shareholders —those shareholders who were hope-
lessly’ lost 10 years ago, and who were saved by, the
State’s benevolence. And the State, whose money and
guarantee did all this, has made nothing out of it all.
And still “ the country is not ripe for a State Bank . it
is only ripe enough to prop up private banks and earn
dividends for mostly absentee private shareholders.
The Government is told that it can’t seize.the
bank by main force and turn it into a State
that if it wanted to purchase the bank £l,(XX),uw
would be asked for the goodwill. M hat, how-
ever, the shareholders really own is a bank whicn
has still an insolvent Assets Realisation branch, an
which is still dependent on State support. If 1
State called in its two millions on March 31, ! iext ; a ']
intimated that thereafter it would have nothing to
with the business, and wouldn’t guarantee
in any way, and wouldn’t do any ordinary banKi „
business with it, and if the State handed hac

In connection with the Penrith Scheme for the
Sydney Water Supply, the New South Wales Government
made a contract with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.
of South Melbourne for the supply of a Triple Expansion
Condensing Duplex Pumping Engine capable of raising
four million gallons of water per day to a height of 250
feet. As later on it is proposed to remove these pumps
to Sydney' to supply the high levels, the engines have
been fitted with a second set of pumps with a capacity
of one and a half million gallons .per day delivered lo a
height of seven hundred feet. The contract time for
manufacture and delivery' of the above plant was 14 weeks',
but the whole was completed and delivered within eleven
weeks. The Austral Otis company' are manufacturers of
every class of water works pumping plant and carry
large stocks of pumps suitable for Mines and Irrigation.*

York ; and represented by Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, of

distinct from any other make of so-called “Cameron’
Pump. They work automatically, and have no dead
centre, neither have they springs nor outside valve gear
to become deranged. They occupy less space in a shaft
than any other Sinking Pump of same capacity.*

The action to settle the rights of Sulphide Cor-
poration (Broken Hill) preference shareholders has gone
against the prefs. in the British courts. A profit was
made in 1900-1, but was not distributed, as it was thought
that the money might be wanted in the working. The
prefs. claimed that, when it became available, it should
be paid to them as their dividend for 1900-1. The Court
holds that, if money is carried forward in this way, the
prefs. are not entitled to it subsequently.

to the bank by Act of Parliament its Assets
Realisation concern and the deficit belonging thereto
and left the bank on its own resources—that goodwill
would take a lot of discovering. The Bank of NZ. would
then once more be simply an institution with not
enough assets to square liabilities, unless the share-
holders were prepared to put up a large amount of new
capital; and a large proportion of its best business would
be gone. The bank, therefore, isn’t master of the
situation yet by a long way ; and if Seddon were a little
riper (for it is Seddon and not the country that isn’t ripe)
he might still deal with it in much the same way that it
dealt with the Government in ICJ4. But the Government
need not do as badly as that. The Government found
the shares worth nothing at all, and it, and it alone, has
made them worth a good deal ; and the State should’pay
the holders full and liberal value. Even then, of course
the shareholders would still have gained enormously by
the transaction, and the State would have lost ; but one
way or other the State generally does lose.

The Liverpool and London and Globe In-

Total funds of the co.’s life and annuity departments are
now £5,583,493. The year 1902 was, it will be seen, the
least profitable for many years. Income was less than
usual, expenses a little more, while claims were unusually
heavy. The Liverpool and London and Globe does a
very large annuity business ; nearly 40 per cent, of its
total income is derived from this source. And, this
being a very cheap business to handle, the effect is
shown in the aggregate expense rate. For 1901 it was
£7 18s. per cent, of total income ; for last year it was £8
Is. lOd. per cent. Like many other English co.’s, this
one doesn’t hustle for business in bad times ; it doesn’t
reckon that, whatever comes, it must show a steadily in-
creasing premium income ; and so it avoids that heavy
rise in expenses which almost inevitably follows on
the attempt to show hooin results in' bad times.
On the life business alone, and charging the whole
of the dividend to this department, the expenses for 1901
amounted to £lO 18s. per cent, of the income of that
department ; for 1902, they amounted to £lO 16s. The
real figure is less than that, because the annuity business
pays part at least of the dividend ; but how much can’t
be said definitely. If the dividend is charged to the two
accounts in proportion to income, then the total expense
rate in the life department amounts to £8 19s. Bd. per
cent., which is a very excellent record. In addition to
the funds of the life and annuity departments stated
above at £5,583,493, there is a general reserve fund
amounting to £1,300,000 : but this is, of course, available
in connection with the company’s fire business as well.
Still it adds another buttress to an already solid-looking
concern.
• * * *
The local authorities of the big Melbourne
suburbs of Fletnington and Essendon have made a
provisional contract with A. E. Morgans, of Westralia
and elsewhere, the latter to provide an electric tramway,
lighting, heat, and power system. The people are to pay
about a third more than Melb. people for the light, and
as much for the tram service as the syndicate can get
out of them. The usual bait is offered in the shape of
powers to acquire the service in a few years—the lighting
plant, etc., may be had in 14 years, or again at the end
of 20 years and 25 years, on “ fair terms.” The tramways
go to the towns in 30 years on payment for the free-
holds only. It always looks very tempting, this offer
of a waterworks, or a tramway, or some other
thing of the kind, in 28 years, or 30 years, for
nothing; but as the 28 years or 30 years move
along the people begin to find out that they are paying
for the thing all the time, and that, anyway, it was only
built to last 25 years, and that it won’t be of any value
when they do get it. Also they discover that, no matter
how inconvenient it gets, or what improvements have
been made elsewhere, the syndicate won’t improve its
service ; it has only got a few years more to run it, and
it won’t spend money on what it will have to give up
presently. So the people have to put up with services
which they wouldn’t tolerate if they hadn’t been fools
enough to job away their rights, and they have to pay a
great deal more for a bad service than they could get a
good one for. And in the end they usually find that,
barring the experience, what they inherit when the 30
years expires isn’t worth mentioning.

* * » *
Illawarra Steam Navigation Co. (N.S.W.) has
had an interesting history. YVayback in the 40’s a Cap-
tain Baxter owned a little schooner, which he ran in a
go-as-you-please style between Sydney and the southern
ports as far as Shellharbor ; but, as this method of trading
didn’t suit some of the hustling pioneers of those days,
John Conway and a few others clubbed together, bought
a schooner, which they called the Dairymaid, and, by
•charging fair rates and having fixed days for running,
they soon got all the trade and ran Baxter off. Then,
some capitalists of Sydney, seeing the good trade the
little schooner was doing, put on a small steamer, and
soon ran off the schooner. But the old South Coasters
were real grit, and they sold their schooner and
bought a steamer, which they also christened the Dairy-
maid, and soon got most of the trade back again.
There was now trade enough for two boats, and so the
pair ran in opposition for a time, until a good offer was
made for the Dairymaid, and the two boats, united,
formed the beginning of the Illawarra S.N. Co.

* * * *
The shocking mess in which Broken Hill finds
itself for want of water illustrates what may happen to a
dry town which trusts to “ private enterprise ” to keep
its tap running. Broken Hill has known for years that
it didn’t have a good enough supply ; again and again it
has been within speaking distance of famine. A supple-
mentary supply, owned by anybody else, would, how-
have out into the existing water co.’s profits ; and,
as the co. has to hand over the whole of its works for
nothing in a few years, it naturally wasn’t willing to
B P® nd m pney on a supplementary scheme itself. Its
chief business was to earn dividend's—that is what it was
formed for. To keep the people and the mines supplied
with water was a secondary matter. Every pro-
posal to add to the supply, and to put the
mining industry beyond serious danger of collapse
t^ )m . ™ ls cause, was, therefore, quietty but
effectively opposed, sometimes in one way, sorhetimes in
another ; sometimes openly, more often secretly and with
great cunning. The success of this policy- was made
easily possible by the appalling ignorance of. the average
-Sidney officer with regard to far-western conditions, and
bi the selfishness and short-sightedness of the mining cos.
A* to the officers, they generally forgot that a supply
which would be adequate for coastal places is not
adequate for a place subject to long droughts, and where
the loss by evaporation runsto about 10ft. 6in. a year. As
to the mining companies, though a largo supply of water
is now absolutely indispensable to them, seeing that the
whole of their ores must be locally concentrated before
they can be smelted, and though they have been well aware
for years that the existing supply was inadequate and
likely to fail, they have taken no real steps to provide
water for themselves. They have talked about it ; they
have made references to it in their reports ; they have
even pegged out catchment areas. And then the Water
Supply Co. has moved in its mysterious way, and the
mining co.s’ projects have been dropped. So far, it hasn’t
mattered much. If there hasn’t always been water, there
has at least so far always been mud. But now, even the
supply of pumpable mud is about at an end —it has come
to an end sooner than was expected. What is going to
happen this writer doesn’t know. The Owe’Sullivan may
carry enough water bv train from S.A. to keep the town
going ; but water cannot be carried 60 or SO miles at a
rate that will allow of its use in ore-concentration or for
steam-raising. The mines may be able to draw some
supply from the soakages at Acacia and from the old
workings ; yet there are but small possibilities in either
of those schemes.
The Acacia Trust, which may possibly be able
to supply the mines with a small quantity of water, was
formed when water was scarce 12 years or so back, in the
days when John Howell was general manager of the B.H.
Prop. ; it was he who discovered this possible source of
supply and projected the trust. It is, however, a very
small affair. The Acacia is a shallow depression, 20 or
30 square miles in area, formed of soft limestone'; and by
sinking in this formation a fair supply of soakage water
can be got; there is a pump-line to the mines. As,
however, there has been less than Bin. of rain over this area
in the last 20 months, and as the supply has already been
freely tapped, it is very doubtful whether what remains
is worth mentioning. The trust consists of the largest of
the mining co.’s.
* * * *
What little water there is left at Broken Hill
will be used for emergency purposes. Ordinary milling
operations ceased at the B.H. Proprietary, Block 10, and
Central (Sulphide Corporation) on Tuesday morning ; the
South closed in the afternoon ; the British on Wednes-
day. The decision to cease work was not expected so
soon. A little mining continues ; but as the ore can’t be
used without concentration, and as it can’t now be con-
centrated, the few miners who are left at work will be put
off in a few days. It is a stunning blow ; but it will have
its uses—. Broken Hill will get another water supply now ;
and nothing less than such a blow seemed capable of
rousing those interested to realise the necessity of it.
* * * *
The Commission appointed to inquire into the
underground smash at the Broken Hill Central mine in
October last has made a colorless report. Nobody was to
blame, and the system of mining couldn’t be improved.
In Other words, smashes are unavoidable, and a few
miners must expect tp die now and again as a mere
matter of business. No doubt that fact will be remem-
bered when it is being considered what is a fair price to
pay the miner. The bodies of the two men who were
buried when this particular creep occurred, nearly nine
months ago, have not, by the wav, been recovered yet.
* * '* *
Mr. Kendall’s report on the Great Cobar (N. S. W.)
and associated properties is a voluminous document. The
great expert is all enthusiasm respecting the shows.
There is not a qualification or a suggestion of hesitation
in the whole of the report. It is summarised by Mr.
Kendall himself as follows:
Extent of property : The different works reported on
are : Great Cobar copper mine, Nymagee copper mine,
Peak and Conqueror gold mines, coal mines at Lithgow,
coal mines and coke works at Rix’s Creek, the smelter at
Cobar, the smelter at Nymagee, the refinery at Lithgow.
Ore blocked out; At Cobar 1,415,000 tons ; at Nymagee
104,800 tons.
Assay value of ore in sight : At Cobar, of 201,500 tons,
•6 per cent, copper and Tosoz. gold per ton ; of 1,213,000
tons, 3‘79 per cent, copper, '0950z. gold, and - 6730 z.
silver per ton. At Nymagee, copper, 3'2 per cent.
Ore probable, but not blocked out : At Cobar, 534,000
tons ;at Nymagee, 128,000 tons This is exclusive of the
large bodies of ore that must exist below the present
workings.
Coal: At Lithgow, 2,100,000 tons ; at Rix’s Creek,
5,088,000 tons, and 40 coke ovens.
Output of refined copper : 14,000 tons a year.
Cost of producing refined copper : £33 per ton.
Assumed selling price of copper : £52 per ton for
G.M.B.’s, and £56 for best selected, or its equivalent, over
the next few years ; to this is to be added the value of the
precious metals from Cobar, i.e., £9 12s. 6d. per ton,
making together £65 12s. 6d. per ton.
Probable profit, £450,000 a year.
Expenditure on works necessary to obtain this profit,
£160,000.
Time required to effect improvements, 12 months from
date of commencing actual work on the ground.
Working capital required, £240,000.
Probable profit from working with present plant:
Taking the price of G.M.B. for the next 15 months at
£5B per ton, the profit would be at the rate of about
£95,000 a year.
“ The above low cost of electrolytic copper as compared
with that of producing the same material at Anaconda
(Montana, U.S.A.) is due to the great facilities attaching
to these properties. (1) A trifling amount of water in the
mines, little timber needed, and an ore which can be
treated in the furnace direct, thus avoiding the great
loss incurred by preliminary water concentration. (2)
Much lower wages in all departments. (3) Very much
cheaper fuel for converting and refining.”
Under the head “General observations,” Mr. Kendall
reports : “I consider this a most valuable property, and
feel satisfied that with proper management the figures
I have given above will be fully realised. The ore
blocked out is equal to 11 years’ output at the present
rate, or nearly four years’ at the increased rate. In
addition to this there is nearly a year and a half’s output
at the increased rate, or over years’ at the old rate,
which, although not blocked out, I consider will most
certainly come out of the property. Then there are
very large possibilities below the present bottom levels
of the mine which are m>£ taken into account in the
above estimates.”
This report must be pleasant reading to the
syndicate which holds the option on the properties ; but
will it please the present owners to be told, on such
authority, that the application of less than the profits of
21 years to development and equipment would leave them
the possessors of mines which would return a profit of
£450,000 a year—mines which they are selling for a trifle
over £1,000,000? If, in the face of such a report, the
deal is not promptly completed, there must be something
rotten in the state of the London mining market.
♦ * * »
Several Melbourne and Sydney speculators
visited the Shuttleton district (N.S.W.) last week, and all
speak in most enthusiastic terms as to the prospects of
the mines there. In the Crowl Creek mine splendid
faces of sulphide ore of good grade are showing in all
parts at the deepest levels, and there is now every assur-
ance that the mine has a great future. The erection of
furnaces is to be proceeded with at once, and it is to be
expected that they will be in operation in about two
months. The directors of the Shuttleton Syndicate—
a Sydney company—have agreed upon a systematic
course of prospecting operations. On two of the blocks
held by them the work already done has revealed
splendid indications, and in Shuttle’s block in particular
it is reported that there is every probability of payable
ore being met with,
* * * *
The failure of the dredges on the Turon
(N.S. W.) has been a great disappointment to the residents
who looked forward to fabulous returns such as would
revive interest in the once famous field. Attention is
now being directed to the auriferous rocks, and the
“ oldest inhabitant ” who knows where there are moun-
tains of gold waiting simply for a little capital to expose
them is busy airing his imagination. The old Queens-
lander mine, near Sofala, which has been tried bj T several
syndicates—the last an Adelaide crowd guided by J. 0. F.
Johnson—is reported to have been acquired by a Si dney
brewer, and a tram-line two miles and a half long and a 100-
head stamp mill on the Turon are talked of. Formation
is diorite interspersed with stringers of quartz and
mineral. There is plenty of rock and a paucity of gold.
Rock is intensely hard, and even if it carries as much
gold as is alleged, it is questionable whether it could be
worked at a profit commensurate with the enormous out-
lay on plant which would be necessary to deal with it on
a large scale. At Hill End something worse than stagna-
tion exists. A recent visitor astonished the publican by
producing a £5 note. He bad to beg and borrow from
almost every person in the place to raise enough
to provide the change. The deep tunnel scheme
to prospect Hawkins Hill is still talked about. It will
almost certainly never get beyond that stage. A Sydney
company is handling the King’s Reef mine with some
vigor. If it can produce bv the ton stone equal to that
shown in Sydney by the lb. it will create a sensation.
Old mines, like old inhabitants, are frequently' unreliable ;
but Ravenswood (Q.) affords a recent instance of the fact
that a deserted field is not always a valueless one, and if
a resurrectionist at Hill End or Sofala accidentally struck
another “ Holterman,” people would speedily be fall-
ing over each other to get in to the abandoned and
discredited shows. As they would probably be quite up
to the average of new ones, no great harm, and a certain
amount of good, might result therefrom.
* ■* * *
During the six months ended February last the
net trading profits of the Smelting Co. of Australia
(Dapto, N.S.W.) were £18,454. An arrangement has been
entered into with a syndicate which is to provide 60,000
tons of smelting ore per annum for five years and to
guarantee substantial divs to the co. The scheme, it is
stated, will necessitate doubling the capacity of fhe
works. The chairman at a recent meeting stated that the
business of the co. was conducted on “ fair and equitable
principles.” Owners of copper mines in N.S.W. will
hardly endorse this statement; they fail to recognise the
fairness or equity' of a deduction of £l2 10s. off the value
of copper, in addition to the ordinary smelting charges.

Mt. Boppy (near Cobar), at a meeting in Lon-
don on April 18th, declared its second dividend of
Is. 3d. per share on 110,000 shares, and carried forward a
small balance from last year’s profits. Stated at the
meeting, by chairman Hoffnung, that a substantial
interim dividend would be forthcoming on receipt of
returns to 31st March. For the quarter £13,500 had been
remitted, but a further amount was expected. Present
appearances of the mine indicate that this substantial
return will be maintained. April return wasl97loz. from
2528 tons. Probability is that the new manager has
improved on this. His name is Dave.v —a cousin Jack for
a certainty—but not the voluble T.G. who used to run
the Harrietvihe mines in Victoria. T.G. is now a copper
expert, and is mainly responsible for the reports on
deposits in Africa, which, if true, mean that copper will
soon be as cheap as coal.

Reported that an offer of £4o,ooo—£Booo in
cash, and balance out of gold won from the mine—
has been refused without hesitation by the eight
proprietors of the Sullivan’s Gully mine, near Cano-
windra (N.S.W.); £50,000 cash being their valuation
of mine. “Blessed is the man who sells” is in most
cases a safe axiom, but, now and again, as witness
the case of Mount Morgan, it is an unsafe one.

Lake View Consols (W.A.) during May treated
7541 tons, or 1000 tons more than usual, for 484C0z., or
1500 oz. less than usual ; and no forecast of this serious
decline was given. The publication of the result was, in
fact, delayed for a week, during which period scrip
dropped back to 435. Somebody must have been busy
getting out. Rumors of a serious decline in the yield
were bruited about the market, but, until the official
figures were published, nothing definite was known. The
above return shows an average of 12'7dwt. per ton,
which is the best the mine can muster from the ore
reserves blocked out. Even on this reduced return there
is an alleged profit of £4600, subject to deduction of
London charges.
» * * *
Great Boulder Prop. (W.A.) appears to be in
luck, as it is alleged that the boundary lode has made
back into Boulder ground at 10 level, after being entirely
in the Horseshoe from 2 to 9 level. This lode is known
as Horseshoe 4, and is probably the richest lode on the
field. The underlie of the Boulder main lode also
changed at depth from west to east, the lode being 52ft.
nearer the shaft at 1600 than 1400 ft. Co’s, main shaft is
well on the way to 1800 ft., but, as there are ample
reserves, this work is not being unduly pushed.
* * * *
Of Oroya-Brown Hill (W.A.) it is said that
quarterly divs. of 2s. 6d. a share will be paid in future
commencing with September. This should make the
shares a good investment. The Oroya blocks are practi-
cally virgin mines of great possibilities, so the co. should
have a brilliant future. The serial tram is well on the
way to completion, and the foundations for the extra
10-head are being pushed on. When this work is
complete the mill should treat 5000 tons monthly for
about 10,000 oz. With this increase in output costs
should be substantially reduced.
* * * *
Kalgurli (W.A.) lias opened up so well lately,
and the ore reserves have increased so much, that the
management has practically decided to increase the capa-
city of the plant to 6000 tons monthly, or fully 50 per
cent. On this the co. should easily earn divs.'of 4s. a
quarter. At the 850 ft. level the ore is worth 18dwt. for a
width of 27ft., while at 1000 ft. the north stope is yielding
3oz. stone, the richest found for some time. The lodes
in the mine are very wide, running in places to 60ft., so
that the mine would not be strained by the proposed
increase in output.
/ * * * *
Westralia Mt. Morgans is being very vigorously
developed at all levels, most of the driving being to the
south, following the dip of the chutes into the Guest
property acquired last year by the co. Large reserves of
ore are being laid open owing to the enormous size of the
lode. At the 300 ft. level the stopes are quite 100 ft. wide.
The ore is becoming so hard in the lower levels that it is
proposed to erect another 40 head of 17501 b. stamps. The
present 60 head mi)l, half 12501 b. and half 15001 b. stamps,
is treating 6000 tons monthly, or a shade over 3 tons per
stamp daily. The ore now averages 15dwt., but a higher
average is expected later on. A number of improvements
are being made to increase output and reduce costs. Co.
is at present working five levels, and the main shaft is
down 580 ft.
* * * *
Millionaire, Mt. Morgans (W.A.), one of A. E.
Morgans’ flotations, has had a disappointing first clean
up; 494 tons only averaged B£dwt. A trial of 200 tons,
treated at a neighboring mill some months ago, averaged
22dwt., so the falling off is very marked. Co. has a
large lode, and working costs are low, so that even 81dwt.
ore should yield fair profit; but a better average is ex-
pected in future. Co. is in 125,000 shares of £1 each
113,000 of them issued. Much development has been
done since co.’s flotation three years ago, and the 5-head
mill is expected to soon treat 800 tons of ore monthly.
Associated Northern (W.A.) has already carried
its main shaft far past the point where the dianv ml
drill penetrated an alleged gold chute, without locating
any gold-bearing ore. Both Prof. LiVeing and Manager
Roberts are very reticent regarding develo] ments, hut
there is little doubt that the bulk of ttie rich ore has
been fairly well gutted out, and it is questionable if tl ere
will be any more shipments after the June parcel. The
third borehole on the south end of the Iron Monarch
lease has fulfilled its mission, but<the resuh is being kept
a profound secret. The “ dirty era ”in W.A. mining is
said to have ended ; but, judging from the secrecy dis-
played by the management and the enormous drop in
the scrip, which foretells a drop in returns, the “dirty
era” is still with us. Co.’s plant, now on order, will
consist of Merton’s furnaces and the largest size of bail
mills.
» * * *
Great Fingall Consols (W.A.) is still forgirg
ahead. During May the 60-head mill treated the record

What is, however, still more satisfactory is that the gold

SOCIETY
The pot knows the complexion of the
kettle. When Prahran council lately declined to
“ part ” a brass farthing towards the up-keep of
Melbourne’s fever hospital, its mayor, out of the
depths of his knowledge, summed up Thomas
Bent in these words: “It has been stated that
the council will be compelled to contribute by Act
of Parliament. While Mr. Bent is Minister of
Health no Bill will be brought in to compel any
municipality to do anything.” Whereupon
another heavy shopkeeper, who was quite willing
that a splendid hospital should lie idle and the
sick poor die in the gutter if he could thereby
avoid a halfpenny increase in the rates, “ I can
see it will cost £6OOO a year to run the hospital. . .
Let us keep out of it as long as we can.” And yet
all the time these weighty Bumbles are paying
three times as much as they need for the treat-
ment of fever cases in gaol.
* * * *
Victoria still hears at intervals of that
new sect, the Flitterers, which arose at Bendigo
and resolved never to do any more work. The
other day Mrs. Flitterer, junior, wife of one of
the most frantic apostles of the new faith, asked
BIS ONE EWE
LAMB.
“ Cockerill seems
awfully fond of his
new wife.”
“ Naturally! it’s the only one he's got now.”
for a Warrant for Flitterer’s arrest for desertion.
He was, she said, in Queensland, and she had
written to him for assistance, but all the Answer
she got was a glowing account of numerous con-
versions to the new gospel of leaving off work,
and of the great good the apostles were doing,
and a strenuous injunction to depend on theLoard
for tucker for herself and infant. Quite a number
of new religions commence by leaving the wife
and baby in the lurch while the male support of
the household is away expounding at street
corners.
Even the lying and swindling importer has
his clerical apologist. Thus Dr. Marshall, recently,
at Scots Church, Melbourne:
I can easily conceive that, as a great moral danger to
the future well-being of our young Australian Common-
wealth, the enactment or administration of our customs
laws may tend to abolish a keen sense of moral rectitude
and self-respect in the public conscience by putting in the
pillory of official condemnation men who in thought and
purpose would scorn to do anything mean, not to say
fraudulent, though they may be held responsible for a
mere technical offence. Indeed, it is one of the gravest
moral dangers in the life of any community when the
majesty of the law lends itself to any political process
that may result in branding honest men as criminals.
Wise statesmanship, to say nothing of fair moral senti-
ment, should bo careful to avoid even the semblance of
such a sin.
The rich, unpatriotic importer who forges invoices
and swindles his country seems to be quite the
church’s ideal of an “honest man.” And a v'
number of people don’t go to church simply
because they don’t care to associate with parsons
who have dirty ideals of that kind.
* * * *
W. T. Stead’s spookish “Julia” fore-
told, as early as March last, so Stead says, the
murder of the Servian king. Why don’t these
people back their spooks ? Stead, if he had any
confidence in Julia, might have insured Alexan-
der’s life for a quarter of a million or so. The
same with the American rain-fakir, Dr. M‘Carthy,
who isn’t going to make 3in. of rain at Broken Hill
because that dry town won’t put up £2soo—only
£2500 ! Why, M'Carthy can make £lOO,OOO inside
a month by backing his spook. All he has to do
is to buy a few thousand Water Co. and mining
shares, then streak along and fill everything in
Broken Hill that will hold water, and call back to
lift the 25 per cent, rise that will have taken
place in the price of stocks. Or he might buy up
the middle of Australia for a farthing or two per
square mile, and sell it in 80-acre farm and garden
blocks, each block furnished with a fake of guar-
anteed 25in. rain power, at about £5 an acre. Yet
Stead wastes his time hawking a sixpenny maga-
zine, and M‘Carthy cadges round for mere thou-
sands and doesn’t get them.

Tom Bent has put himself in a position to
hear a good deal of what happens at the Yic.
Railway Commissioners’ office, for the new mes-
senger who haunts the premises is Terrible
Tommy’s son-in-law, formerly a head porter at
Spencer-st. station. The only visible reason for
appointing this well-seasoned porter to the light
and simple duties of messenger boy lies in his
relationship to Bent, the howling advocate of
small economies. Maybe Bent has instructed him
to qualify himself for the position of “ loyalist ”
Commissioner, on the off chance that Mr. Tait
may be bullied into a strike.
* * * *
Premier Seddon’s great idea of sending 26
members of Parliament and a large quantity of
liquor on a cruise through Polynesia to view his
prospective empire of pin-point islands and reefs
with wrecked ships on them was not a great
success. One of the chief troubles was a politician
with few clothes on, and those mostly torn, who
gave way to excitement, and hollered loudly,
and waved his arms like flails for about a week at
a stretch. Also there were other troubles. A
rather disgusted band came home a few days
before the date originally intended, and a lot of
them will never smell whisky again without
thinking of Polynesia.
Justice Cooper, of Queensland, in sen-
tencing a returned warrior to five years’ imprison-
ment for robbery with violence :
It did not follow that because defendant fought for his
country that he could return and commit crime with
impunity. . . Men like the prisoner were a sort of
human wolves, savage, unscrupulous, utterly careless of
the feelings or rights or sufferings of other men, greedy
to possess their property, reckless of consequences.
How many such human wolves did Australia
send to unhappy S’Africa ? And how many
judicial protests were there at the time?
It was confidently expected that when that
base malefactor who stole the Cardinal’s night-
shirts discovered who owned them
he would repent. One morning,
the whisper sped through the
cathedral that the thief, God
rest his sowl, had repented, and
returned the ill-gotten duds, and
the good Fathers gathered round
a bulky parcel, which the Dean
opened, to find—some soiled
lady’s garments. The Black
Protestant again.
FRIENDS WERE SAYING “ GOOD-BYE,
GOOD-BYE.”
“ Hop” left on Saturday for a holiday tour
round the planet.
Since the last local option poll Maoriland
hasn’t the least idea where it “ are.” No provision
was made by the Legislature for a second poll if
the first poll was declared void for any reason,
and in nearly every district where “reduction”
or “no license” was carried, Mr. Public-house
lodged a petition to have the poll upset, on the
ground that the returning officer showed afi.un-
due tendency to wear elastic-side boots, or that
he had a maiden aunt who suffered from paralysis.
In most cases Mr. Public-house won his case. At
Newtown prohibition was carried, but the poll
was declared invalid, and at the first meeting of the
licensing bench the chairman told the committee
things would remain in statu quo. The other five,
who were all prohibitionists, replied that they
didn’t care a wisp of hay for the legal aspect of
the case, and wiped out every license in the place.
They have behind them the moral force of the
no-license majority, and Mr. Public-house has
the law behind him, only he will probably have
to apply for that law to a very unsympathetic
court, and anything may happen.
* * * *
Re the passing of the old order;
Dr. Norton Manning’s death recalls a Jot of the
abominations which the good and supreme old conserva-
tism tolerated even in recent times. Not very many
years ago we got the “cat” out of the common J.P.’s
hand. But Bumble and Podsnap still had their fling
amongst the sick and the afflicted. Norton Manning,
with Parkes behind him, won for the insane the humane
treatment they now receive. And how hard it is to
realise now that yet another department remained with
all the rags, shackles, scars and blood stains of the old
times about it right down to the 80’s. Dr. Markellar
found the N.S.W. Quarantine and Health system at that
date foul and corrupt, and consequently cruel and
cowardly, as a convict lazar-house at. Saghalien or a
bureau at Tobolsk. So recently as 1881, small pox patients
were run down to South Head on the open deck' of a
launch, poked along past stacked coffins to the isolation
pens on a hulk, served with food on 20ft. poles, dragged
out with hooks when dead, and buried in quicklime
graves. The good old Herald and all that cult never
heard or smelled it. Mackellar swept that away, and in
subsequent organisations did the good work of his life.
He is dickering up again now with a Neglected Children’s
Bill, but the fear is that Toryism has him too tight for
any thorough reform.
• * * *
“ 6 x 8 ” to The Bulletin :
What has often pleasantly struck me in Australia is the
almost total absence of the wet-nurse system so flourish-
ing in England, America and elsewhere. It’s a cheerful
fact and healthy sign that nearly all Australian mothers
rear their own babies. In England fully 60 per cent, of
middle-class and practically all Society babes are reared
on the milk of strangers ! Many medical men assert that
numbers of children thus reared inherit the charac-
teristics, inclinations, temperaments and general dispo-
sitions of their -wet-nurses. The presence of the pro-
verbial “black sheep” in many a highly respectable
family is thus probably often accounted for by the wet-
nurse who reared it.
It is, unfortunately, not a fact that nearly all
Australian mothers rear their own babies. Con-
sidering social circumstances, they resort to
artificial feeding to a far greater extent than in
Britain, though it is the bottle and not the wet-
nurse to which they turn. The “ black sheep ”of
the “ respectable English family ” needn’t be put
down to the wet-nurse. There is generally quite
sufficient explanation in the history of his pro-
genitors. And why not charge the respectable
members of the family to the wet-nurse ?
A report (in the D.T.) on Wentworth
Falls (N.S.W.) Consumptives’ Sanatorium, by a
cured patient, is very satisfactory. When he
entered, the upper half of his left lung had col-
lapsed. He followed the regime of the place-
rise at 7, cold bath, then plentiful meals and
gradually increasing exercise. Each patient had;
three pints of milk a day, plenty of meat, farina-
ceous foods and bread and butter. They lived;
practically in the open air ; half the wall space of
the sleeping room was open day and night in cold,
frosty weather. After three weeks there was a
marked improvement. Special dumb-bell exercise,
was then prescribed to strengthen the lung. Soon.,
after the patient left—cured. /

It was reasonably expected that the new
Bishop of Melbourne would be in favor of pulling
down the useless old pro-Cathedral in Little
Collins-st. and selling the valuable land. This,
decayed tabernacle, erected on a tract of wilder-
ness which is nowadays hemmed in by ware-
houses, provides a fat income for the aged incum-
bent, Dean Vance, and benefits nobody else, at
present. The Bishop intends that it shall benefit,
another member of the profession. He has in-
vited a London shepherd (from the Whitechapel!
district) to come along and find rest as the curate
of Dean Vance’s church. The new man from
crowded Whitechapel will have little to do beyond
praying for commercial parishioners whose homes
are pitched far from Little Collins-st. Presumably
lie is a person in delicate health, and a friend of
the Bishop, and the change to Australia will suit
his complaint.
NOT FOR THE PRICE.
“I saw your new baby to-day; isn’t he-small?”
“ Oh, well, we only pay our doctor sixpence a
week.”
Another death under chloroform “in a
dentist’s chair ” is reported from Tas. The
amount of chloro. necessary to be administered
for a dental operation is not very large and should
not be dangerous ; but it adds enormously to the
risk if the patient is in a chair. Chloro. should
be taken lying down. '
AUTRE TEMPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The R.O. Archbishop of Adelaide sent £5 to the local
Hebrew Mayor, Cohen, as soon as the subscription list
for the relief of the Russian Jews was opened.
It was good of His Grace,
And a strange thing and new,
To be kind to the race
Of the heretic Jew.
In the days that are past
The same Hebrew would play
The chief part in the cast
At an auto-da-fd.
Then His Grace would adjourn
A sweet service of prayer,
All to see Cohen burn
At a stake in the square.
Now these differing men
Are in spirit allied,
And they coincide—then
It was spelt Cohencide.
In the virtuous race
(God send cloaks for our sins !)
Faith and Hope have their place,
But ’tis Charity wins.
CREEVE ROB.

•* and Troubles.
Vic. Govt. Medical Officer, J. A. O’Brien
girds once again at his employers’ infamous
habit of sending the sick poor, who have been
turned away from the overflowing hospitals and
asylums, to gaol, there to recover or die He
gives specific instances of pensioners and others
who had never before seen the inside of a gaol’
being imprisoned for three or six months with
the sole object of getting them out of the wav
so that they might not die in the streets and
thus shock respectability. O’Brien appeals to
the Government’s humanity, which was probably
meant as a burst of humor. The great problem
is why the Iceberg Government, however much
it may enjoy its inhumanity, should persist in
throwing away good money over it; also why
the blessed Reform League doesn’t restrain
Shiels in his reckless piling up of deficits In
’Ol to ’O2 the average cost of each prisoner in
Victoria’s gaols was £4B 19s. 4d., while the
inmates of the Benevolent Asylums cost £l3
4s. 4d., so that for every poor old invalid sent
to linger out his last year in Victoria’s gaols
Shiels is deliberately throwing away £35 of that
harassed State’s already too scarce revenue. If
the Kyabram Retrenchment League intends to
justify its existence and hold up its tail feathers
honorably to the light of day, it has here a first-
class chance to do so.
* * * *
Another cry from Victoria :
Bent’s present railway system—or the country part of
it, at all events—is a weariness of the flesh and an all-
round abomination in general. Where I live some days
there is one train, some two, some none. A genius from
Kew Asylum couldn’t have devised such a chaotic service
on purpose in a month of Sundays. The trains that do
run, don’t, so to speak. Instead, they crawl over the
landscape with the pain and pathos of an endless serpent
dragging itself in a circle through Hell, and with frequent
stoppages when all hands—driver, stoker, guard, passen-
gers and all—have to climb down and urge the tired, lame
beast along with main strength and jacks and crowbars
Freshly-imported Commissioner Tait (just unpacked) has
a job ahead of him as big as Kosciusko—or bigger.
* * * .
At Charters Towers, the other day, Pre-
mier Philp told a W.C.T.U. deputation that,
if the local authorities didn’t take steps to
wipe out the Japanese-kept houses of ill-fame,
the women from Yokohama would probably con-
tinue soliciting the sinful “ fi’ bob ” unmolested ;
the matter of their suppression being entirely out
of the hands of the Government. Seeing that local
government is so much in the hands of the class
whose rapacity for rent is proverbial, the Japanese
dens seem to be fairly safe. Rents are always
paid promptly and on a generous scale by the
“gimme five bob” house.
* * * *
A witness who made a reluctant statement
in a Sydney court in a capital case involving the
lives of two men, having obtained beforehand an
assurance from the Judge of a general pardon for
most of the sins of his life, now receives hints that
the assurance does not cover everything. Assur-
ances in similar circumstances have often beenqual-
ified ; sometimes they have been simply ignored.
Sydney’s first gaoler-Governor leaves it on record
that he once said to a girl, “ Speak the truth and
on my honor as a gentleman I will acquit you.”
The girl spoke the truth, and Phillip ordered her
to be hanged. Phillip was a very poor kind of a
gentleman.

But only a very clumsy parson would drag in the

♦ * * *
The first week in J une saw the Maoriland
licensing committees at work. Most interest
centred in the Scotehby-
terian city of Dunedin,
where the hotels were to
be reduced, and the com-
mittee appointed to do it
consisted solely of rabid
fuglemen of the pump.
Dunedin is at present in
weepful condition, be-
cause the committee has
decided that while it
will abolish thirteen
hotels, it will not grant
licenses to the others un-
less they will (1) Abolish
the counter-lunch in its
present extravagance of
oysters, Welsh rarebit,
lettuce, oelery, radishes,
fishballs, sausage rolls,
and so on, and substitute
plain bread and cheese ;
(2) abolish barmaids;
(3) close at 10 p.m., in-
stead of 11 p.m. as
hitherto; and (4) keep
only one bar in each hotel.
The barmaid was objected
to on the ground that she
is so attractive that she is
a dangerous rival to the
gratis lunch in inducing men
to come along and drink.
There was no suggestion
that she was a menace to
the morals of the thirsty.
or that the thirsty were any
particular menace to her.
Dunedin is a dreary and
lingering place already, and
it is almost impossible to
imagine what it will be as soon
as the new regime comes into
force. Then we shall see the
long-nosed Dunedinites walk on
their cold red feet to the hotel,
and buy a beer in great depression, and mourn-
fully supplement it with a slice ol cold hard
cheese, and glare stonily at the hard-faced male
behind the counter, and finally go into the
outer darkness again, and probably bang another
saxpence under the same sepulchral circumstances.
Going out on the jag in Dunedin in future will
be like going to a series of funerals in wet
weather and swallowing the tombstones.
The Bulletin would like to hear how
much Rev. Dill Macky made out of his sectarian-
row promotion tour in N.S. W. When setting out
it was announced that he would raise some thou-
sands for a Protestant Defence Fund. But it
seems to have been a case of much excitement and
bruised eye and very little tangible results. Did
the fund benefit by as much as a tenner?

The Archbishop of Belgrade, who owed
his pomp, office and dignity to the royal house of
Obrenovitch, refused even to hold a requiem mass
for the souls of the murdered king and queen, who
were buried, like Jehoiakim of old, “with the
burial of an ass.” The Obrenovitches are down,
and the Karageorgevitches are up, and the church
hastened to grovel to the assassins and make its
job and its cathedral sure. It is all so like a
church.
* * * *
The most pleasing social incident in
Sydney last week was the falling in of Father
Abraham Abrahams. In that fell splash ,£3OO
damages and heavy costs vanished, and in one
corner of Jewry there was weeping and male-
diction. Father Abraham, according to the story
of the girl who obtained the damages, was a dear
old soul who, in the fulness of his paternal sym-
pathies, gave a little Gentile music teacher a free
room in his musical establishment for the purpose
of teaching budding Melbas and Crossleys how to
warble the “ Cannibal Maid ” and the “ Shriek of
Anastasia.” In lieu of rent there was some vague
sort of talk about commission on any pupils the
ancient brought along. But the offer of a rent-
free room from a man whose name consisted abso-
lutely of Abraham looked, at the least, unusual.
In due time, according to the girl, father Abraham
indicated that, even to a Hebrew who had cele-
brated his silver wedding, a little plump music-
teacher was not anathema, and when the girl cast
mud upon his beard the old man seems to have
got nasty. He went round saying “ things ”
about the girl. One Brown also seems to have
pressed his attentions on her at this time,
possibly because the language of Abraham led
him to regard her as a girl who liked “atten-
tions,” and Brown wandered in an irrelevant sort
of way through the case for some distance. Ulti-
mately Abraham became absolutely violent in
his language, and accused the music teacher of
breaking, entering stealing, and general im-
morality. Then the ancient was brought into
court, and the court manifested a very low
opinion of Abraham, and gave the damages
above mentioned. It is a long time since the
court has appeared to have a lower opinion of
anybody than it had of Abraham.
» * * *
One of the first notable cases in which
Premier Irvine appeared after he was called to
the Victorian Bar was that of Nyulasky v. Rowan,
in which a man had to pay £BOO for a joke.
Nyulasky had 400 shares in the Melbourne Tram-
way Co., and, one day, was deploring to Father
O’Reilly, in the Melbourne-Exchange, the state
of the shares in the market. Andrew Rowan,
wine merchant, who held a great quantity of
Tramway shares, happened upon the scene, and
told Nyulasky not to sell the shares then, and in
three months he would give him £8 each for
At the end of the three months Nyulasky called
on Rowan and asked him for £3200 for the shares,
but Andy wasn’t “on.” Nyulasky then went
and sold the shares at £6, and sued Rowan for the
difference —£800. The defence was that Rowan
was only joking ; but Father O’Reilly’s evidence
convinced the court that the old wine-dealer
couldn’t joke if he tried, and Nyulasky got the
verdict.
* • * *
It is a matter for regret that the Australian
Government has wasted valuable time, ink and
paper, corresponding with Britain on the matter
of “ Ecclesiastical Precedence.” There’s no State
church in this continent, and there ought to be no
question of precedence, and the puffed-up fol-
lowers of the Teacher who washed the feet of His
disciples ought to be taught a lesson in Christian
humility. Clergymen, whether they wear red
hats or pot hats with tags on them, should be
treated at official functions as private persons. If
there is to be any precedence the oldest shepherd
should be allowed the privilege of showing the
other shearer the tail of his coat.
A violently unusual sort of a ruffian tran-
spired at Inverell (N.S.W.) (Quarter Sessions,
recently. Accused, one David Brown, was
charged with horrible cruelty to a boy. In evi-
dence, it came out that Brown had somehow got
it into his head that the small boy had been rob-
bing him of certain money, so he unmercifully
thrashed him to aid a confession as to the where-
abouts of the missing cash—if there really was any
missing'. Finding, this ineffectual, the determined
cash-misser then tied the boy to a sapling in the
scrub for three nights running (in winter, in New
England 1): but, finding this also unavailing,
probably because the miserable youth knew
nothing about any money, Brown then tied him
to a pole and immersed him in a water-hole to
his neck for three hours ! When this also
failed, Brown seems to have become really
severe, as he then proceeded to hang the
boy up by the neck with a saddle-strap, and
threatened to cut his throat if the money didn’t
appear, forth with. Luckily, somebody happened
along just then, and the case matured further in
the arrest of the dreadful Brown. Seemingly,
there are some people in this world who would
cut their grandmother’s throat in an endeavor to
discover three-halfpence.
• » * *
“ Let the women of Australia remember
this,” says the.D.T.’s most oracular correspondent
on the declining-birth-rate subject: “ God will
never send a man and woman more children than
they can provide food and clothes for.” If that’s
the case, then somebody else seems to be sending
along children, and the D.T.’s correspondent
ought to get it stopped. But what nonsense it is.
As well say. that God never sends a cat more
kittens than it can find house-room for and save
from the water-hole.
* • * *
One rather mean member of the Maori-
land Parliamentary picnio to Polynesia is said to
have offered a, shapely island lady a box of
matches if she’d show him certain tatoo marks
concealed about her person. She cheerfully un-
dressed and gave an exhibition, but the legislator
didn’t consider the tatoo illustrations good art,
and demanded his matches back. Considering
that the lady had all the trouble of struggling out
of and into her clothes, his conduct was considered
degradingly economical.
* * * *
Stated in N.S.W. Supreme Court last
week that when a Jew marries outside his own
persuasion he generally marries a Roman Catholic.
* * * *
During the hearing of a maintenance case
at the North Melbourne Court recently the man
stated. he was earning 10s. a week as a driver, and
was willing to contribute ss. out of it towards the
infant’s support. An order for ss. weekly was
made, Dr. Lloyd, J.P., remarking that the sooner
the parties got married the better. The man
said he was “thinking of it.’’ Ten shillings a
week and a wife and child, or five shillings a
week and single blessedness, make up a problem
that takes a lot of thinking over.
• * * *
A returned swaddy, who served with one
of the recent contingents in S’Africa, had the
misfortune to “go under” at Gisborne (M.L.),
whither he had travelled with a circus. As lie
died moneyless, the local Bumble Board was com-
pelled to bury him. Two persons attended the
funeral —the driver of the hearse (who couldn’t
very well be absent) and a parson (to read the
service). At the graveside things were a little
more cheerful, the crowd being augmented by the
presence, of the sexton. The services of a stranger,
who was spending an hour or two in the cemetery
to rid himself of an attack of melancholia, had to
be requisitioned in order that the coffin might be
lowered. And all this change in two brief years !
* * * *
“ Peto ” : Par. in B. (13/6/’O3) contained
a shudder at the ghastly horrors of blouse-
making at by London “ out-workers,” a
condition of affairs which the M'Millan and
Smiff cult would joyfully welcome in Australia.
Said price has been the average payment to
out-Workers in Adelaide for some years past.
Trousers, blouses, boys’ clothing, and white-work
generally are made in Adelaide as cheaply as in
the meanest German-Jew sweater’s den of
Whitechapel. Ample proof of this statement
can be had from the report of the S.A. Factories
Inspectors. That one of the largest soft-goods
houses in Australia should have sent a parcel of
material from Sydney to Adelaide to be made up
and returned, and, after paying freight both
ways, could still sell the garments at a profit is
another proof. Some reasons for such a deplor-
able state of affairs are: (1) Bad laws ; (2) worse
administration ; (3) influx of Syrians ; (4) the soft-
goods merchant of Adelaide is invariably a pillar
of a church, of several alleged charitable institu-
tions, and of the Anti-sweating League (now
sleeping); also, he can influence many votes.
The press—well, the man who sells clothing is
far and away the biggest advertiser in the State.
*' * * *
The Federal spirit. A South Australian
t was charged lately with assaulting a four-year-old
"girl. After two months’ observation it was
reported that accused was “a bit queer,” but not
“queer” enough to be supported in an asylum.
So the prosecution was withdrawn on the under-
standing that he would leave for another State—
a State, presumably, where there aren’t any little
girls, and where it doesn’t matter if people are
“ a bit queer.”
• * * *
Rookwood Cemetery and miles of country
around it constituted the “Liberty Plains” of
N.S.W. early records. It was to that then pro-
mising part of the country the free immigrants
were first sent. The land did not turn out well,
and most of them shifted to the Hawkesbury.
Now it is chiefly an old man’s home and a gather-
ing of tombstones.

LINES BY A GROCER—TO MARY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thy subtle charm, like candied peel,
Sultana currant of my heart
How can I tell thee what I feel
When thou art near my lingering cart ?
Molasses cannot all express
The slow, sweet movement of Love’s dream
That clings about thee, who dost bless
More than refrigerated cream.
The best of coffee, chocolate,
In fond libation should be poured
To one so nobly spiced by Fate—
One so confectioned, raisin-cored !
HUBERT CHURCH.
