[VARNISHED BOARD
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ Sydney, June 23, 1903.
My Dear Arini,—
A Tasmanian resident who, as a boy, was a
pupil of Lady Hector Macdonald’s father, writes,
protesting against the suggestion that she would
have been out of place amongst the other officers’
wives. Lady M. was, he says, the only child of
A. M. Duncan, who, with his brother, kept a
school in Perth, Scotland, under the style of
A. and R. Duncan’s Commercial Academy.”
They were both scientists, and (with a curious
mixture of the keen man of business and the
dreamy swisher of the rising generation) they
were the owners of many vessels trading between
k“ e Tay and the Baltic. Lady Macdonald was
perhaps better born, and, almost to a certainty,
better educated than many of the women who
tiptilted their noses at her.
Dr. Norton Manning, who died last Thursday
f a struggle with an acute renal disease,
had been for many years a valued public servant,
do him the comparatively high state of N.S.W.
Lunacy Department is due, he having remodelled
the lunacy system in 1867. As a specialist in
mental alienation Dr. Manning had numberless
opportunities of showing his sympathetic and
kindly nature, which, allied to a distinguished
degree of ability and a markedly handsome
appearance, made hiiii one of the best-liked men in
the Sydney medical profession.
How assiduously the names of “mourners” at
funerals of well-known men are chronicled in the
morning dailies ! And the day after the list
has appeared there is almost sure to be a supple-
mentary one—which proves that “the gentlemen
whose names we omitted ” have gone and button-
holed the recording demon, or editor. Wonder,
when cremation is general, will there be a long
black procession to the crematorium, and a state-
ment next day that “among the names of the
gentlemen who gathered round the furnace that
of Samson Smith, Esq., J.P., was inadvertently
omitted”! No doubt in a while we shall be
able to incinerate our dead in a few minutes with
electricity. In fact, then we may, if we live close
to a tramline, just switch on a current to our
house, and there won’t be any need for long, black
processions.
London newspapers report the engagement of
Baden Powell (the gentleman who is always
photographed wearing his hat or flourishing his
sword) to Miss Daisy Leiter, sister of Lady
Curzon. One would have thought that one so
fond of limelight and ring-master effects as B.P.
would have selected a circus girl. Concerning the
voice that Breathed, &c., Lady Bertha Wilbra-
ham, who came out here on a visit to the
Beauchamp brother and sister, was married lately
in London to one Major Dawkins, whom she met
at the Durbar. That great elephant parade led
to the striking of so many matches that there’s
been a chronic wedding breakfast ever since.
Sydney Society is smiling on the quiet at a cer-
tain youthful-looking woman who has a daughter
two years over the coming-out age, but whom she
still keeps short-dressed and long-haired, and will
not allow to any but schoolgirl dances. The girl
announced her intention of “coming out” on her
own, short dresses notwithstanding, at the Syd-
ney Uni. Sports Union Ball, on the second of
next month ; and the mother, who tries to appear
28, and mostly succeeds, has had to compromise
with her. She is to come out this winter, but in
a neighboring State, and to be chaperoned by an
aunt. The story leads up to one about Mrs.
Langtry. She was resting in. Boston a month or
so ago, when a telegram came through to one of
the papers announcing the birth of a son to the
lan Malcolms (her daughter and son-in-law).
Mrs. L.’s manager said to the reporter who
sought an interview, “ I wouldn’t take her such a
message for anything. Let someone else break
the news to her that she can no longer be con-
sidered an ingenue .”
‘‘Warm with delirious love and fight.” Now,
isn t that a fine Brummagem jewel to put on the
finger of time—the time of last week, when
Mayor Cohen, of Adelaide, gave a great ball. A
correspondent assures me that the sensuous, not
to say sensual, phrase was used by a respectable
elderly daily in describing the brilliant scene,
lour thousand persons and five thousand five
hundred electric lights, and white, frost-like
decorations, and Governor-General, Lady, and
suite. Mayor and Mayoress left the decorations
standing, and next evening they, the Cohens,
were “at home” to, it is estimated, 20,000 per-
sons who could not be asked to the. ball. About
3000 mortally-offended persons stopped away from
the reception because they were not asked, to the
ball; otherwise the Mayor and ’ess are the most
popular couple in S.A. up to date. Tennvsons
went in largely for arboricultural pursuits during
their 30 hours’ stay in Adelaide. They planted
trees at the Maternity Home, Botanical Gardens,
and Oval; and the planting of a shade tree is,’
according to an Eastern belief, one of the three
things that will help to save your soul alive.
That loan exhibition in aid of the heathen-in-
hiS-blindness which State Governor Rawson
opened last Tuesday at Redfern is well worth the
door moiiey (there must be door money, how else
could the heathen be aided ?) if only to see Sir
liarrv s collection of West African horror-curios.
These were garnered after the Admiral and his
handy men had got into the Negro city of Benin,
only to find it deserted but drenched with the
blood of human sacrifices and Ju-Ju horrors.
Amongst the collection are some weirdly and
delicately wrought bronzes, which must be close
upon 400 years old. Supposed that early Portu-
guese travellers taught the art, which the savages
promptly forgot. One plaque Shows' two men fn
toe agonies. of strangulation and crucifixion ; and
•on the cross beam above their gibbet, perch three
solemn, horrible black birds. Every strand iri
the ropes, the fingernails of the victims, and the
parting of the birds’ beaks are made clear.
Another bronze shows the king giving an audi-
ence. Soldiers stand on either side of him holding
up his arms, which are loaded with ornaments.
There is an immense ivory tusk, closely carved—
an historical and human document; but most of
the curios and carvings, all taken from off the
Ju-Ju altars, have to do with torture and death
carved bronze circlets which supported the
severed heads on the altars, and bells which
the priests rang when summoning the inhabitants
for sacrificial selection. Hadn’t the inhabitants
of Benin reason to say—“ Alas, those chimes ! ”
Miss Mabel Ward, a music teacher to whom a
sympathetic jury awarded £3OO damages against
one Abrahams for slander, is described as so pre*
possessing as to quite win the collective heart of
the junior bar. Her spelling wasn’t as good as
that of a dictionary and it seemed a decimal of a
point against her that when asked by the
judge how she would spell “able,” she gave it
“abel.” But if one is a musician one
may spell like Artemus Ward, or the Uni-
versity professor who spells comedy with two
m’s, and occasion with one c and two s’s. You
don’t spell a top-note.
Obituary notices of Cardinal Vaughaq (by the
way, he was expelled from Stonyhurst for the
mild crime of cigarette smoking) remind us that
his brother Jteginald, who came to Sydney on a
visit some 25 or more years ago, married whilst
here Miss Shanahan, a sister-in-law of the
late Patrick Jennings. Squatter Shanahan, her
father, who dowered her well, acquired his lands
and flocks as did most of our early sheep kings.
“If you eats yourself,” Squatter Shanahan used
to say to the shepherds seeking work, “you’ll
get so much a week; if Mrs. Shanahan eats you,
it will be half.”
The largest.piece of embroidery-work ever done
in this State is the curtain for the new theatre—
Her Majesty’s, which is to be opened next month.
Curtain, which is the work of Mrs. Edwards, of
S.A.N.. Soc., is of pale blue silk plush. In the
centre is a shield measuring 4ft. 6in. by 4ft., in
the middle of which is the monogram ' “ H.M.T.”
worked in two shades of gold silk. Gold cord out-
lines the device.
Sultan of Johore, while in Sydney, bought an
automobile and shipped it Malayward. I reckon
he had to pay cash before delivery, though.
Dealing with an independent potentate who can’t
be sued for his washing bill is a risky thing these
hard times.
One “ Cheetah ” writes to me from Bendigo:
In “ A Woman’s Letter ” (B. 30/5/’O3) “ Akenehi” writes :
I have seen but two short-skirted women in Sydney up
to date.” If “ Akenehi ” pants for a glance at short skirts
on a large, ever-enduring scale, let her strike Bendigo.
Here, the billy-goats wear their whiskers long, and the
eternal chit of a girl wears her skirt short and her hair
a-hanging down her back. Fashions may come and trains
may go, but the years and looks of Bendigo spinsterhood
aspire to remain young; and their lingerie peeps from
under the skirt hem for ever! For 25 years now, have
been visiting the goldfields’ city, and since my first
glimpse at the Bendigo girl’s many attractions as she
stands on the bridge that skirts the two platforms, she
has never changed either in her hair or her frock-length.
But age is coming upon me ; likewise, methinks, the
Bendigo girl is not so full about the calf or so trim around
the ankle as of yore. Her flowing locks are stringy, and
the aroma of hair-dye hangs around her presence ; and
she appears to be in too great a hurry to get to the rail-
way station (which would look lonely without her
presence—there for quarter of a century every night,
excepting Sundays) to spare the time to massage out
the ‘cracks of age ” that will weave their tell-tale net
oer her ever-smiling visage. Still, she bears up well.
Three cheers and a tiger for the Bendigo girl. Age is the
only stranger she fails to greet at the local barrier when
the train comes in.
Basso Peterkin remains in Australia to takepartin
Ada Cross]ey’s tour in September. Peterkin’s wife,
Marjorie Nairne, made her first appearance before
a Sydney audience at the Town Hall last Satur-
day.. Her soprano voice is curiously like herself,
fragile and thin, with a pathetic quality. She is
a young woman—looks 30 years younger than Ho !
Jolly Jenkin Peterkin, and evidently had little or
no musical training. All the same she has a lily-
maid-of-Astolat appearance that will make any-
one but an audience of bears kind to her. She
should have been an Elaine instead of a Marjorie.
Contralto Emily Foxcroft, the star of William-
son’s English Concert Co., has frequently mingled
her deep chiming song with that of Aucklander
Tom Jackson, who has been in the Great Smoke
this many a year. The blooming (adjective not
used in its slang sense) contralto is one of the few
English visitors who does not look at us and all
about us through smoked glasses. Our weather is
her especial favorite. “Look at that sky,” she
exclaimed to a tired Australienne, who, so chal-
lenged, blinked up into the opal afternoon and
asked, “ Yes what’s the matter with it?”
“ Why, if we in London had one day in a year
like this roofed with a sky like that—We would
talk of it as, for instance, ‘ that wonderful Sunday
we had seven years ago, in May.’ ”
Foxcroftian taste on Saturday’s platform made
for a pink rose under the left ear and a long pink
silk scarf, with fringed ends, hanging from the
decolletege of the gown to the foot. In the
matter of smoky-gold hair and opulent figure, the
contralto is like the pianola-poster gin, whose
appearance on our street-advertising spaces daily
causes partial paralysis of traffic, both horse and
foot.
Liza Lehman’s “In a Persian Garden” (Omar
Khayyam’s verses) will be produced next month
at Sydney Town Hall by the Pianola Co.. Artists
engaged for the performance are Miss Violet
Mount, Miss Dalrymple,. and Messrs, Weir and
Robyns. The “ Garden ” includes a delightful
d uet for soprano and tenor, “ A Book of
Verses Underneath the Bough”; and the tenor
solo, “ Alas that Spring should Perish with the
Rose,” is a real peach., A quartette, “Wake, for
Morning in the Bowl of Night,” starts the
Persian night’s festival.
My Melbourne correspondent continues :
“ The Governor Tennysons are beginning to
wear a travel-tired, srnoke-tinted look. On
Friday, when driving along to embark for
Harborville, after only a few hours’ interval from
arrival by the alleged express from Adelaide,
their Exes, were less ready to beam upon the man
in the street, and showed less than usual of that
specldess, fresh, immaculate state that breathes
of righteousness and the frequent tub. Their
journeyings from state to state of late have been
very frequent. A recent day in the Holy City
was given over to an astonishing flight of func-
tions. There were receptions, tree-plantings,
Mayoral sprees, visits of inspection, in one brief,
dizzy day. Then their Exes, were ‘expressed’
over here, whiffed through more ceremonies, and
at last carried off, weary-eyed, to Sydney. Lord
Dick Nevill’s eyes grow saucer-shaped, and his
lean visage as sorrowful as an owl’s. But he’s an
uncomplaining sufferer.
“In a few weeks, the bleak-looking glass-house
on the St. Kilda-road will break into life and
illumination, the Tennysons having decided to
take a long-breath pull in this Federal rendezvous.
Later on, they intend to look in at Tasmania, but
not before a good quiet restorative spell.
“Two of the serest early Victorian parliament-
arians are at present tenderly regarded by their
friends. The one, Graham Berry, takes his airing
abroad when the sky is at its brightest in a wheeled
chair. The other day he assisted at the knot-
tying of a daughter—Miss Edyth, who was
marrying into the wealthy wool-broker circle—in
this invalid carriage. The other ancient is Dunky
Gillies, who has taken to sunning his feeble self
in all his grey'and tall-hatted lustre on the Block.
There’s something pathetic about this effort to
figure in the giddy promenade of former days.
Dunky’s eyes look at you clearly, shrewdly, but
his tongue rambles apace, and reminds you that
the keen-headed politician of early Victoria has
lost his grip on the world’s affairs.
“The music mania is going to be a curse of
eroticism. Wild enthusiasts are at the city’s out-
posts waving their wands and flapping their
coat-tails before bewitched bands of followers.
Sensationalism runs along the lines like a bush
fire. Ardent young conductors are rising up to
keep aflame the fire kindled by Marshall Hall.
A recent sheet of Beethoven’s music with word
illuminations ends :
Here, with a swirling rush, joyful and exuberant youth
asserts itself.
O ye millions, I embrace ye !
Here’s a joyful kiss for all!
This from one of the young conductors ! ‘ Joyrul
and exuberant ’ youth will get a black eye from
an indignant brother one of these days.
“ Two wealthy young dames, who married from
the chorus ranks into the squattoeracy and have
since walked in scarlet and delights, like the
dazzlers of Saul’s time, are making tremendous
preparations for a joint ramble in Europe.
“Malcolm M'Eacharn seems to be ever out on
the house-tops, telescope in hand, watching for a
visiting church, political, military, or social star
to show above the horizon. When he has made
sure of its class, he invites it to come and revolve
at Goatlands. When it isn’t a Federal lion, it’s a
colored somebody, a foreign mogul, or a church
nobleman. There’s always someone of compara-
tive distinction occupying the elaborate guest
chamber of the Goatlands domicile. Does Mal-
colm ever read ‘Pickwick Papers,’ and refresh his
recollection of Mrs. Leo Hunter and Count
Smorltork ? :f
“The Rev. George Robinson—the opo-time..
guardian of Amy Castles —has departed from his'
prosperous parish out Camberwell way a physical
wreck. Once aßandsome figure, he left the other
day, a prey to paralysis that has deprived him of
all peace and comfort. For years he has been
afflicted but struggled along, making good-;
humored jest of the matter. Latterly the face'
twitchings became a trial to behold, and, a severer
attack than usual occurring, the smart cleric had
to give up his parochial charge. He has taken a
trip to Ireland. Probably no priest was as useful to
Archbishop Carr, none a greater platform success,
certainly none more popular among the faithful.
“Miss Van Buren boasts of a martial ‘ poppa,’
a whole. host of belligerent relatives in Dutch
America, and a ‘ mommer ’ who has smelt powder
and proved as. brave and courageous as the his-
torical mother of the Gracchi. There is a childish
play of hands and a perfect rounding of the eyes
when the Van Buren talk turns to things native
and Amurrican. ‘Haven’t you been there? Is
that so? Then you can’t know half the joy that
I know.’ You wonder for a moment if you are a
genuine object of/pity. She has an air of simple
faith which assists the very young, unsophisticated
drawl of voice, and the innocent ping-pong eyes
slip ten years or so off the Van Buren birth-
day records. 1
“Mrs. York Sjfme—an easily recognised sister
Dry-dog of the cosy, free-and-easy
conditions of the Empress Club, London* A
smoking room is provided for the club members,
and, as all the families of any consequence in the
great hub s.lay time there, the picture is presented
to the mind of London’s loftiest women whiffing

reform of any hind ’ is the reason of the club’s

“ A huge amount of mystery attaches to the
masked ball which is to happen sometime next
month. We hear of novel sets where the identity
of neighbors is to remain unknown, and only the
leader can place those in her traces. There is to
be a general drop of mask and domino somewhere
before midnight. Thereat all sorts of things
may be expected to happen, and the wailing of
the disillusionised is likely to rend the walls of
the Town Hall in twain.
‘•One trophy of Senator Staniforth Smith’s
jaunt to New Guinea is a portrait of the eight
dusky Papuan belles whose bare shoulders bore
the weight of his personal togs through certain
parts of the malaria province. Brother Senators
gaze with awe and envy on the group of choco-
late Graces. The girls look very alluring in their
way.
“The Shaksperian mummers of the Princess’s
are firm believers in fresh air, and they take it in
liberal portions three times or more a day. Some
of the stars actually rise what time Phoebus is
supposed to be ‘dappling the drowsy East,’and
revolve about the Gardens among the cold
statues, to return with large appetites to the
‘ Grand. ’ Anon they do the St. Kilda boulevards,
and later the Brighton beach, furred to the ears.
“ Miss Gertrude Alger, debuting at the Town
Hall on Saturday night, cradled the violin under
her intelligent chin with a certain amount of
fondness and loving protection. She gives a per-
formance that warms an audience, and the sparse
one assembled that night, though wearing its top-
coat and its muffler, wanted something of the
kind. The advertised age of Miss Alger is put
down as tender seventeen, and the performance
was a more than good one for that age.
“ The limit of the concert-goer’s patience has
about come. It’s the frost-nipping time of mid-
winter, and the City Council ought to be waited
on with a gun for the means to warm up
the Town Hall. The old joke about enthusiasm
catching a chill is a bit of cruel irony to the rheu-
matic subject and the weak-chested. On Saturday
matters reached a climax. The women sat
huddled up to their chins in cloaks and furs, while
elderly males whistled in their beards to keep
their lungs, at least, exercised. Over the arid
spaces of the hall the cold air swept, causing ears
and noses to take on a blue bloom, and gay
aigrettes in stylish thatches to swing to and fro
in pendulum fashion. Several young people war-
bled execrably, and a hard freeze was only
averted by the electrifying numbers of the girl
violinist.
“A cruel joke was played upon a smart bride
the other day. The dear gushers entrusted with
the packing-up of the going-awav clothes set to
work with active needles to make'all the lingerie
a wild tangle. Having sewn and cross-sewn
every single article of underwear, they set the
lot into elegant layers, scattering violet
sachets between, and then strapped up the boxes.
The joke was circulated, and the wretches have
been giggling ever since over a freezing form (or
a pair of them) laboriously untying the Gordian
knots and plying impatient scissors at the mazed
lines of thread. It isn’t the first time gigglesome
girls have tricked the jubilant bride. The label,
‘ Passengers for Paradise,’ is becoming a popular
gag among the luggage porters. Football hose
have been inserted instead of dainty embroidered
stockings, and other strictly feminine ‘ sells ’
been introduced into the sacred toilette trunks.
“ What becomes of all the one-appearance
concert girls ?. At recent concerts given by
visiting stars in Melbourne there was quite a
budding forth of young girls with small voices,
all of whom seem destined to go back immediately
to an uncongenial obscurity, as with so many of
their predecessors. Their one big concert will, no
doubt, form the subject of frequent reminiscence
in Jater years, and the half-line of dubious com-
mendation in the papers will be indelibly engraven
on their memories. There must be 50 or 100
Judies of this kind in Melbourne alone, but the
single-appearance man vocalist is a rarity. The
man with a small unworthy voice doesn’t get a
chance to figure on a big programme, mainly
because he can’t look nice and fetching in a short
white frock with flowers and strings of pearls,
and therefore doesn’t afford picturesque relief to
the dark-browed pianist or ’cellist who is giving
three-fourths of the show.
“Mrs. John Sandes, whose husband goes to
Sydney Telegraph shortly, cuts the stage for
good. Her infatuation has snuffed out, She is
going to establish the pots and pans of domestic
life in Sydney.
“ If you want to see the real soul of Eve, catch
her—all nerves strained, all the lust of greed in
her eye, all her selfishness concentrated —at a
bargain sale. There is the rare good-natured
huntress, that laughs as she loses. But the com-
moner sight is of her who snatches and snarls,
who fights for her remnant, tooth and nail, who
claws the contenders; and grins fiendishly every
time she nails a prize.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

politician who lately became an inconsolable
widower, and publicly bedewed the earth with his

again.
Tunbridge of Townsville, ex-Lieutenant-
Colonel of Queensland Defence Force and South
Africa, is going to try his luck at next Federal
elections either as Senator or Rep. He hasn’t
decided yet, but it is likely the electors have.
Mrs. R. Cohen, of Mudgee (N.S. YV.) i U st
dead at 87 years of age, left 14 children, 46 grand-
children, and 29 great grand-children. Children
and grand-children are almost the only crop that
conies up well of late years in rural Australia.
The late Dr. Norton Manning carried his
studies of the insane into private practice from
time to time. “As long,” he told a leading poli-
tician, “as you can laugh next day at what you
solemnly promised the day before, you are all
right.
Of the handful of men convicted at the
most recent Adelaide criminal sessions one had
been previously convicted 12 times, another 13
another 18, and another 23. That means that
the State has had 66 tries at reforming four
people, and has failed.

Died, this week, at Geelong (Vic.), Arch-
deacon Slattery, author of the long-famous
admonition to his flock to “draw a free and
flashing sword” in the religion-in-schools fight.
He was long reckoned the liveliest man in dead
Geelong.
James Scandrett, who died at Kapunda
(S.A.) the other day, established and owned till
his death (a period of about 40 years) the local
Herald, S.A.’s top country paper. He was an
apprentice in the Government Printing Office in
its very early days.
That stately greybeard, William Jukes
Steward, of Maoriland, journalist, poet, politician,
knight, etc., got off nearly 1000 lines of execrable
verse as a result of his Island trip with the
Maoriland political drunk and picnic. Steward
can’t try on a pair of new boots without making
a pome about it.
Dr. Lloyd has been re-elected chairman
of the North Melbourne Justices for the 38th
consecutive year. Lloyd is the eccentric gentle-
man who almost always awards the girl a liberal
baby-support allowance, however frantically the
male defendant may protest that he never saw or
heard of her before.
When J. G. Duffy, Victorian M.L.A., was
speaking at Seymour 17 years ago he promised
butcher Thomas Bolton a bonus out of his own
pocket if ever he became the father of 20 children,
provided the Government refused to pay it.
Bolton's 20th child arrived recently, and Duffy
sent him a silver-plated jug.
As a vocalist N.S.W. Minister Owe’Sulli-
van has a rival in Westralian Treasurer Gardiner,
who has been singing at way-out political meet-
ings. The last N.S.W. Minister who committed
a crime of this sort was poor old Francis Abigail;
and worse things happened to him shortly after-
wards than to any other Minister or ex-Minister
that The Bulletin knows.
Canon Boyce has celebrated, in cold water,
the fiftieth anniversary of his arrival in Sydney.
The Canon’s wrestlings with the Australian liquor
bill, in which he always includes the duty paid on
the liquor as part of the cost of the drink itself,
are a national institution. He was a bank-clerk
originally, and takes to columns of figures as
other men to pints of beer.
The oldest Australian native is said to be
Mrs. T. Phillips, who resides in Brougham-street,
Woolloomooloo. Her age is given as 96. The
ancient lady, who is still active, says that she was
born in Kent-street, in the house next door to
ITArcy Wentworth’s. Mrs. Phillips is still strong
enough to go to funerals, the functions which kill
off so many younger people, and attended Dean
Cowper s the other day.

Wanted: A Bill for the Suppression of
•tlenniker Heaton. This pleasant-faced, round-
stomached old gentleman appears in Australia for
a fortnight every two or three years, and then
goes back to Britain and claims to speak for
Australia. Strange to say, whatever Henniker
trunks is also the undoubted public opinion of
Australia according to Henniker. And it is
time Australia objected.

1. S. Hall, the Mt. Morganaire, who died
in London lately, had the luck to get deep into
Queensland’s great gold mine through being sent
up to Rockhampton by the A. J.S. Bank when he
was a very junior clerk. Afterwards, when he
had joined the Q.N., he went back there as man-
ager in 1876, favorably remembered by the old
hands—it’s easy enough for a junior to be “ favor-
ably remembered ” ; it isn’t so easy for a manager
to be. Anyhow, this favorable remembrance
brought him clients and would be clients, among
them the discoverers of the rich show. He took
it up, and was the first chairman of the co.

income. ”
HIM: “Hum! I thought you'd done something

F. W. Amsinck, pensioned Victorian
Post-office official, whose death is recorded, was
related to half-a-dozen British admirals and other
weighty persons. The Amsincks, originally from
Groningen (Holland), branched out into German
“noble” families, and produced a long line of
lawyers, bankers, ship-owners, burgomasters, and
other personages of wealth and stomach. The
Amsincks of Hamburg are in the shipping busi-
ness. Another of the race is the veteran Sydney
journalist, connected with Reuter’s telegraphic
agency.

13r. Norton Manning, N.S. W. ex-Inspector
General of the Insane, survived his colleague and
friend, Critchett Walker, but five days. Both
were bachelors, living in chambers a few doors
apart, and died in the same week, unattended by
any near relatives. Critchett Walker had one or
two unobtrusive connections, but Dr. Manning
was in no way related to either of the Manning
families prominent in Svdney, and left no one
connected with him in Australia.
Fustian of a man without imagination.
John See, the other night, flaring up at the inter-
jections of his opponents in Parliament: “ I know
that some of you would treat me as the conspira-
tors treated King Alexander and Queen Draga;
but I defy you.” Another N.S.W. Premier,
similarly excited, cried : “Ha ! I am not the first
man to be hounded down. ‘ Away with him, away
with him ; crucify him, crucify him ’ —that is the
wa .y you talk ! ” This habit of talking in large
capitals and italics always suggests a barren mind.
Governor-General Tennyson indicates he
would like to send his children to school in Aus-
tralia, but etiquette obliges him to school them in
England. Etiquette has been just arranged then.
Gov. Hamilton, of Tasmania (when it was a
‘sovereign State”), sent his boy to the local
Christs College. King Leopold of Belgium, by
the way, sent all his children, princes and prin-
cesses, to the local public schools. It was demo-
cratic, but it was also bad policy. A prince is
j jst like anybody else, but the monarchical tradi-
tion depends on keeping the fact dark.
1 he death of Cardinal Vaughan reminds
that a big book about Roger Vaughan, his
brother, is in manuscript somewhere about
Sydney or Melbourne. The late Father Barry,
one time prefect of St. John’s College, Sydney,
compiled a biography of the Archbishop of
Sydney. The Vaugh ans are a remarkable family,
in State and church. The youngster of the group
—Kenelm—lately performed a mulebaek ride of
thousands of miles with the supposed bones of a
murdered Jesuit he wants canonised. And the
Jesuit was murdered a couple of hundred years
ago.
At a Melbourne meeting the other day
(called to sympathise with the woes of the Jews
m Russia), Rev. Bleeds Fitchett referred to Rev.
Peace Rentjul’s absence, “which appeared con-
spicuous” to him. Rev. Rentoul promptly
retorted in a letter to the press, which concluded :
lam aware how Russians answer back. They say :
“ You English have killed 20,000 children and women in
the fever camps of South Africa. You drove away from
their Christian homes over 100,000 Protestant women and
men, and left two Christian republics desolate. The
Rand is now in the hands of Jewish financiers. You are
suppressing a people’s language there, as we also are in
Finland. Don’t be hypocrites.”
And I only wish that Dr. Fitchett and others would
become true Liberals, and protest against other tyranniis
done to peoples and nationalities, whether Jew or
Christian, whether Catholic or Protestant.
And the Rev. Bleeds bled in silence.
Two old men :
Adye Douglas, Tasmanian politician, and Daniel
Murphy, Tasmanian R.C. Archbishop and champion
chess-player, are both 88 years old. Douglas celebrated
his birthday on May 31, and last week the Archbishop
celebrated his on June 18, the anniversary of Waterloo.
Dr, Murphy was born while the battle was being fought!
Twenty years ago he went to Rome and saw Leo XIII.
When they were parting, Leo said to Daniel: “ Good-
bye, this is the last time we’ll meet on earth.” Ten
years later Leo was still keeping house at the Vatican
and Daniel dropped in once again to see him, and told
him that it didn’t speak well for his infallibilitv that they
had met again in the flesh. Last year a deputation
headed by Minister Teddy Mulcahy, waited on the Arch-
bishop to congratulate him on his birthday, when the
gay old gentleman told the above yarn. “ And,” said he
“ now after another ten years if I could go to Rome I’d
remind his Holiness again of this lack of infallibility
which I pointed out before.”
Latest addition to Victoria’s Assembly is a
specimen of the full-fledged Kyabramapootra.
J. J. Carlisle, who was returned for Benalla and
Yarrawonga to replace Hall, M.L.A,, deceased,
is a wheat-grower and wine-maker. In the lat:er
capacity he has received large bonuses partly paid
by the city workmen, and is thus a bumptious
pauper who lived in a measure on the charity of
the Trades Hall people. Needless to say he isn’t
grateful.
“ X. 0.” has a reminiscence :
Alex. Fyfe, whose death was recorded in Bulletin
16/5/’O3, was at one time a member of Queensland
Parliament, returned for Rockhampton and district
about 1864, I think. He was probably one of the two
earliest paid members in Australia. The industrial and
commercial sections of the district subscribed £4OO a
year to pay him as an anti-squatting member. Another
member advocating similar policy and paid bv subscrip-
tion was returned about the same time. Fyfe’s failure
was caused by the kind of drought that settled many
othe.s. An incident of his election was the issue of
posters by his supporters—“ Vote for Fyfe. You Will
Always Find Him.” Beneath which opponents pasted a
tag with the word “ Drunk.”
If Stafford Bird had not lost his seat for
Franklin at the last Tasmanian election, he would
have been Speaker of the new House. “Nick”
Brown is ponderous and obscure, and there would
not be a row if a change were made. Brown de-
feated Bird for the Speakership in 1897 —after
Bird had occupied the chair a Parliament—by a
narrow majority ; and a like thing happened the
Parliament before that, when Bird just beat
Lrown, who had also held the Speakership for a
Parliament. In fact, it is a case of Brown and
Bird turn about, only the turns aren’t shared
evenly, and there is too much Brown.
Vic. Railways Minister Thomas Bent
masqueraded as adjudicator in a recent municipal
dispute. His judgment is described as a master-
piece of crass ignorance. In reading his judg-
ment he stuck fast more than once, fell over
the English language badly, and misread
things in an alarming way. After the judgment
had been delivered there was a great silence, for
neither side had the least idea what it meant, and
no one knew which had gained the verdict until
explanations were forthcoming. Bent, by the
way, is reputed to write “stopped” with one “p,”
and to take other liberties with the British lan-
guage.
News comes of the marriage in London of
Cyril Forster, the soldier son of N.S.W. Premier
and fighting Parliamentarian, William Forster.
There was fine work in old Forster. He was a
biter and hitter all his life. When called to form
a Ministry (he only formed one) he tilled Sydney
with trouble and made enough enemies to fill a
dozen Ministries. He and all his team are now
dead. Forster was perhaps the most intellectual
of old schoolmaster Cape’s pupils. His bent was
towards classical literature and the romantic
myths of the outer world. He was a native of
Madras, and was for a time Agent-General in
London.
Governor Kintore, now a London guinea-
pig. was lately sued by a shareholder in a Cold
Storage co. on account of misrepresentations in
the prospectus of said co. Kintore and other
directors settled the case by agreeing to take over
the plaintiff’s shares, which arrangement seems to
leave them open to legal proceedings by all the
rest of the suffering shareholders. The judge “ex-
pressed regret that gentlemen had lent their
honored names to people carrying on the most
unwholesome and sometimes unholy trade of com-
pany promotion.” Kintore’s name is still regarded
in London as something religious and good. His
father was an amateur preacher of the Word.
One heart-breaking incident occurred in
connection with the mysterious mission of a
boat-load of Maoriland law-makers to the South
Seas. Directly the vessel cast anchor off one of
the islands, a prominent Minister, in his scurry to
get ashore to fire off a patriotic speech and present
the local king with a quarter of a pound of bad
tobacco, missed his footing, and, instead of
stepping into the boat alongside, fell into the sea,
where he tried to curse but only gurgled. The
seat of his waistcoat was promptly snatched by
a large-fisted member of the boat s crew, and the
dripping legislator was hauled aboard and spread
out to dry. But the cheap, immaculate frock suit
he had donned to strike awe into the naked and
savage breast had shrunk to a large baby’s size
and was quite useless.
Captain H. D. Haggard, secretary of the
Adelaide Hospital, just dead, was the alleged
cause, in his earlier days, of the breaking up of a
bouth Australian Governor’s more or less miserable
home. He was an aide-de-camp then, and a
useful one. . But the Governor had given his wife
a ring, which was afterwards discovered in the
possession of a noted lady of loose walk, hair and
conversation. The police undertook to unravel
the mystery, but suddenly the Governor stopped
proceedings, and (presumably because Haggard
knew too much) dismissed the A.D.C. with great
violence. Which was ungrateful on the Gover-
nors part, for he and the A.D.C. looked very
much alike, especially in a dim light; and many
a time when his Ex. got hurriedly out of a house
that he shouldn t have been in, he, or some oblig-
circulated the report that it was the
A.D.C., and not his superior, who fled home in
the dawning.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —In the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
“Werrimee”: Re shearing query (B.
13/6/’O3). “Jimmy” Power is generally con-
sidered the champion machine shearer of Aus-
tralia, his highest tally being 315 mixed sheep in
7hrs. 40mins., made at Barenya (Q.), Sept. 21,
1895. It is said, however, that the majority of
these sheep were bellied, and many of them were
rosellas. His best season, for a run of sheds,
totalled 40,000. But seasons vary in length, as
shearers go from State to State. “ Jacky ” Howe
has done better with the “tongs,” shearing 327
full-fleeced sheep in 7hrs. 20mins., at Alice Downs
(Q.). This, I think, was in ’95, after a period of
drought. Record tallies are only made in sheds
where the men are allowed a free cut; most
managers restrict the men to a certain number,
seldom over 150, to avoid tomahawking. As
machines cut low and clean, the tallies will
average better than those made by hand. Breed,
condition and weight of sheep, weight of wool,
and the season just passed through, have a lot to
do with the tally-barometer. Fastest cuts are
made among lambs ; next come fat merino ewes;
while a big drop occurs in the wethers. Such
particulars should always be given ; then one could
at least get an approximate value of the records
posted.

Noticed lately a Melbourne cab-horse with
jive feet. The extra foot, a well-formed one, but
small, is attached to the pastern of one of the
hind legs. The animal is otherwise just a common
or back-street cab-horse.
“ Spring ” : Some people
have strange recreations. I once
met, in a cheap Sydney lodging-
house, a man who had a high
social position in another State.
He was dressed in shabby-genteel
clothes, lived on about Is. 6d. a
day, smoked cheap cigarettes,
saving the butts, and generally
behaved like a gent in reduced
circumstances. He confided to
me that he was over on a holiday,
enjoying the pleasures of poverty,
a month of whicli sent him back
to his work fit to enjoy his salary
and tackle the biggest of din-
ners. There lived in the same
house another eccentric indivi-
dual, whose shabby gentility was
quite real. His sole amusement
was to haunt the vicinity of high
buildings in course of erection,
and gloat dismally over the
chances of a bricklayer falling off.
He came home quite cheerfully
one night and told me that a
bricklayer nearly fell off, and
that he had great hopes. He said
bricklayers were a disappointing
race, and he had only seen one
fall in three years, but he was
now on the track of a reckless
artisan from whom he expected
much. Next night that crank
was missing. The bricklayer fell
on him just when he was lighting
his pipe, and he never even had
the pleasure of knowing what
struck him.

An Adelaide market-garden-
ing Chinaman has worked out the
size of money as it looks to the
European and the Chow ; in his
will he provided that everybody
who attended his funeral should
be paid—Europeans 25., Chinese
6d. Apparently he wasn’t sure,
on reflection, that he hadn’t been
too liberal towards the Chinese,
wherefore he added that they
should only draw their sixpences by instalments
of twopence a time.

“ Elmore ” : Saw a good go t’other day.
The warm weather we’ve been having evidently
made one gohanna think he’d turned in too early ;
anyhow, he got prowling about till something
scared him. Then he made for a big dry box, and
was just going in a hollow when he suddenly
backed out on to a small limb followed by a
’possum. The ’possum was evidently excited, and
was clawing for all he was worth. Out backed
the gohanna further and further till the limb
broke and both fell about 30ft. The ’possum
quickly clambered up his own tree again and the
gohanna, after a few seconds’ rumination, went
further on, neither being apparently hurt by the
fall. How far can animals fall with impunity ?

“ C.8.J.” : An old identity of the Upper
Murray told me the following:—“Hotels then
were few and far between, and shanties every-
where. On many a bush racecourse it was not
unusual to see a hollow log pressed into service as
a booth. The log contained bottles of spirits,
corkscrew and glasses, but no barman was to be
seen. You felt dry. Well, you led up your
friends to the log, had your nip, replaced every-
thing as you found it, and left your money in the
log. No one ever thought of ‘ doing the publican
in.’ Speaks well for the honesty of the ‘old
class.’ On one occasion a curious bobby watched
the ‘ hotel ’ for a whole day, but was no wiser at
the end of it as to who was enjoying the profits.”
* * * *
“ R.S.” : He birds’-nesting. The jackass
mostly nests in a rotten and horizontal, or slightly
drooping tree-limb, so that the nests can only be
examined at the risk of a broken neck. Rose lias
nest in any handy hollow, high or low. Lories
try to dodge the hawks by nesting far down in
pipey tree-trunks. Cockatoos choose the highest
hollow trees, and make a great noise where they
don’t intend to lay. And, by the way, has any-
one noticed the close friendship at nesting time —
apparently for protection—between minahs, mud-
larks and magpies ?
MISS MARY VAN BUREN, LEADING LADY
OF THE FRAWLEY COMPANY.
Photo, by Thori, San Francisco.
The native cherry tree, which used to be
largely found along the banks of the Northern
(N.S.W.) rivers, is rapidly disappearing, and now
almost a thing of the past. The berries are beau-
tifully flavored, and make excellent jam. Yet no
attempt has been made to protect this shrub,
which, by the way, carries handsome dark-green
foliage, and is as ornamental as it is useful. The
fauna and flora of Australia seem to be rapidly
passing away—barring the gum-tree, which holds
out pretty well.
WHEN HE DO.
THE GOOD PASTOR (to lady whose husband has
just wrecked the place): “ Dear me, Mrs.
Spicer , I always thought your husband was
such a nice, quiet, sober man, who would
never do anything like this.”
MRS. S. : "So he is. But when he do, he do.”
Evidence given in a Palmerston (Maori-
land) dog-ownership case:
One day my dog and his dog were fighting on the road.
He came along, and we both saw the fight out. Then I
was certain he owned the dog.

“Elmore”: Any of your Aboriginaliars
got any good records of a rabbit catch ? Here’s
my best: Two trappers, 130 traps, set on about
300 acres. First round (sundown) 58 pair ; lantern
round 46£ pairs; morning 53 pairs; total, lolk
pairs, and out of this lot 127 pairs were classed as
firsts. This was on Table Top station (N.S.W.)

“ J.D.” on a public disgrace :
As the cocky’s overworked youngsters find so
many to voice their woes, will some one stand
on the fence and call loudly on behalf of the State
kid, boarded out to hag boarding-housekeepers
and lodging-houses ? The cocky has some ex-
cuse, for the State contributes nothing to the
support of his large family of embryo agri-
culturists, while it pays ss. weekly for the scanty
food and clothing the State kid receives until
said kid is 12 years of age. The only protection
the State child receives from overwork and
tyranny is the occasional visit of a languid
inspector who, in nine cases out of ten, trusts to
his own eyesight to tell him of the child’s treat-
ment. The sbe-cat who ran my last “diggings,”
kept, in place of a general servant, a tiny State
girl aged eleven, whose life was a round of misery
with a junk of Hell thrown in. She got on
the wheel at 6 a.m. and finished all scrubbing,
cleaning, and dusting before breakfast. I have
seen her scrub out washhouse, kitchen, and back
and front verandahs, polish hall linoleum and
do a general dusting, besides lighting fires and
laying breakfast-table, before 8 a.m. She bolted
a scanty meal in the kitchen, then washed up
and went full speed till 9.30, when the harridan
would inform her that she could go to school.
Then the tiny machine would race breakneck
speed up the back lane to evade punishment for
being late. Many mornings she was not allowed
to go to school till 11 o’clock, the hag saying
that she had not performed sufficient work for
the morning. Washing days she was kept at
home all day, and assisted in all the heavy work.
At lunch hour she laid the table, and flew mess-
ages, sneaking occasional bites at her dinner
between her runs to the butcher and the groeer.
After school she tore round like a racing screw,
prepared all vegetables for dinner, cleaned
knives, and set table, then washed up the whole
caboose of dirty plates and dishes, and made a
general cleaning of the dirty’ kitchen. If she
finished before eight o’clock, her usual bed-hour,
she was allowed to sit in the kitchen with the
gas turned down and count the cockroaches that
came out fora look round. During the visit of
one of the hag’s daughters she slept on the floor
of an empty room, the daughter occupying her
bed. My wife inspected the bedding, and found
an old mattress, a worn-out blanket, and rug, with
no sheets. It is difficult to suggest any scheme that will
check the evil. The inspector sees nothing to cavil at,
and the sweater is so pleasant when he calls that he goes
away impressed that the child has seven courses for din-
ner, and sleeps in the big brass bed in the balcony room.
• • * *
“Paw-Paw” (Goondi) : The “sewing
bird ” (about the size of a tomtit) sews the leaves
of grass, or the wild strawberry, together for a
nest. The sewing is done with spider webs, and
when the nest is complete and lined with spider’s
“ silk ” it is a charming little residence.
THE SPIELERS AND THE GYRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It wa9 in the humble parlor of a Brisbane public-house,
There was James and there w’as Joseph and The Girl.
James and Joseph they were spielers of considerable nous,
And possessed the style and manners of an earl.
Very ugly were the attributes of Joseph and of James,
For they earned an easy living by acquiring artful games
Which the magistrates are apt to call by very nasty names,
And the trusted silent partner was The Girl.
James had lately struck a notion of a quite uncommon
kind,
And explained it unto Joseph and The Girl.
He had bought two hundred packs of cards as good as he
could find.
’Twas a scheme to make a person’s whiskers curl !
He would go as far as Adelaide and not attempt to sell,
But would leave some packs as samples, free, at every
large hotel,
And would quote a price absurdly low (and mark the
backs as well).
’Twas a plan approved by Joseph and The Girl I
Joseph had to stay in Brisbane till he got a wire from
(I should mention it was James that owned The Girl.)
When he’d go by boat to Adelaide and there begin the
games,
Working back by rail to Brisbane and The Girl.
See the dodge? ' It was one spieler left the cards along
the route
While the other had to play the games and gather in the
loot.
Fair division of the proceeds, and to save all coarse dis-
pute,
All the cash to go to Brisbane to The Girl!
James and Joseph met in Adelaide, where James soon
made it plain
Which hotels would have the sample cards on hand.
James then took the boat for Brisbane while his partner
took the train.
Twas as neat a spieler’s plot as e’er was planned !
But when James got back to Brisbane, then The Girl
explained to Jim
That his partner had persuaded her to be untrue to him '

the familiarity of these beastly Australian plague rats
is astounding. Perhaps the poor brute is — aw — delirious.”

really didn’t know

Until Joe got through Victoria, and she got desperate.
For “ she loved him, and for Joseph she had nothing else
but hate ! ’’
She was loaded with repentance to the brim !
Now, when Joe arrived at Goulburn he had done ex-
tremely well,
And the coin had gone to Brisbane to The Girl!
But the luck changed all at once, and how it did he
couldn’t tell,
The disaster set his senses all a-whirl.

Australia may see, in what Broken
Hill is being driven to do through
its want of water, a shocking ex-
ample of what some day it will
be driven to do itself if it
doesn't mend its ways.
Broken Hill has one mine
alone which, in 12
years, has paid
£7,600,000 in divi-
dends, most of
which money
has gone out
of Australia;
yet, whenever
the suggestion
was made that
it should col-
lect a decent tax
from the mining
co’s. (all the mines
are within the
municipality) and
get a respectable
water supply of
its own, there
were agonised
cries that it
would be
“ suicidal to
tax our own
bread and but-
ter ” and that,
if such a thing
w one, “ capital would
be driven out of the
country.” So nobody
was taxed, and the
town, out of which all
this wealth was being
carted away, was left
without a public water supply, or
lighting, or anything that a town
should have. When it wanted to
build a town-hall even, it borrowed the
few hundreds necessary, and when it hasn’t
been able to borrow, it has leaned up against the
central Government. And now, just because it
didn t have sense enough to get a water supply out
of its own vast wealth, lest it should slightly reduce
some foreign person’s pile, it is hanging out its
tongue and cutting a quaint figure before Aus-
tralia, while it chaffers with some Yankee rain-
fakir.
• * * «
The long sandbank which blocks the mouth
of Lake Illawarra (N.S.W.) is just now strewn
with thousands of dead eels, aggregating a weight
of many tons. The same thing lias occurred several
times previously in this lake. The mortality is
said to arise through the large quantities of fresh
water which periodically flow into the lake, which
has no outlet to the sea.
* * * •
“6x8”: Faith in molasses cure for can-
cers still seems to hold out in N.Q. One Alfred
Faux, of Kavandale, near Mirani (N.Q.), recently
wrote to Mackay Chbonicle that he had cured a
mare of two “ ugly, red, swamp cancers ” by only
two external applications of raw molasses, without
even leaving a scar, while hair again covered the
erstwhile diseased parts. If true, this is extra-
ordinary. Severe horse-sores invariably leave
hairless scars. Unless all we hear and read
is fabrication, or result of coincidence, crude
molasses must possess some curative power. The
matter seems worth a thorough overhaul by ex-
perts. Since, as has been frequently asserted,
treacle (t'.e., refined molasses) has not the effect of
the raw material, it seems to indicate that the
crude product contains a substance which is des-
troyed in the refining proc&ss. [Molasses as a
“cure” for cancer was tested and failed years
ago.—Ed. B.]

“A.R.Y.”: At a shearing shed near
Tumbarumba a man offered for a wager to shear
a sheep while on the top strand of a six-wire fence.
This he successfully accomplished. He began by
taking up his position on the fence and removing
his coat and waistcoat. He next sharpened his
shears; the sheep was handed up and shorn in
about the time usually taken on the board. It
was uncomfortable for the sheep, but the man
seemed all right.

The road from Sydney to Parramatta,
begun in 1798, wa3 made on the eight-hour prin-
ciple. Kesidents in good positions were compelled
to lend their employees in batches, and Governor
Hunter fixed the regulation that “each day’s
work shall consist of eight hours.”
WHEN YOU TACKLE IT AGAIN!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I had done hard work in boyhood—was accustomed to it
then,
Look’d upon it as no trouble, but the common lot of men
I had all day the mattock, “ breaking-up ” the
virgin soil,
And in summer in the quarry did the hottest kind of toil.
But I never called it hardship, or was tortured by the
pain
I have felt since, having left it, I’d to tackle it again 1
I had bought a “little bus’ness” and put in some years
at that—
It was easy work, although the profits didn’t make me
fat.
In an evil time I sold out, and soon after mourned the
fact,
For I found the labor market inconven-i-ently packed.
I had weeks and weeks of trying ; then, when Hope was
on the wane,
Came an offer of some hard work—so I tackled it again !
I’d a friend with land for farming, so he set me
“ breaking-up,"
And before the job was finished I had emptied Sorrow’s
cup 1
Though I started at it blithely with a thankful heart and
gay,
I was feeling mighty doubtful ere had dawned a second
day;
For the pain of aching muscles, and of untired, wakeful
brain,
Made me dread the coming daylight when I’d tackle it
again !
Day succeeded day in torture ; night succeeded night
the same X,
Naught but aching joints and muscles seemed my over
wearied frame. ,- . -! ■
And ’twas full a month, ere hard work and my muscles
got in chime— .. -. >
One long month of keenest suffering ; seeming to me all
the time
“ Breaking-up” was breaking-nw up, for I nearly went
insane;
Oh ! it’s Hell and Inquisition when you tackle it again I

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will betaken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. So liability is under-
taken in any ease re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no ease will MS. be returned unless stamps (oj‘ any
State) accompany.
Via: Who is the ex-P.M.G. referred to? You
might take u» into your confidence A. Williams:
Don’t agree with you fully. The Commonwealth should
take over, and consolidate at reduced interest when
possible, and start paying off the State debts. Whether
they are called Federal or State debts the same people
owe them; but the Australian Parliament looks like a
body which might have the courage to tax the people for
the purpose of clearing off the liability, and the State
Parliaments don’t. Therefore the Australian Parliament
should control the debt. It wouldn’t be “ a splendid
thing” to be able to point to the Australian Parliament
as a body that is free from debt while the Australian
people owe £200,000,000. But it would be “ a splendid
thing ” to be able to point to Australia free from debt....

sell over £3,000,000 worth of goods of one kind or
another in order to get the money with which to pay
interest on its public and private foreign debts. A
country in that state can’t put on export duties

A Maorilander of 70 years married a
second time the other day. As he was receiving
congratulations on the platform, prior to depart-
ing on the honeymoon tnp, the train moved off
with the bride, and left him vainly shouting
“Stop! Wait a minute?” Then the youthful
bridegroom hired a coach, and drove furiously by
a short-cut in the hope of intercepting the train
at a station ahead. Failing in this (it wasn’t
a Bent train), he hired a bicycle and completed the
journey, arriving at the wedding retreat the day
after his bride.
“ Engineer ” writes :
I used to think tbc tales about the fearsome state of
the Victorian railways and engines were exaggerated,
but I have altered my opinion. Taking a short-cut
recently, I walked for about a mile along a suburban line
with a heavy traffic of its own, and with three country
lines branching from it. Happening to kick against a
loose bolt, I observed the track carefully. I found
many dog-spikes (which hold the rails to the sleepers)
with their heads off. Each fish-plate has four bolts, with
check-nuts; dozens of these bolts I found loose (and,
therefore, useless); some plates had three out of the four
bolts loose. Two I unscrewed and replaced with my
fingers. Many dog-spikes were not hard up against the
rails. Rails were often fin. apart at ends. The sleepers
were so rotted they should nearly all have been replaced
months ago. This was on one of the steepest grades on
that line, and—quaint ftnd significant detail —the up-grade
was laid with 601 b. rails, the down with 801 b.

How little they know of New South Wales
who only Sydney know ! Attention having been
drawn last December to .the perilously low state
of the water supply at Broken Hill, the principal
Government engineer in that Department went
up from Sydney, found that there was enough to
last 'till May, and reported that there was no
need to worry or hurry—it was bound to rain
before then. Bound.to rain iqside five months?
Why, out there there’s sometimes only a spatter
and a promise inside five years !
Victorian Lands Minister Taverner has
been “ taking steps ” fQr a long while towards
abolishing the Leongatha Labor Colony—an
institution which, in spite of amateur manage-
ment and many bungles, has relieved thousands
and to-day is actually relieving 200 necessitous
cases by more economical and less pauperising
methods than any other “relief” agency. It
assists the “ stone-broker” without degrading him
and turns him out able to earn a living in any of
the “great natural industries.” Taverner has
seemingly been got at by land speculators or
hungry acre-eaters who want the Leongatha block.
“Jaybell” spreads himself:
Passing down Queen-street not long since I stood for a
moment to view the stock-in-trade of a curb-stone toy
merchant, who dangled a very ingeniously-contrived
monkey at the end of a piece of elastic. The man’s face,
was expressive of one given to much brooding over
worldly misfortune—just as Henry Parkes may have
looked what time he greened the sportive simian and
ochred its whittled stick for the bread of life. While I
stood, two fatted bookmakers bore down upon the scene,
and stood to scoff. The cream of their remarks was, in
effect, that the State should lay violent hands upon such
a lazy beggar and make him work. Then I thought of
the hours and deft fingers it .took to make the little
stock-in-trade of childhood’s penny-wonders. Likewise,
I tried to call to mind the honest toil, or effort, or
avocation, or whatever you like to call it, of the ordinary,
illiterate, cheating, prevaricating, mean-spirited, close-
fisted, obscene bookmaker, with his swindle and his
cheap note-book and his stump of pencil. The ran-
sacking hurt my brain, and, as I moved on, I marvelled
why an all-seeing Providence hasn’t made provision for
a special annual tornado, to sweep the said bookie and
his pocketful of sinful memoranda away to the back of
where his awful voice would come handy to wail with,
and his big shop-teeth could do some good, useful
gnashing. There is an awful amount of gnashing which
should be done, but which hasn’t vet been attended to.

PRIVATEERING ENTERPRISE.
“Now, what is the principle upon which the commercial activity of that marvellous people depends. It is
that of leaving the people of the United Kingdom in their varied enterprises as free from the interference of law
and regulation as possible.”— Dry-Dog Reid to interviewer.
HUMBLE FREETRADER (discovered by eminent and expostulating ditto in eminent and expostu-
lating ditto's jewel chamber): "Now, sir, don’t be unreasonable, I pray of you.
Surely you wouldn’t go for to hamper our young industry wif the interferences of law
and regulations!”
“ E.”: I know a man and wife who have not
paid any rent for six years, barring an occasional deposit
of 2s. 6d. or ss. on taking a house. They haven’t nearly
enough furniture to make it worth while putting a bailiff
in, and if that little is seized—which happens very rarely
—they just stay on. It takes at least three weeks, after
the hopelessness of the case is recognised, to get such a
tenant out, and then he generally gets square prior to
his departure by tearing the paper and breaking the
mantelpiece. The worst house I ever went through was
that one in Balmain in which a portion of Lane’s first
New Australia contingent had camped prior to their
sailing for Paraguay. It took close on six weeks’ rent to
repair damages and clean up—especially clean up. I have
often wondered since if Cosme smells at all like that
house.
» * * *
A correspondent tella of a comparatively new
swindle :—Witnessed a pathetic incident to-day. A blind
organ-grinder’s machine broke down with a wild whirring
note as he was finishing " Love’s Serenade” in front of a
suburban hotel. The landlord rushed out to see whose
soul had just been lost, and 17 men, eight women, and
44 children collected before the last wild cry had died
away. Two men offered to fix the machine up. The
blind man was still frantically turning the handle while
his little girl was trying to stop him, saying it was no
use. The two men started to overhaul the machine
while the crowd increased. Rumor spread it round that
a drunken man had wrecked the machine, the sole sup-
port of the blind man and 14 kids. The women turned
up their eyes, and said, “What a sheemel” The two
experts, whose efforts were breathlessly watched by the
crowd, at last pronounced the machine incapable
of further torture. A pin which connected the
revolving disc with the handle had been lost —had fallen
into the bowels of the machine probably ; and it was all
up with it until another pin was fitted in its place. At
this juncture a forceful-looking man with a bleary eye
proposed a collection and made a short speech picturing
the 14 youngsters starving at home. He wasted no time,
gs the crowd showed an inclination to dodge down a side
street or slip into the adjacent pub.. The collector fol-
lowed them into the pub. and levied on all, the land-
lord contributing a shilling “as it was his pub. the
machine shied at.” Finally the forceful man presented
the organ-grinder with 6s. 3d., and that worthy wiped
his sightless eyes and said “ Gawdblessyer ” 17 times.
Then he left. Three minutes afterwards a cynic arrived
add, addressing the few who were still lounging about,
said, “ Collecting for a horgan-grinder here ? ’’ Somebody
nodded. “Fourth collection I know of to-day,” said the
cynic ; “ he takes the screw out of his machine and puts
ft in his pocket. Then the other bloke takes the hat
round.”
A choice bargain was secured by the canny
committee of Dunedin (M.L.) Athenaeum lately, when
a lot of books were sold at brown paper prices. They
got a complete set of Voltaire’s works in French for 3d.
a vol.—a very ancient edition, published between 1784
and 1790. The books for many years adorned the shelves
of an old resident of Dunedin, who once possessed a ven
fine library.

• * • •
The proprietors of the “ Commercial” and th.
“ True Blue ’’ were vicious, and reviled each other ; con
sequently the bagmen who journeyed that way, canvass
ing for whisky orders, could only do business with the
house where they stayed. One wilv French bagman got
over the difficulty. He arrived 1 . the 6.15 p.m. train,
drove to the “ Commercial,” ordered a room, and left hi 6
portmanteau. He hastened to the “ True Blue,” ordered
a room, left his grea< coat and umbrella, and went in to
dinner. He had a lignc meal and a bottle of claret. Half,
way through, he got up from the table hurriedly, and
explained to the waiter an urgent telegram had to be sent
off immediately, and fled to the “ Commercial." There
he had another light dinner and a bottle of claret. He
slept at the “ True Blue,” and came down sharp to break-
fast. Ten minutes later he was explaining at the “ Com-
mercial ” about an all-night card party at a friend's house.
He managed a second light breakfast of a grilled ohop
and a cup of coffee. Also, he booked an order for liquor
from each hotel, and left, over-full of provisions, but
happy.
* * * *
A note concerning pearls, from the wild North-
West : “ The buyers carefully sort the gems into classes
and buy them by the ounce, or, in case of pearls over
lOgrs., by the grain—No. 1, No. 2, barrocs (pearls of good
lustre but of bad shape), dummies (discolored), and blis-
ters. Yellows are kept separate from the whites, and
are classed in the same way. Orientals prefer the
yellows; Europe takes nearly all the wb'ies, though
many pass through India first. The Indian jarket has a
very large say indeed in the prices, which, locally, fluc-
tuate a lot. No. l’s, white, bring here from £2O to £l4O
per oz. ; same in yellows, from £lO to £4O. The
seed pearls so much used in middle-class jewel-
lery can be had down to about 30s. per oz.;
discolored pearls down to 6s. But there are dis-
colored pearls which are very rare and valuable
‘barred’pearls, ‘tipped’ pearls (a few straw-colored
with pink tips), a rare black one, and a rarer all-pink.
They run in all shapes, too —cones, buttons, pears, ovals,
and clusters. * Blisters,’ though included in the list of
classes, are not true pearls, but are the pearly excres-
ences inside the shell. Sometimes, however, when the
skin of a ‘ blister ’ is dissolved a pearl is found within.
Of the ‘ dummies ’ a good many of the cheaper ones go
East, where they are ground up and used asmedioine.
• * * *
Decided in the Adelaide local court that a land-
owner can recover from his lessee the full value of all
limestone taken out of the land, even though the farmer
only received a royalty of 3d. a yard. Witnesses showed
that all excavations had been filled up, and that the land
was probably better for farming than before, but the
plaintiff pressed forand recovered £99 from the defendant,
who had only received £3O altogether. The court
“ regretted ” having to give the decision.
• * * *
It is absolutely necessary that something should
be done to reverse that infamous decision given by a
Sydney magistrate, to the effect that the time-payment
furniture man, or the fiend with the bill of sale, may,
when one instalment is overdue, break into a house in
the absence of the householder and carry off everything.
The results of that decision, which legalises burglary and
lends the authority of the law to midnight breaking and
entry, are most disastrous. Now with a certain infamous
class of money-lender life is one long struggle to persuade
the borrower to fall in arrear with his last or second last
payment. When the debt is nearly paid, the agent never
calls for the regular weekly instalment. If it is taken
round to the office, the shark’s clerk almost implores
the debtor “ not to pay unless he is quite cer-
tain hs can spare the money.” He is assured that
there is no hurry, that it doesn’t matter even if the
payment does fall in arrear, and so on. And if he can be
persuaded to fall in arrear, half-a-dozen minor sharks,
with vans, burst in the first time the house is left un-
guarded, and everything goes—for, perhaps, a balance of
10s. overdue. The tenant returns to find a broken door
and utter ruin. The new legalised burglary system gives
the debtor no chance to tender the money. He can’t
find out where his furniture has gone to. When he does
hear about it he learns that perhaps £4O worth of goods
were sold at auction (presumably to the shark’s agent),
and only realised a few shillings. Unless the legal deci-
sion which authorises all this is rescinded Sydney will
have a tragedy on its hands one of these days—and ia
dead shark.

A White Australia ! Stated in the Westralian
Arbitration Court recently that the cabinet-making
industry found employment for 200 Chinese and only 50
European tradesmen.
Possibly the biggest price paid for any agri-
cultural land in the Commonwealth is the £5 per acre
charged for a season’s “ cropping" of the comparatively
•mall patch of rich black volcanic potato-land in the S.E.
of S.A. The period for which the land is held varies with
the weather, but it never goes over about nine months—
often it is much shorter. It there isn’t any “ blister,” or
" black spot,” or other potato trouble around the grower
may dig 14 tons to the acre. Five pounds for, say, seven
months’ use—and the fool State sold the freehold of it
for 12s. fid. an acre 1
• • * *
The question of converting the debts of the
States into a Federal debt, with all-round advantage to
the States, appears to have suggested a scheme for the
conversion of the debts on the R.O. churches of N.S.W.
into a N.S.W. Holy Roman liability. The churches are
almost all in debt, many of them paying as high as 10 per
cent; on the other hand, wealthy R.O’s., ladies in
particular, are only drawing 8 or 4 per cent, on deposits
in hanks. Between these two rates there is plenty of sea
room for a competent financier to construct a financial
concern which will give the R.C. lady 5 per cent., and
reduce the interest bill of the holy edifice to 6 per cent.
• • • •
“Chassela3”: The imports of agricultural im-
plements and machinery into Victoria increased from
£296,964 in 1899 to £473,766 in 1902. The lowering of the
tariff by the Australian Parliament Is having a disastrous
effect on Vic. manufactures. In all farming districts you
may see American implements at work ; a Freetrade
English machine is unknown. Australian makers are
very poorly represented. Out of 60 ploughs sold in
newly-settled country in S.W. Vic. 46 were of American
make, and the same thing applies to all implements and
tools used on the farms. ’Murkan firms sold all machinery
on extra good terms, and the 8 per cent, interest charged
will hang like a mill-stone around Vic. farmers’ necks for
vears.
• * ♦ •
There’s “business, robbery, ete.,” in the chaff
trade, too. With fowl-feed at a decent price, it pays to
knock out the half-formed grain ; a machine has been
contrived for the purpose. The now almost-universal
“sheaving" of hay makes the operation simple. Then
there’s the watering. The disgusted farmer, who has
iust run his waggon-load of hay over the weighbridge
dry, and has been bullied if a shower has fallen on ft,
finds that the hose is turned on it all the time it is being
thrown into the cutting mill; and often it gets a second
dose as it is going down to the cutters. Even after that
the chaff-buyer has to keep his eyes open or hs will be
fold “ short” tons—2ooolb. instead of 22401 b.
The sort of letter which N.S,W.
Advances to Settlers Board reoeiveS when
it won’t advance the nation’s money on bad
security : “ Sirs,—Permit me to return my most
sincere thanks for the attention given to my
letter asking you to increase the amount of
my loan. I regret to have troubled you during
the festive season, and that you could not see
your way clear to make the advance. Had
it been a squatter who had asked for a cool
thousand, he would, no doubt, have got it with-
out trouble. I hope you will never want a loaf,
and seeing the well-greased billets you hold,
I have no doubt you never will. But I hope
when you cross the border line your tongues
will hang out a yard to all eternity. With
the season’s compliments, I am, yours
A recent survey of a Southern Vic. estate out
up for closer settlement showed a clear gain of from
9to 12 acres on every section of land. It would be inter-
esting to know how many acres of land the old surveys
have robbed from the State. Syndicates buying big
estates always count on these pickings, and the State
should find out where it comes in before the amended
titles are issued.

“J.F.D. A man came round our back
lane yesterday hawking rabbits. He was offering them
in exchange for bottles, old rags, and scrap iron. I
looked into his van, where a dozen “bunnies" were
smothered up with a collection of pickle bottles, old
dresses, and remains of bedsteads. The nice, fresh
“ wile rabbi’.”
• * • *
Through absorption of moisture, dry-country
wool sometimes gains as much as 201 b. per bale (say 4 per
cent.) after being landed in Sydney stores. There is in-
weight and out-weight; most times the grower is paid
on the in-weight, and sometimes he kicks in conse-
quence. There is the same absorption in connection
with wheat grown in dry districts ; but there is a better
chance for the middleman to work a small swindle there,
because he pays the farmer on the weight at the local
railway siding, while he sells on the seaboard weight—
when every bag is a couple of pounds heavier.
Vio. cockies, to evade income-tax, put wages
for all their sons against income. Of course the wages
(so-called) are never passed over to the boys. Half-a-
crown once or twice a year to spend at the local show or
races is the extent of the old man’s expenditure. Writer
knows well-to-do cockies, with several sons, who have
never paid a penny income-tax for years by this ruse.
Some day a cookie’s son will sue for these wages, and let
loose the dogs of law on the old man.
* * * *
The Bulletin receives a circular, issued, naked
and unashamed, by a godly firm, which tells the butcher
how he can be a larger thief than usual. The secret lies
in a sausage machine, which has these virtues (quota-
tions are from the godly firm’s circular)
“ You can work in 60 per cent, more water. We have
seen a butcher take 1401 b. meat, and, by adding water
when cutting, when smoked and boiled he bad 1151 b. of
Wieners, 751 b. of Frankfurters, and 651 b. of ham sausage.
This is 1151 b. clear profit. At-3d. per lb., they would
make 28s. 9d. clear profit on the extra water, without the
profit on the balance. The knives go through the meat
so quick that they cut it in a doughy condition,
which absorbs so much more water. In another case, we
have seen 301 b. of water worked in 401 b. of meat. A
butcher can make a great deal more money by making
his own sausage. If you buy it you will pay all the
profit to the manufacturer. A cutter would make
you considerably more profit in a year from the extra
water it will work in."
And, then, after giving more information about the
profit the butcher can make by adding just a little more
water to the sausage, and selling water as sausage
without being found out, this fool firm puts its customers
away by printing a list of the people who are using the
swindle machine.
• * * *
The erstwhile smellful buildings known as
“ Kidman’s,” in Market-street, Sydney, have been “done
up ” and are now known as “ York Buildings,” by way of
misguided compliment to the Dook. The pile was erected
by the late Charles Kidman out of the building material
of the old Sydney Hospital—the Rum Hospital—which
was erected early in the reign of Governor Macquarie
and was demolished in 1879.

inner one is usually Jfilled with dried apples or other
marketable com-modity.

One large Melbourne firm contributed 10
guineas to the “ loyalist ” fund for blackleg drivers ; also
it closed its establishment at five ipstead of six to suit
the convenience of the Railway Department. At the end
of the week it docked its employees for the hour each
evening—a number of girls receiving the munificent
salary of 2s. 6d. per week were cut down to 2s. 3Jd. 1!
And it was a “loyal” and Christian firm, after Bent’s
own heart. What a number of steals, meannesses, and
cadgings- of •an - odd-copper-out-of-a-blind-man’s-hat are
covered by the word “ loyalty ” 1

“B. Rid ”: Mention of rams and German
sausage (“ Ed.” 8., 16/5/’O3), reminds me of a butcher in
a small N.S.W. inland town who had purchased a line of
aged rams. Being in forward condition he hung the
carcases in the shop. One day a specially big one was on
show. A cocky came along. “ Fine sheep that; where
did you get ’em ? ” “Oh ! one of Mr. So-and-So’s cross-
bred wethers,” unblushingly answered the cleaver-man.
“ By Jove ! he must be pretty good then, and I like a bit
of good mutton, too. I’ll take the lot.” “Right you
are, old man. I know you’ll like it I’’ Next day an
infuriated cocky and three-parts of a ram’s carcase filled
the shop, but the butcher fled when he saw them coming.

were formed to plant violets and dandelions on the
graves of “our heroes” in S’Africa, are at their
last gasp ; some of them are only kept going now
by that last resort of the cadger, the bazaar am
art union. The patriot doesn’t part with t
shilling any longer unless he can get a pincus!
or a picture for it.
Sydney D.T. wails dolefully that the
“ freetrade” railway rate for newspapers is higher
than the rate for milk. If the Herald made that
complaint it would be reasonable; where milk
goes for a halfpenny, milk-and-water should surely
pass for a farthing. As regards the Telegraph
the real question is : “ What’s the rate fm
venomous reptiles?”

THE THIEVING GOD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a store-house great and strong
That mortal eyes may never see ;
And safe it is, and doth belong
To some old god who’s miserly—
Who robs us mortals that he may
Make sure against a rainy day;
And many imps, right scurvy pimps,
Are thieving in his pay.
No other way may I explain
Why Jones from debt is never free,
Why, every time I “call again,”
He tells the same old yarn to me :
A cheque is coming—thus the tale—
To-morrow week he will not fail;
He said ’twas rum it did not come
This morning by the mail.
Now Jones is not the only one
Expecting what he does not get,
And I am not the only dun
Who calls for gold and finds regret.
And so I say some miserly
Old god is robbing Jones and me—
For Jones would pay the very day
That this cheque came, you see 1
There is a store-house great and strong
That mortal eyes may never see,
And safe it is, and doth belong
To some old god who’s miserly.
Oh, his dark vaults contain a pack
Of stolen cheques, and, too, a stack
Of pilfered gold—the wealth untold
All hopeful debtors lack.
H. A. CONANT.
TO A RELIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TCx stand, old tub, beside the race, your staves are
v arped and worn,
Yet in their rusty hoops’ embrace what ardent hopes were
born 1
The hopes we knew when Fortune strayed about the Flat
so long,
When rollers creaked and paddles swayed attuned to
joyous song :
Of what you held, tales yet are told ; when rocked by
eager hands
The yellow wavelets lapped the gold upon the shelving
sands ;
When nuggets decked that mimic strand, and ranges rang
with glee,
Clear loomed the homes our hopes had planned for brides
beyond the sea.
Our hopes now mostly drink-wards trend, and that’s the
solemn truth;
We’ll make of you a fire, old friend of golden, wasted
youth !
Perchance old Fortune left behind some gold in hidden
chinks,
And in your ashes we may find enough to pay for drinks.
vie.
FRANT BELLMAN.
