POLITICAL
POINTS
M.L. Premier Seddon, who has not shed
all of his sense by any means, proposes during the
coming session to increase the tax on absentees.
Senator Higgs has tabled a motion in the
Senate that the Imperial Government should be
invited to appoint an Australian as Governor-
General.
The Irvine Govt, has definitely announced
that it is not its intention to introduce a Bill
compelling Victorian civil servants to wear rings
in their noses.
If Seddon can pass a Bill which he has
prepared, future M.L. Judges will retire at 70.
Some recent scandals have prompted this. And
even 70 is sometimes too old.
According to the Sydney Freetrade press,
unlimited competition leads to improvement.
When are the daily papers, that have just lost
their free carriage Protection, going to show the
beneficial results of the cool, bracing tonic ? There
-are no signs so far.
a The unemployed followed leader Fleming
up to Melbourne gaol last week, and desired to be
locked up out of the cold. As they were not
importers or Kyabramapootras, no charge could
be levelled against the deputation, and it was
still at large when this issue went to press.
The Owe’Sullivan achieved a distinction
lately which is making Philp and Leahy writhe:
The Queensland town of Goondiwindi welcomed
him with a procession, a banquet, and a brass
band. Certainly Goondiwindi wants a railway,
and the Owe’S. is the man who promises rail-
ways.
London Daily Chronicle from its Aus-
tralian correspondent re the Vic. railway strike :
It is rumored that when the strike ia over a proposal
will be submitted to Parliament for leasing the railways
to an En-glish syndicate.
That probably was the game of Irvine and Bent
from the first; and it is still being played.
The dancing-master tripping about of
Black Rod at the opening of N.S.W. Parliament
the other day provoked several members to laugh.
That is a bad sign, but a worse one was when
several ladies audibly tittered. As long as sham
can be treated seriously there is health in it;
when it is giggled, at it promises soon to topple
into the grave.
The See Government, at the opening of
N.S.W. Parliament, put into the mouth of the
Governor a strong “ leading article ” in its defence.
John See claims that the daily newspapers won’t
give fair play to his speeches and statements, and
he took advantage of the fact that they couldn’t
well cut down the Governor’s speech, to force them
to publish a solid two columns of his side of the
case.
KEEPING THE WHEELS GOING .
Rumored that Thos. Rent is training for another
railway strike.
Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, is boasting
now that he is a Freetrader and always has been
a Freetrader. Still another Dry Dog leader—a
man who has been for 10 years Premier of a State
with a thorough-going Protectionist tariff of no
less than 304 lines ! The Freetrade Premier who
maintains 304 protective duties is no less of a
fraud than the teetotal fanatic who owns 304
public-houses.
Even Servia, which has just murdered its
Obrenovitch so horribly, has its good points. It
has free and compulsory education, which includes
agriculture and training in domestic duties; it
maintains public granaries as a precaution against
glut and famine; there are State-worked coal
mines, and there are no fat landlords and practi-
cally no poor. “ The poorest have some sort of
freehold property,” says the Statesman’s Yeah
Book.
' The “ great natural producing industries,”
w iich “don’t require coddling,” are coddled all
the time. S.A. has for years manfully struggled
to make its own ploughshares in spite of the want
of locsl iron and coal; but it finds that, though
the railways put local farm produce in class A,
they put local ploughshares in class I. So a ton
of easily-handled ploughshares, in bundles, costs
£3 in freight where a ton of unwieldy wine-casks
costs only £l.
Victorian Lands Minister Taverner has
been discovering that the pious example of the
last Minister (M’Kenzie) has been followed in the
Lands Department:
The Minister has found that one of his officers has been
holding 26,000 acres of grazing land under lease, of which
he is claiming a seven years' renewal. It is also stated
that surveys of 200 and 300 acre blocks in this man’s dis-
trict, recommended to be allotted to certain applicants
three years ago, have not been made, and thus the men
have been debarred from settling on the land.
Since Q. Minister Leahy took over the
administration of the Charities Department,! he
has been moving heaven and hell —and a bit of
purgatory—to revolutionise the administrative
methods of his predecessor, Foxton, now Minister
for Lands. Foxton, with all his impetuosity,
is a more humane man than Leahy, and hesitated
to cut down vital expenditure on relief. But the
new broom is sweeping clean with a vengeance,
and threatens to brush the old indigents into the
street.
At the Adelaide Conference of the Cham-
bers of Commerce, Meeks, of Sydney, speaking on
the question of levelling the wharfage charges
throughout the Commonwealth, said that if the
wharves were owned privately the owners had the
right to charge what they liked. This is con-
sistent Tory doctrine. Capital may demand
what it likes for its services, but, if Labor tries to
get the best for its services, there is a howl of
execration all through the respectable portion of
the community.
It is a pity some original-minded member
didn’t propose, as an addition to Carruthers’
motion that the N.S.W. Assembly disapproved
of the Government, the statement “also this
House doesn’t think much of the Opposition
either.” Then a level-headed and independent
member would have a chance of voting against
both parties and could.thus do his duty all round.
The system by which a member can’t disapprove,
of See without approving of Carruthers is an
insult to the public intelligence.
The tricky move of the Victorian House
of 48 Rich Landlords, whereby all new Wages
Board decisions under the existing temporary
Factories Act must be carried by a seven-tenths
majority, has, as a matter of course, prevented
anything further being done under the Minimum
Wage law. Seven boards have been sitting since
last October trying to get the necessary majority
for even the simplest division ; so far, they have
cost the State £550, and not one has done any-
thing. Some more Kyabram economy.
Weird rumor from Melbourne :
Treasurer Shiels is getting a bit nervous over the 5$
million Victorian debentures maturing December 31.
Consultations with bank managers are now frequent and
Cabinet meetings numerous. The British money-lender
declines to “convert” or even discount Vic. Treasury
paper. As the State Trust Accounts and the Savings
Bank funds are pretty well used up Shiels is said to con-
template commanding the 10 banks to take up half-a-
million Treasury notes each. Meanwhile Irvine is urging
further economies and the civil servants expect another
cut. Anyhow it is pretty certain that the reductions
made last year will continue indefinitely.
Last Tamworth (N.S.W.) election—inci-
dents of whioh are to come before the law courts
shortly was full
of fun and bedevil-
ment. Labor party
advocates, while
addressing open-
air meetings, were
followed around by
a small crowd of
boo-hooers, and so
resorted to strategy
to get to the ears
of those who
wished to hear
them. A meeting
was announced for
one hotel balcony,
and a leat her-
lunged friend
yelled there at a
hooting crowd,
whilst the real
speakers of the
evening slipped
away and gathered
orderly meetings
at other corners.
Soon the leat her-
lunged man
found his work
hard and stopped
speaking, but con-
tinued to mouth and
gesticulate. For half-
an-hour he said nothing,
to the accompaniment of a
storm of.groans and hoots. Then
an Irishman in the crowd detected the deception.
“Begorra, bhoys, he’s sayia’ nothing,” he yelled,
and the meeting broke up.
A case that showed the necessity of more
honest administration of the old age pension
system turned up at South Melbourne court
lately. It was shown that two unnatural sons,
to escape paying a couple of shillings a week
apiece towards their father’s pension, had repre-
sented the ancient as a thriftless drunkard;
whereupon the miserly Treasurer, without giving
the old man a chance to refute the slander,
promptly cancelled the allowance; and it was
only when the ex-pensioner started to die publicly
of starvation that the hideous injustice done him
became known. The whole pension system can-
not be too soon transferred to the Federal
Government, if only because'its administration is
certain to be such a pleasing contrast to the mean,
huckstering, “charity-giving” spirit displayed
by Shiels and his friends.
Some evidence re the blessings of a low
tariff given before the iron bonus commission :
Joseph Vaughan (iron master, of South Melbourne)
stated that he manufactured angle, T, and bar iron, the
raw material being scrap iron drawn from the various
Australian States. Witness employed 80 hands. They
worked only 26 weeks last year, the cause being com-
petition by Belgium, from which place a lot of steel was
imported. Belgian competition was so keen that he
anticipated pulling his works down and taking them to
South Africa. Some of the best of his men would be
taken over too.
BENT’S NEXT MOVE.
“ When asked about the loans to employees, Mr. Bent
said : ‘ I have been thinking about this for some time. I
have seen in the papers many insolvencies of railway em-
ployees, and I think that these should be stopped.
Tradesmen and sellers of goods must be protected, and
whether their customers are railway employees or not,
they have to be paid. We will have to bring in a scheme
of paying our employees so that they will not have any
excuse for dodging their tradesmen.’ ” — Melb. Herald.
Bent (to railway employee): “Your tradesmen’s
• bills prove to me that you Have been squander-
ing too much of your money on trades unions.
In future I will draw your screw, and settle
your bills — in fact, manage all your domestic
affairs. ”
The Federal tariff damaged Victoria badly
by reducing Protection, but it vastly assisted
New South Wales by increasing Protection. In
1902—the first complete year of Protection—
N.S.W. gained 4561 people by excess of arrivals
over departures ; and considering that, during the
previous six lamentable years of comparative
Freetrade, it lost 10,223 people by excess of de-
partures the record for 1902 is something remark-
able. The total increase of population, by births
and immigration, in 1902, was nearly 26,000, a'
figure which hasn’t been approached since 1892.
Australian Premier Barton is occasionally
able to stir himself and to make a dignified stand
on behalf of the Commonwealth. Extract from
a letter of his secretary to Sydney D.T. (replying
to a shriek on the free carriage cadge):
In view of the fact that you are prepared to allow the
publication in your columns of statements on this sub-
ject, which have been proved to be baseless, and of
charges which you know to be false, I am to state that
the Prime Minister feels hound to decline to hold any
further communication with you upca this question,
until Charges, thus publicly made, are as publicly with-
drawn.
One Bibby, Chairman of the Pacific Navi-
gation Co., has arisen in London to whine that
Australia ought to be so beastly grateful for the
good treatment that it has received from the
shipping companies that any talk about prohibit-
ing black labor is hideous ingratitude. Well,
this country is determined that people like the
Pacific S.N. Co. shall not be allowed any longer
to sacrifice themselves for us. We must not im-
pose on their generosity, and by abolishing
cheap black labor we shall, no doubt, awaken
them to a proper respect for their own welfare.
Q. Govt, is considering the advisability of
wiping out its system of out-door relief—ss. a
week. The money dole is being abolished, and
rations sub- tituted. AQ. Minister asserted pub-
licly he knew of one man getting a wage of 6s. 6d.
a day who drew the old-age allowance. The Q.
Minister did not give the name of the individual;
neither did he prosecute him for obtaining relief
under false pretences, neither did he punish the
officials whose neglectful administration was
responsible. He only mouthed this probable lie
in public with the intention of blackening the
outdoor-relief system.
The Freetrade party will be long in re-
covering from that disastrous speech by British
Freetrader Campbell-Bannerman, in which he
declared that practically one-third (12,000,000) of
the people of Freetrade Britain have insufficient
food, and live perpetually on the verge of hunger.
Certainly there are not so many paupers in
Britain as there were, but a pauper is simply a
person who receives poor relief, and the autho-
rities can reduce pauperism to any extent—can
abolish it -altogether, in fact—simply by refusing
relief. Callousness can extinguish pauperism
even more rapidly than prosperity.
Already Westralia feels the pinch which
follows its reckless dealings with land-grabbing
railway syndicates. Some growing towns find
themselves hemmed in ; and a repurchase policy
is coming. It is a beautiful swindle altogether. (1)
The public gives the syndicate land for practically
nothing. (2) The public gives the land a value.
(3) The public pays the syndicate the full value
which it has itself created. Having given the
land, the public can’t get that back ; but it can
get some of the value (which it, itself, has created)
by a land-tax. So a land-tax ought to precede a
repurchase policy every time.
The demand of the Tory party that Vic-
torian Premier Irvine should do something to
protect Bent’s blacklegs against the Tocsin
which has been publishing their names and ad-
dresses, is a curious one. The Tory party says
that these men are heroes, admired by the great
mass of the community, and if it believes this it
should be rather pleased to have the Tocsin iden-
tify them so that the great mass of the community
may go and worship them. 1
Queensland’s Redistribution of Seats Bill
will, it is hoped, give the Philp Minis-
try a longed-for chance to flatten out
some political opponents. There is a
great art in redistributing seats. Very
many years ago, Elbridge Gerry of a
New England (U.S.) State, laid the
foundations of the art. He found
when a redistribution of seats was
on, that he had become so unpopular
that no ordinary method of dividing
the State would leave a single elec-
torate that wanted Gerry at any
price. So the few scattered dis-
tricts where he had a pull were
made into an electorate regard-
less of everything else. And
when the work was finished
Gerry’s electorate was in the
shape of a very long salamander
and straggled right across the
State. Then the people made
a compound word of “ Gerry ”
and “salamander” and “gerry-
mander” was invented.
Premier Philp’s recent N.
Queensland tour foreshadows a
fresh large batch of ‘shindycat’
measures. Normanton, George-
town, Cairns, Ravenswood, C.
Towers, and every other place he
visited heard nothing but flam about “our vast
natural resources,” and hosannas to private
enterprise in the shape of shindycats.
Last year Victoria had about the poorest
harvest for the past 20 years, and even with only
a handful of grain to move, the helpless, hulking
Bent broke down in his manngement and there
was a deadlock. This year Victoria seems like
having the biggest harvest for 20 years, and what
' 111 happen with the rotten permanent way, and
the broken engines driven by Bent’s new drunks,
and the reduced staffs, and the crippled rolling
stock, Heaven only knows.
Again Carruthers, the weary platitudi-
narian, states the policy by which he is going to
save N.S.W. if he reaches “hoffis” :
The cause of the masses will be safe in my hands, for j
will aim to secure to them equal justice, equal rights and
better opportunities to employ their labor. Believing as
I do in the noble work of education as a means of up-
lifting a country and its people, believing as I do that the
land is the source of all wealth, and that an educated
people settled on the soil and developing our resources
by their enlightened industry will tend more to national
wealth and greatness, I shall always make every effort to
improve our educational methods, and to more largely
encourage a system of settlement on our lands, whereby
the trend of population may be diverted from over-
crowded cities to the land, which is pining for population.
It is on a broad-minded policy such as is embodied in these
ideas that the salvation of this State and its workers
depends. All other matters such as we hear so
much vaunting over, such as pensions, early closing, and
artificial restrictions on man’s freedom—justifiable as
they may be —are but superficial remedies for a disease
which is deep-seated. We want in this country more
intelligence in productive efforts, more self-determination
in the struggles of life, and freer access to our land and
all its undeveloped riches. These are the means of per-
manent betterment of our people, and with a reformed
Parliament, which will shake off the myths and fallacies
now so blindly followed, we shall see the inauguration of
a new and a better era for our country and our country-
men.
“Broad-minded policy,” “settlement on the
lands” “trend” “productive effort,” “develop-
ing our resources,” “access to the land,” “un-
developed riches” “inauguration,” “freedom!”
The writer camped out one night last summer by
the Nymboyda River, and a cow-bell was making
just these same remarks on the bank all night.

Thirty Years a Sufferer.
VITADATIO
OT7RBDIIIM.
Mr. S. A. PALMER, Sydney.
Dear Sir,—lt is now close on 30 years since I had the
first attack of a cold, which has troubled me continuously.
At first the-cough was only slight, and, as most people
thoughtlessly do, t did not take any particular care of
myself, thinking the cough would soon leave me. I was
then about 14st. in weight; hut I began to get much
worse, and, in spite of medical attendance, I soon found
the cold had such a severe hold on me that nothing appar-
ently did me any good. For years I tried many of the
advertised remedies, but they all failed to cure me. The
cough increased, and before I could lay down in bed at
night I would be seized with a fit of coughing, which
frequently lasted for an hour, during which time I would
expectorate a great quantity of phlegm. I lost flesh
rapidly and my clothes were simply hanging on me like
bags. However, a short time ago, I commenced taking
“ Webber’s VITADATIO,” and, after the second bottle, I
began to improve, and now, after taking six large bottles
of VITADATIO, I feel splendid, and have regained my
loss in weight. lam 14st. again. I cannot express my
gratitude for the recovery of my long-lost health. I
intend taking a few more bottles, so as to make-the cure
thorough and complete, and I am sending you this
testimonial in the hope that other sufferers may be
induced to give your wonderful medicine a trial. —Yours
gratefully', • „
(Signed) W. R. LYONS.
Junee, late of Albury, February 26, 1900.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,
Seatl Office: Clarendon Street N»,
South Melbourne,
Correspondence Invited. Write for Testimonials.

It was hoped N.S.W. Inspector-General
of Police would be left to police duty instead of
sitting.about on a multitude of boards. But when
Mr. Fosbery vacated the chairmanship of the
Public Service Tender Board, last week, Assistant
Inspector-General Garvan was promptly appointed
to the position. The police service is big enough
work for a whole man.
The Sydney organ of black labor is again
braying ferociously that “the claim of che Aus-
tralian Parliament to dictate to a company whose
boats travel but 1000 miles in Australian waters
for every 11,000 in foreign, what kind of labor it
shall employ, is a piece of unparalleled impudence."
But Australia doesn't wish to do anything of the
kind ; all it claims is the right to say with whom
iti will spend its money, and that it will not
employin its mail service ships that are worked
by spider-legged Hindus. Surely that is not
dictating to anybody. Next time the black labor
editor goes to buy a hat, and the shopman passes
him one two sizes too large, presumably he will
buy the thing ; to refuse to buy it would render
him guilty of the unpardonable impudence of dic-
tating to a man who sells thousands of hats to
others and only one to him, what sort of hats he
should sell. Also every civilised Government
does dictate to ships that do not travel more than
3$ miles of their waters that they shall not
employ labor afflicted with plague, small-pox, and
many other unpleasant attributes.

COMMISSIONERS OF SAVINGS BANKS.
FOR ADVANCES DEPARTMENT
(KNOWN AS THE CREDIT FONCIER).
SIXTH ISSUE OF
DEBENTURES FOR £lOO,OOO,
AT 3| PER GENT.
TO MATURE 30th APRIL, 1909.
To be issued under the authority of the Acts of Par-
liament of Victoria, Nos. 1722, and 1778,
CONDITIONS FOR TENDERS.
1. Tenders will be receivable at the Commissioners’
Head Office, 29 Market-street, Melbourne, for the whole
or any portion of the above amount until noon on
MONDAY, 29th June, 1903, when Tenders will be opened
in the presence of- Tenderers or their Agents who may
attend.
2. Each Tender must be sealed, addressed to The
Commissioners of Savings Banks, Melbourne, and
endorsed “Tender for Debentures.”
3. Debentures will be issued in denominations of £lOO,
or £5OO, as mav be desired by successful Tenderers, and
Applicants must state in their Tenders the number and
value of the Debentures they desire to receive.
4. Interest at 3£per cent, per annum will be payable
half-yearly on 30th April and 31st October, the first such
payment’ being due on 30th April, 1904, at the Commis-
sioners’ Head Office, or at any Savings Bank in Victoria.
5. Any Tenders below £9B per £lOO will not be enter-
tained. .
6. Tenderers of. the highest premium will have prefer-
ence, and to Tenderers of even rates, debentures will be
allotted proportionately to the applications, as nearly as
may be practicable. Tenders at a price including a
fraction of a shilling other than sixpence will not be pre-
ferentially accepted by reason of such fraction.
7. Any Tender received from the Savings Banks
Department shall not be allowed to compete with other
Tenders, but all other Tenders shall have preference.
8. Tenders must be accompanied by a deposit in cash,
or by a Bank Draft payable to the Commissioners of
Savings Banks, amounting to 10 per cent, of the face
value of the Debentures applied for, and if the Tender be
accepted in full, or pro rata, the balance must be paid on
or before 31st August, 1903, or may be paid at any earlier
date. Interest at 3£ per cent, per annum will be allowed
on accepted Tenderers’ lodgments of 10 per cent, and
over from date of receipt to 31st October, 1903, and will
be payable up to that date on the issue of the debentures.
9. The deposit of 10 per cent, will be forfeited to arid
become the absolute property of the Commissioners, in
> the event of tljfe balance remaining unpaid after the dates
above specified'.
10. Commission will be allowed, only to Brokers who
are metphertof recognised Stock Exchanges, at the rate
of ten shillings per cent, on the face value of Debentures
for accepted Tenders.
11. Principal will be payable at the Head Office of the
Commissioners in Melbourne.
12. The due payment of these Debentures, and of all
interest thereon, is a charge on all the securities held by
and the funds of the Commissioners, under Division 3 of
Part 1 of the Act No. 1481, and is also guaranteed hy the
Government of Victoria.
13. Copies of the authorising Acts may be inspected,
and Conditions and Form of Tender can be obtained upon
application at the Head Office of the Commissioners or
any of their Savings Banks.
14. By Section 36 of the Act 1481, Section 5 of Act 1722,
and Section 13 of Act 1778 ,any corporate body, Charitable
or Friendly Society, may invest any portion of the funds
or moneys under their control in the purchase of these
Debentures ; and any executors, administrators, or trus-
tees having the power or duty of investing any moneys
may purchase these Debentures with or out of such
moneys (unless forbidden to do so by the Act or instru-
ment under which they act), and every such investment
and purchase shall be deemed a due investment of any
such funds or moneys.
By order of the Board.
GEO. E. EMERY,
Inspector-General of Savings Banks.
4th June, 1903.

The Plain Brown Han in Politics.
At the opposite pole to the “clown in politics
the merry-Andrew with his glozes and his quips,
his insincere merriment and his affectation of good
humor—stands the “plain brown man” who
occasionally finds his way into public life, some-
what after the mysterious fashion in which the
fly gets into the amber. He is a man without
jokes and with a harsh inability to spin out the
unmeaning insincerities which pass current as fine
sentiments on the hustings. After his own fashion
—sometimes a dull and plodding fashion—he aims
at working for his country’s good, and his very
sincerity inflicts on him an unamiable seriousness ;
he knows nothing of the courtship of the
people, of the craft of smiles, the strategy
of flattery. He cannot joke over his prin-
ciples, any more than the devotee can joke
over his religion. Seeing ill in the State, it
puts him in ill mood to compete with the political
clown in the exchange of those small pleasantries
which are so popular and so absolutely useless.
The plain brown man generally gets into politics
more or less by accident. A constituency happens
on occasion not to be wooed by a glib speaker and
returns the quiet man, who has ideas but no
flatteries.
. * * * *
But once in the political arena, once given a
chance to show his mettle, it is astonishing how
quickly the plain brown man begins to establish
for himseff a position of respect. In Parliament
there come numerous occasions when a quip
is of no use, and the finest piece of humbug
does not help things one jot, and then the
plain brown man scores. By-and-bye, when
he attains to Ministerial office, the chances
of displaying his usefulness increase greatly. Ad-
ministrative work calls for sound common-sense
and steady application— not glittering generalities
and artful flatteries. There was never a pound
saved in a national ledger, or a settler put on the
land, or an industry started, or a department
carried through a difficulty, by a joke. A pretty
bit of tumbling, a unique feat of contortionism,
may have a great effect “outside,” but in the
actual governing of the State it is useless and
worse than useless. So, the plain, brown man has
only to be given proper opportunity and his capa-
cities,- which are the capacities the State really
wants for the work of government, begin to
show out in- bold relief—such bold relief that
the electors can't help noticing them. And
the electors, with all their follies, have,
in a community where everybody can read
and write, at bottom a great amount of
common sense. They may hail with delight, and
reward with office, the man who tickles their ears
with flatteries and amuses them with jokes, but
very rarely will they deliberately reject a man of
common sense, tried and proved in administrative
work, for -a clowning opponent; and, if they
should do so, the choice is generally regretted
with promptness, and rectified at the first chance.
As in a recent popular drama the man turns
in time from the garishly-attractive courtesan to
the neat, plain, clean comfort of his lawful wife, so
the elector, if he has a plain brown man of proved
integrity and ability to fall back upon, does not
long wander after the humbug-politician.
* * *
Unfortunately there is not always available
that refuge from the banality of the humbug.
The supply of plain brown men is very limited
indeed. There exists the idea, which has some
foundation in fact, and yet which is on the
whole fallacious, that only the humbug and the
postuter are wanted in politics, and that no other
sort need apply to the electors. Consequently,
the other sort do not apply to anything like a
reasonable extent. They too often leave the
field without a contest,‘without even a challenge,
to blatant insincerity and comic irresponsibility.
So, very frequently, the elector has only the
choice between entertaining and shallow insin-
cerity arid dull, ponderous ignorance; and is not
at all to be blamed for choosing the former.
* * * *
So far as practical experience goes, the plain
brown man contemplating a political career has
really not much to discourage him. It is true
that many politicians have climbed to high places
on no better ladders than those of gab and flattery;
and that, indeed, very many political contests are
decided, not so much by the intrinsic merits of
the candidates as by totally irrelevant and un-
worthy considerations. It is true, also, that the
plain brown man finds it hard at first to get a
hearing, that he must often make some sacrifice
of time and patience before he will be allowed to
give his business-like talents to his country. Still,
whenever the man of this type has came forward,
has surmounted the preliminary difficulties, and
has had some chance of showing his power of
management, his reward has been the continued
confidence of the people ; and the cheap humorist
has not prevailed against him.
* * * *
There have been some conspicuous examples in
Australian politics of the success of the plain
broWn man. Sometimes he has been a very plain
tnan ; sometimes his texture has inclined a little
to the ornamental. But—grouping together men
whose conspicuous qualities were that they relied
on work rather than gab, that they did not
seek power by means of insincerity or hold
it, when attained, with levity—such instances
as Ballance, Kingston and Turner prove that
insincerity, “iloquence” and jocoseness are
not essential to political success. John Ballanoe
held office in Maoriland aided by no graces of ora-
tory, making use of no trickery, sacrificing no
principle, turning no gay and frivolous somersaults
or boneless contortions, simply by his serious,
earnest and high-minded devotion to a democratic
ideal. Kingston had the same experience in
South Australia. Boasting of few of the graces
and none of the hypocrisies of the time-serving
politician, he secured the continued confidence, of
the people by sheer honesty and sturdy devotion
to principle—qualities which now serve him as
well in winning the esteem of a wider electorate.
George Turner in Victoria is a typical
plain brown man, whose greatest glory i 3 the
sneer thrown at him by his enemies that he is
always pursuing with plodding eagerness a half-
crown saying in the national ledger. He came to
the front in Victoria when there was. grave need
for a strictly honest and careful administrator, and
for a man who could see 2s. fid. further off than
any other man could see it; and, having pulled
the State through a most trying time, he was re-
warded with its full confidence, which follows him
into the Federal Cabinet.
**. • *
Indeed, the treatment of the plain brown man
in Australian politics justifies the electorate, when
it is accused of paying too much regard to the
“gift of the gab,” in claiming that it is willing
enough to recognise plain, honest merit, but is
not offered too much of the commodity ; that, if
there is great lack of solid, business-like, prin-
cipled men in Parliament it is through want of
them offering, rather than through the folly of the
ballot-box. At any rate, there is a reasonable
degree of encouragement for men who can’t clown
and can t talk in whirling words about less than
nothing, to go into politics. In the higher ranks
some, with exactly these disqualifications, have
won great honor ; among the juniors men of the
type Of J. C. Watson seem to win their way
rapidly. It is a prevalent idea that entertaining
insincerity has far the best, if not the only, chance
of success in politics. But is that idea a correct
one ? *
Letting Things Slide.
Heaven only knows where Australia got the idea
that it isn’t the customary thing for a country to
try to pay off its debt; but it certainly has that
idea, and hugs it with both arms. And when it
hears by accident of a country that is getting out
of the hands of its Bull-Cohen as rapidly as
possible, it smiles and almost pities that country,
and quizzes its people as at least peculiar. Thus
Robson, M.L.C., of Sydney, who has been to
1 iji, has been telling the Sydney Telegraph the
strange things he observed on the islands, and this
was among them—so strange a thing that the
lelegraph gave it a heading all to itself and
talked about it in its leading columns—“A country
which reduces its debt.” Yet the hard fact is that
there are only one or two disreputable countries
besides Australia which are not trying to pay off
their debts. So absolutely is it part of Great
Britain’s policy to pay off its debt that no period
of peace has passed for a century and a half with-
out a large reduction of the burden. In the few
years between the close of the American War of
Independence and the trouble with Napoleon
.£3,399,724 was wiped off; in the 30 odd
years between Waterloo and the Crimea,
£56,812,695 ; from 1857 down to the end
of last year no less than .£89,233,490. Belgium,
which is in the same position as Australia,
inasmuch as it has no huge military expense,
has a sinking fund of 2\ per cent, per annum.
A very large part of the debt is represented by
railways, and the total interest is now more than
provided by these works. Holland, in the same
way, injikes an annual contribution to its sinking
fund ; since 1850 it has raised £26,575,000 for this
purpose, and its total debt is now only about
.£l6 per head of the population—Australia’s is
£55 17s. 4d. Sweden, whose debt has been
incurred for public works, as Australia’s
has ostensibly been, has recognised that
every public work must fall to pieces some
day, and has accordingly issued no loan without a
sinking-fund provision. The Swedish debt is con-
sequently down to such proportions that the
interest now amounts to 2s. 7d. per head per
annum ; and the railways make about enough
profit to pay that. Norway’s position is much
the same—in 1901 it paid £350,000 in interest and
£130,000 sinking-fund. France has been shock-
ingly handicapped in several ways ; yet in some
of its periods of rest it, too, has wiped off some-
thing, and a provision for sinking-fund is now as
much an annual charge as interest is. And, any-
way, in spite of all its handicaps, France’s annual
interest bill is only £1 4s. 7d. per head, while
Queensland’s is £2 17s. Id., and Australasia’s is
£2 2s. 3d. Even Spain, tired Spain, has in the
past three years raised £5,110,000 more than was
absolutely necessary to pay its creditors their cur-
rent demands. As for the U.S., they faced a debt
of about £580,000,000 in 1866 ; to-day they have a
net debt of just under £200,000,000. About the
only independent country, in fact, which isn’t
paying off its debt is Portugal; and Portugal, in
consequence, will probably be sold up before long.
Even now it is held up in Europe as the Shocking
Example, the nation whose opinion or wish
nobody cares twopence about. Yet, in spite of
all this, Australia goes on hugging the delusion
that it isn’t the ordinary, expected thing for a
country to pay off its debts; and, comforted by
this strange delusion, it goes on leaning up
against a post and letting things slide.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Australian turf people have the notion that
S'Africa is a land of milk and honey, and atop of those
already on the ground and journeying that road, num-
bers more are arranging to go. Hard to see where
inducement comes in. Examination of entrance money
paid and prizes available in connection with recent three
days mfe’etirig at Johannesburg showed former total
£■3124, while latter only aggregated £7450, which means
expenses are excessively high and prize money compara-
tively low. ’Murkan jockey Ames, just back, said it’s
the last place racing men should think of going to
Australia, at its worst, is heaps better for them. Even
Mo, with much the better end of the game, finds things
none too agreeable—he has to pay a registration fee of
£5OO, and often finds big losers non est on settling day ;
still, however, the bejewelled one keeps at it, and waxes
fat, despite his lamentations.
* * * * .
Malvolio—winner of Melbourne Cup 12 years
ago—died last week from kidney trouble. In 1890 he
scooped Oakleigh Handicap (at a difference of 101 b.) from
Highborn by a neck after a hot finish, and Highborn, a
month or so later, ran second to Carbine in Melbourne
Cup. Highborn’s owner vainly offered 1500 guineas for
Malvolio, who was then a 3-year-old. Subsequently
Malvolio annexed Hawksburn Handicap, and, though
weightily supported for Melbourne Cup weeks before,
his failure to get a place in Melbourne Stakes (won by
late George Hill’s Marvel) at same meeting, caused those
behind him to “unload” considerably. But he won all
the, though only by three-parts of a length from
Sir William—and at 100 to 6 offered. Sir W. was well-
backed by Sydney people at long prices, and gained on
Malvolio every stride near the finish. Malvolio never did
any good afterwards. By Malua from Madcap, by Robinson
Crusoe from Giovanna, by Yattendon, he was foaled in
1887. Admiration, who won the Cambria Plate at
Caulfield, Saturday, is half-sister to Malvolio, who was
full brother to that sterling mare, Maluma. Malvolio’s
Melbourne Cup was worth £10,124 independent of bets.
* * * *
Melbourne Cup winner of three years ago—Clean
Sweep—hash’t so far done anything “down under’’
towards recouping his speculative owner. Last month
he ran unplaced in Newmarket Apprentices’ Handicap
(6fur.) among 17 starters, but betting indicated that no
one was in love with the nag’s prospects. Possibly he
may do better later.
* * * •
Aptly named (on appearances) 4-year-old gelding,
Dromedary, was at Canterbury (N.S.W.) on Saturday, re-
sponsible fora very lively scene. Nag ran in Stewards’ Mile,
and after showing prominently over greater part of the
trip, finished nowhere at the end, though heavily backed
by stable connections. Later he shaped in Canterbury
Handicap (1J miles) and, starting at 10 and 12 to 1, won
in hollow fashion ; when the crowd hooted lustily—
particularly the Leger section and clamored for the
complete annihilation and casting into perdition of horse,
jockey, owner and trainer. For a time the row was
awful and after the last race it was renewed. The horse’s
running unquestionably looked bad—such a distinct
reversal has rarely been witnessed in one afternoon,
but the nag won’t stand being kept under pressure
long, and, again, while he carried Bst. in the mile he only
had 6.7 in the other; conditions of the latter race
probably hit his inclinations better. Dromedary’s
. indifferent display in the earlier event discouraged
backers from putting their money on the race won—
hence the fact of the horse starting such an outsider.
Stipendiaries held an inquiry and readily accepted the
explanation offered.
*-* * *
The committee of the Auckland (M.L.) R.C.
nas got itself seriously disliked owing to its failure to
take notice of a riotous, warring crowd when Battleaxe
won the Grand National Hurdles. This tine nag (who
was taken over for the Vic, G.N. last winter) was donkey
licjced on opening day in an inferior field of six. But in a
toff lot of 11 second day (stake £500) the other 10 never had
a hope. The crowd quickly saw this, and hooting actually
started iloirg before Battleaxe came to the last hurdle.
Once over it, there occurred one of the wildest and most
enthusiastic racecourse rows ever recorded in the coun-
try, and that is saying a great deal. Jockey told
stewards that on the first day the horse struck the
second hurdle from home, and thinking he was badly
hurt he didn’t persevere after getting over the final. The
precious stewards found that the prad had a scar all
right—a very usual occurrence—and accepted the
explanation. Then there was more nightmare.

Seventeen runners in AYanda Steeple at
V.A.T.C. meet Saturday had among them one or two
worth noting for the big G.N. ’Chase wherein all but
three of the starters are engaged. Mir.gay was favorite
and Delngoinar, Glenvale, Goldfinder, and Springfield
also had support. Country was too much for Mingay who
cried ago and fell back among the ruck after banging a
few of the jumps hard. At last fence Springfield and
Offender were almost together but former struck the lep
and. though jockey Tomalin pulled him together beauti-
fully, the straight saw Offender streak away and notch 7
lengths best. Winner has 9.5 in G.N. Steeple and should
be hard to beat, but ’tis certain that some of those behind
were only out for an airing. Freedom (third) was running
on at the end in a manner which suggested his prospects
in the coming big plum as worth considering. Offender
is well bred (by Bengal from Apology, by Oudeis from
Romola, by Gemma di Vergy) and first saw light at Berry
Jerry, near Wagga (N.S.W.); he won V.A.T.C. Amateur
Challenge Cup in Aug., 1901.
* * * *
In winning Toolambool Hurdles at Caulfield on
Saturday from 25 others, Marmont squelched the highest
quality field ever stripped for a like event. Rearward
section included high-class leppers Colonel Shillinski,
Lowland Chief, Report, Nansen, Matong, Shoddy, and
Steelrail, as well as Tit for Tat. Pierrot, Quo Vadis, and
other Grand National possibilities. Fact of Marmont
finishing in front with 41b. over and above his G.N.
Hurdles allotment has already brought him into the
position of a very strong tip. My Mistake lined up first
choice (his endorsement being successes in several hurdle
races), and Matong also had a big following. Favorite
was prominent till coming into the straight, when Mar-
mont got to the front and stayed there, winning by three
parts of a/length. Speculation was second and Kipling
third. Marmont hails from Warrnambool district, and is
an aged gelding by Wellington from Lady Gay. He is
asked to hump 9.1 in G.N. Hurdles, and will have no
penalty through Saturday’s success.
* * * *
Recently imported (from M.L.) nag Belmont
won mile handicap trot at last Richmond (V.) meeting in
2.47 3-5 from 35 yards behind scratch, which is a record
for the State, previous best being Emulator’s 2.59. Bel-
mont is a 2yr.-old brother to Almont, and brother in
blood to Dubbo (N.S.W.) crack Fritz.

during the year just closed. Balance-sheet (with a liberal

improvements.

C. Jenkins tops all M.L. winning jockeys’ per-
formances with 83 successes.
* * * *
Horse owners in these days do not seem to hold
the strength of their convictions as firmly as did the men
of old. In the fifties and sixties matches between noted
“ flyers ” were as plentiful as rice at a wedding. To-day
such events happen about as often as a cat voluntarily
goes to sea. As a matter of truth, horse-racing is
becoming less of a “ sport,’’ and more of a fleecing busi-
ness, every day.

Sultan of Johore’s newly-acquired Rapid Pil-
grim went out second favorite for Richmond Handicap
at V.R.O. meet, Saturday, but first choice, Undecided
(starting 3 to I—a point shorter than R.P.), beat the
Malay potentate’s nag by half a head. Rapid Pilgrim is
unfortunate. Heavily backed for V.R.O. Birthday Oup,
t’other day, he ran third, half a head behind second
horse, between whom and winner there was only a head.
* » * *
When the big chronograph was first presented
to the V.R.O. for the information of Flemington race-
goers, it was intended more as a business “ ad.” for the
late Thomas Gaunt, the old-time Bourke-street horolo-
gist, than anything else. Since then it has become an
institution. John Trowbridge, Gaunt’s brother-in-law,
devised the first Flemington chronograph. The most
intricate feature is the extra-fine-toothed intergearing—
the wheels in which have teeth up to 300, of minute
exactness. They consist of three circular layers of
ebonite, ivory, and gun-metal, placed and cut together,
the teeth being so fine as to be barely distinguishable to
the naked eye. These work into similar wheels in the
reverse position, the brass wheel working into the
ebonite, and so on—thus the fifths of seconds are
registered. During the past 25 years the famous
speed-recorder has undergone many mechanical changes
for the better. It is started by electricity on the latest
plan, but its correctness is entirely dependent on the
smartness (and coolness) of the man who sets it going,
and the man who stops it. If the man who starts is
anticipatory at the nerve centre, or the man beside the
judge gets excited over a close finish, the time suffers.
The V.R.O. official timekeeper is only interested in his
“art”—a truly difficult one, requiring a cool nerve,
quick eye, and splendid judgment, and the racing itself
doesn’t interest him in the least.
* * * *
Dual success (Park Stakes and Shorts Handicap)
of 3-year-old filly Lady Brockleigh (Brockleigh—Fly) at
Canterbury (Sydney), Saturday (with crack lightweight
W. H. Smith up), was scored in fine form, and so deci-
sively as to make it worth keeping an eye on her in future
engagements. Lady Brockleigh’s sire was a fine per-
former, and will be remembered as an A.J.O. Epsom
winner.
* » * »
Apropos continued successes and high quality
of nags with Musket strain in them. Not generally
known that before Maoriland Stud Co. got him,
Musket was in Waikato district as a sire for light harness
and carriage horses.
* * * *
Among many things told of recently deceased
Mr. T. S. Hall (Mount Morgan) is that he kept Rock-
hampton Jockey Club going for many years, and made
that body’s meetings the best out of Brisbane. Thirteen
years since, when leaving that centre, Mr. Hall provided
a committee with a big sum of money to arrange and
carry out a week’s amusement for the people of the
place, and they had a high old time, business being
suspended the while. Also said that Mr. Hall liberally
subsidised that big regatta carnival on the Fitzroy in
1888, when Hanlan and Trickett rowed for a valuable
stake.
* • * *
One thing that does much to injure boxing is
the short-sighted managerial policy which requires
people who have already paid admission-money to throw
in coins for the boys who put up the preliminary scraps.
A man who pays ss. for a front seat is now blackmailed—
for it is nothing else—into subscribing to the “ shower ”
or, what is worse still, the hat is taken round to each
individual spectator by the lads who put up the fight.
Considering that dozens of lads jump at the chance of
pounding each other into insensibility for half-a-sovereign
apiece, the meanness of the average boxing management
is beneath contempt.
* * * *
Since bis last fight — beating blacksmith Taylor
—some good judges are tipping Bill Heckenberg as
possible champion on grounds that he improves
with every match and owns a dirty left (also
none too clean a right, though it isn’t as unclean as it
might be). Certainly Scanlan knocked the axeman out
in the second round of their fight, which might happen
to a Jeffries or a Fitzsimmons if he left himself open.
What the onlooking crowd thought of matters up to the
landing of the fatal punch was demonstrated clearly by
the fact that where 5 to 2 was freely offered before hos-
tilities began there was no even money at the conclusion
of the first round. Heckenberg wants to meet black
dude Felix now, and it would be a good match physically,
but the colored man has the greater stock of skill, which
might or might not be balanced by Heckenberg’s superior
force and greater capacity to take thump.
* * * *
Apropos the knock-out punch. In the sedate
pages of the British Medical Journal Dr. James Gray
Duncanson discusses the knock-out blow of pugilism.
He says a flush hit straight on the nose will knock a man
down, but there is not the same collapse as when a man
receives a hard sharp blow on “ the symphysis of the
lower maxiliary bone.’’ He goes on to show that the
knock-out blow invariably puts a man out of court, even
when he is in perfect condition and otherwise going
strong, but is practically never fatal. The blow “sud-
denly twists the muscles of the head and neck, and thus
disturbs the semi-circular canals of the ears, which have
to do with the maintenance of the equilibrium.”
* * * *
“Jab”: A writer in Melbourne Sportsman
contends that boxers nowadays know more about the
game than men who have gone before. Rats! Boxing
is an absolutely lost art these times. Among the hun-
dreds now following the game—to the dozens in evidence
less than 20 years ago—there isn’t a possible Foley, Jack-
son or Griffo. Those who saw Dan Creedon oppose
champion Billy M‘Call, at Sydney National Sporting
Club, were electrified by his skill. Dan is the only one
of those who fought in Australia a dozen or more years
ago still going.
* * » •
Sydney Gaiety A.C. has matched axeman Bill
Heckenberg and elongated pot-hatted black bruiser Felix
to fight five weeks hence. A good match physically, as
both exceed 6ft. and are lengthy-legged and long-reached
Thew and aggressiveness are with the white, and the
black chap will have the bulk of the science and show
most style. If Felix doesn’t settle Heckenberg in the first
few minutes the son of Ham will go perilously near
defeat, for Heckenberg owns a good left now, and has
power and stamina enough to out-fight half-a-dozen like
Felix, who made so poor a show against “ slogger of the
seas” Tom Ball in Sydney. Heckenberg is a better man
than Ball. Success against Felix would put the axe-wielder
on the same plane as champion M’Call, Doherty James
and co.
* * * *
Bantam pug supremacy of the continent was
decided at Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Saturday night, between
slim Queenslander, Mick Raff, and stocky-built Corn-
stalk, Ted Green. Raff showed superior skill, but Green’s
forceful rushes, though dodged and stopped occasionally
were too frequent, and the left and right swings he hurled
on Raff’s ribs, jaw, chin, and stomach were fatal. Raff
stood the banging well till the fourth round saw him
knocked down twice and spread-eagled beyond recovery.
'Twill take a good man to squelch Green.

monwealth. In Coolgardie (W.A.) t’other day a whit*
man was seen “shining” an Afghan’s boots.
• * * *
“ Hammerlock ” : Concerning dead wrestler
Harry Dunn : At 15 he was champion lightweight of his
county (Cumberland) and 16 saw him middleweight
premier. Sixteen vears ago Dunn defeated Englishman
Tom Cannon in Melbourne for the five styles world ■
supremacy. Their two matches were sensational.
Cannon, winning choice of opening style in first, °bose
Graeco-Roman, and, getting the “ garotte ” on early, had
Dunn so limp that he couldn’t go on. Second match saw
Dunn get choice of styles and win with his favorite
(Cumberland) method. Cannon then named Grajco-
Roman. They writhed and wriggled till suddenly the
umpire gave a fall to Dunn, but Cannon .vehemently
objected, and most onlookers protested noisily. Dunn
agreed to go through it again. Cannon was beside him-
self with rage and rushed madly, when Dunn, quick as a
flash, threw his man a flying buttock and “ a fall.’
Cannon kicked again, but it was no use, and, declining
to go further, he forfeited supremacy to Dunn. Deceased
athlete was not quite 40 and a fine sample of physical
manhood till he got on the down grade.

Specialists in language say that Voliva, the
representative in Australia of Dowie with the Purple
Wings, can easily beat John Norton in vituperation.
Couldn’t a match be arranged by one of the clubs for a
big purse, best out of 20 rounds of remarks ?
* » * *
Competition for amateur billiards premiership
of N.S.W. began, at Sydney National S.C., Tuesday last,
and ended Monday, this week. Numerically, entrants
didn’t reach expectations, nor for the most part were
they of particularly high quality. Champions of last and
previous years (Belfield and Rumball respectively) stood
out far ahead of their fellow contestants, and met in the
final (1500 up), Saturday and Monday. First night’s play
saw Belfield, at one stage, 388 to t’other’s 181, but at
half time figures were only 387 to 350 in Belfield’s favor.
Resuming, Belfield seemed to go to pieces, and Rumball
reached the points for the night (750) with an unfinished
59, 148 ahead. Best breaks were : Belfield, 131 and 48;
Rumball, 35, 37, 75, 36, 33, 31, 69, 33, and 59 unfinished.
Resuming Monday, Rumball failed to add to his 59, and
play was depressingly slow for some time. By and bye
Belfield put on a superb break of 129 ; just before this
scores were 960 to 824 against him. Interval saw Rumball
with 1145 (in play) to Belfield’s 1080. Subsequently
Rumball fell off as did Belfield the previous night, and
lastnamed improved considerably—he caught Rumball at
1189 and went right ahead, ultimately winning by 170.
Greatest runs were : Belfield, 40, 129, 30, 41, 71, 56 and
54 ; Rumball, 27, 23, 26, 20, 51, 23 and 23.

The N.S.W. Amateur Athletic Association is a
slow body. On Oct. 6t,h, 1902 —eight months ago—at
Bathurst, W. O’Reilly put up two records (161 b. hammer-
throw to 151 ft. lin., and 161 b. shot 44ft. 3in.), and these
records weren’t passed by the governing body till last
week.
* * * *
Pigeon-shooting matches, as regularly carried
on, are a cruel sport everywhere ; and Governor Cherm-
side’s insult to the sport at Rockhampton should spread
to other quarters. The brute in man now chiefly shows
itself in “ sport.”
» * * *
Rev. Mullineaux, who captained the last Eng-
lish Rugby team to Australia, states that N.S.W. Rugby
Football Union has approached the English authorities re
an English Rugby team visiting Australia ; and the cleric
is also anxious to take a team to Maoriland—provided
the gates, guarantees, etc., are satisfactory. The rev.
gent wasn’t a bad captain, and was a broad-minded
sport. A genuine English Rugby rep. team under his
management would be welcome.
* * * *
M.L. Rugby team to visit Australia next month
comprises 22 men of repute. Backs number 10 (average
weight 11.5) and forwards 12 (average weight 13.4£).
Former, on the whole, do not quite please some Maori-
landers in Sydney, but forwards are voted exceptionally
good, and equal physically to the best M.L. has ever sent
over. Auckland has seven representatives, then come
five Canterbury, four Otago, three Wairarapa, two Wel-
lington, and one Southland. Lightest of backs is 10.4,
and among them are three over 12st. Least weighty of
forwards is 12.6, while five scale 13st. and over, and three
14st. or more. Veteran Duncan (here six years since) is
in. Best of the backs include fore-fronters Kiernan,
Asher (Maori), D. and R. M'Gregor, Wallace, and Wood.
The forwards embrace powerful players Armstrong,
M’Minn, Udy, Tyler, John Spencer, and B. Fanning.
* * * *
The fixtures of the M.L. Rugby football team
have at last been arranged, the first match against
N.S.W. taking place on July 18th. Altogether there are
ten matches in five weeks, including travelling to
Bathurst, Maitland, and through to Brisbane. The pro-
gramme is calculated to knock the visitors out
by the time the representative match against Australia is
held at S.C.G. on August 15. The matches against
Western and Northern districts should be dropped,
and the final match against the Combined Country
team left for the pick of the country teams to
prove their prowess. From the spectator’s point of view
the programme is satisfactory, since there’s a chance of
seeing the visitors play twice a week at least; but it will
require exceptional stamina and real grit to successfully
stand the knocking about consequent upon such a heavy
campaign. The rush for gate receipts has caused the
mulling of what promised to be a most successful visit.
* * * *
So far, there is little between contending Sydney
senior Rugby teams in point of play. Eastern Suburbs is
top of the lot with 13 points to North Sydney’s 11. and
Glebe and Newtown are bracketed 10 each. Past two
Saturdays saw Eastern Suburbs (holding highest position)
account for South Sydney (bottom of the list) by one goal
only each time. Two successive matches between ’Var-
sity and Glebe are similar. Saturday week saw former
triumph (15 to 3), and next meeting (20th inst.) resulted
in favor of Glebe. Though Eastern Suburbs only beat
South Sydney by one goal in each of the two matches
referred to, its prospects for the season’s premiership
are very rosy, while South Sydney hasn’t a hope of
winning.

A sporting ©vent is the egg-laying com™
tition being decided in Adelaide. All the birdf havJbeen
taken to the Reformatory—why hens should be taken m
a Reformatory it is hard to say. The sporting element
comes in with the amazing reversals of form possible in
an event which takes 12 months to decide A schoni
master’s pen of fowls is at present (about quarter-timei
almost out of sight with an immense lead, but other,
with a hitherto almost blank record are doing astonishing
things every week now the cold and wet have set in whi S
the leaders have almost “shut up”; so that anything
may happen before all these hens come into the straight
at the end of next summer for the final sprint home. *
* * * * ’
Australian Waterloo Cup, at Rooty Hill w
week, under N.S.W. National Coursing Association’s
management, proved biggest success the State has
known. Crowds were greater each day, dogs better and
more representative, and there were bigger “books” on
the outcome—volume of betting was a record. Victorian
champions—The Hermit, Gold Bar, Army Girl, Leontine
Tribune—and those dividers (little sisters Verse and
Planet) of a previous Waterloo Cup were engaged ■ and
heavily-backed N.S.W. top-notchers Great Heart (winner
of N.S.W. Derby and Challenge Stakes, 128 dogs last
year, and Orange Cup this season), We Two (winner of
last Champion Cup), Rowland (runner-up to Great Heart
in Orange Cup), and crack of three years past, Braddon’a
Blot, also competed. Ultimate winner, Kilronan waa
among the outsiders at the reading of the card before
opening round, and, on night prior to concliidino- stages
when only three were left in, he was the outsider •Mo
laying even money Great Heart, 7 to 4 Gold Bar,’and
offered 5 and 6 to 1 against Kilronan. People in the
know who backed the dog early, worked off some, or all
of their coin before the finish, yet Kilronan beat every!
thing opposed to him handsomely.
**' * *
Kilronan, a big though not too impressive
second season dog by Kilmainham-Flora, was bred by
an Eastern (N.S.W.) District coursing enthusiast, who
gave him to a Gatton (Q.) priest, the present owner
His sire scooped the 1889 Waterloo Cup, and was by
Parnell from Kilmorna, and also a second season dog at
the time of his success. Speed, cleverness, and stamina
of the sire are conspicuous in the son, and were all
needed for the company he met. Kilronan’was brought
straight through from Q. to Sydney, arriving last Friday
—five days before he had to run—and after travelling 800
miles in the dog box of the train without stops. Trainer
Keighrey, who handled Kilronan, has had a dog in each
of three Waterloo Cups: first (Drinan Dhu>was among
last 4 of 64, second (Kilmainham) won, and third
(Kilronan) also got away with the prize.
* * * »
Arrangements have now been completed by the
Dunlop Tyre Co. as regards the Westralian test rime to
select the representative for the big Victorian event, to
be held on August 22nd, over the Warrnambool course.
The W.A. race will be over a course of 116 miles between
Beverley and Perth, and will take place about the 25th of
July or the first Saturday in August. The last race held
over this course was won by ex-Victorian A. Jewell, off
scratch. The rider establishing fastest time over the
Beverley-Perth course will have his expenses paid by the
Dunlop Co. to Victoria.
* * * *
First heat (Saturday) of Dunlop Co.’s supremacy
under auspices of N.S.W. Cyclists Union brought 15
competitors (representing three clubs) with 12 pacers.
Course was 22£m. Favorites (Redfern) won easily
(through Maidment) with northern suburbs next anil
Manly last. Hundred yards from finish Roose looked a
sure winner but he fell and broke his machine. Of first
six, four were Redfern men. Altogether nine clubs have
entered ; other six will figure in heats next month.
**** . „ i
The Sydney “Thousand” bike race, which
caused so much excitement last year, threatens to verge
into an annual reality. The date will be altered from
March to January, so that English, American and Con-
tinental riders, who may compete, will have plenty of
time to get back for their own racing season.' The syndi-
cate which controlled last year’s event—“ the Summer
Nights Amusement Committee,” whatever it mav be—
has the matter in hand, but it is to be hoped that the
management will be better, and that oversea visitors
will be firmly told that, because they came a long way
to take part, they aren’t going to run the show—a
state of things which has been allowed for a long time in
Australia.
* * * *
League of Wheelmen trouble now looks like
being amicably settled. Police-court action against
secretary Branagan for “breaking and entering and
stealing ” where and what, as secretary, he had a right
to, ended in smoke ; proceedings were withdrawn, and
the question of ownership of property, &c., is to be
decided by arbitration. Outcome will be an appeal to
the general body of members in th<B form of a fresh
election.

Re “Ike” (6/6/’O3) on short rowing boats.
Inventor Chris. Neilson says he is satisfied that it wasn’t
the boat that beat N.S.W.’screw; and the fittings were not
his. It is only a matter of time when the good qualities
of short eights will be recognised. It took years to satisfy
rowing men that the short sculling boat was the thing.

Horace Tozer, Queensland Agent-General,
says that he was compelled by the oppressive
Immigration Restriction Aot to intimate to six
men, engaged to go to Charters Towers on account
of their “ special expert knowledge ” in the matter
of round brick shafts, that they wouldn’t be
admitted to Australia, and, therefore, there being
no expert knowledge available in Australia, the
shafts won’t be constructed. As the law specially
provides that men of the kind Tozer describes
shall be admitted, it looks as if Horace was, in
this case, an especially pernicious and addle-
headed ass. But Tozer’s salary and billet depend
on the Queensland Government, and just now any
slander or misrepresentation of the Common-
wealth authority tends to make Tozer’s billet
more secure.
(Rev.) J. N. WILLIAMS, Tarentum, Allegheny
Co., Pa., writes:
“ Thirty years ago a physician told me I would soon die
of consumption. I commenced to use Jayne’s Expector-
ant whenever troubled with colds or hoarseness, and it
has NEVER FAILED to bring relief.”*
Ernest Brodribb, the turf plunger of a few
years back, who died in England lately, didn’t
have to toil for the money which he “played up.’ ?
The Brodribbs had a comparatively small sheep
run in the dry, useless and horrible Far West
(N.S.W.) when Broken Hill was discovered ; and
like most of the other squatters and managers
they got a large leg-in—they were the only people
on the spot with cash to buy syndicate shares.
The Brodribbs’ holding, Poolamacca, was handiest
to Broken Hill, so when the first smelting
furnace was blown in it was Mrs. Brodribb who
“performed the ceremony.” The late W. R.
Wilson, who established St. Albans, was set on
his turf career the same way ; likewise David
James, winner of a few of Australia’s biggest
events. But James wasn’t a squatter ;he was a
teamster and station tank-sinker.
Careful and unprejudiced inquiry in the realm
of modern medical science shows there is no better
curative and cleansing agent than the Thermal Bath.
The “Century” Thermal, sold by Mr. A. Troup, of
Royal Arcade, Melb., has already had its praises sung by
the medical faculty, and hundreds of grateful beneficiares.
And the number of grateful ones increases rapidly, for
whereas the general public in the old days merely talked
casually of Turkish baths from hearsay, every person who
has experienced the inexpensive domestic luxury of a
Thermal Bath becomes an enthusiastic advocate thereof.
Mr. Troup will send pamphlets and testimonials on
application.*
“ D.” writes from Camden (N.S.W.) :
Lieutenant Col. Onslow, of Camden Park, Camden,
writes to the Sydney Telegraph (2/6/’O3) stating that
"one” of the six branches of the “Reform” League in
the Camden electorate had asked him to accept nomina-
tion as its candidate, and he had replied that he would
contest the seat if selected by all the branches of the
league in the electorate. The Macarthur Onslow holds a
great Government grant in the electorate, extending
from Menangle to Camden, and about 150 voters are serfs
on the estate, or small tenants. Further, the neighbor-
ing towns of Camden (where the Onslow is Mayor) and
Menangle bow down in the dust when one of the family
passes by. The parson is dependent on the Onslows, so
are the storekeepers, postmaster, police, and the inspec-
tor of nuisances. Also the publicans, kept by the wages
of Onslow’s serfs, must “barrack” for him. So he
tells Camden and the five branches of the league that
have not yet humbly addressed him that he' is ready to
be humbly addressed. The representative of six square
miles of country, granted to his grandfather by an idiotic
Governor, where the workers lift their hats and rush to
open the gates as his carriage dashes past—he is ready to
stand as the People’s Reform candidate.

Bishop Webber, broken down and seeking
a coadjutor, has practically killed himself over the
building of a colossal cathedral in Brisbane. It
was in his thoughts morning, noon, and night. If
he dined with friends, or friends with him, the
burden of talk was the cathedral. In hotels,
he was as bad. Sometimes, when paying an hotel
bill, he would ask Boniface to refund a guinea
“for the cathedral.” He went to England and
dunned everybody. He returned to Queensland
as anxious as Again he started for England
and dunned afresh. When Queen Victoria died,
he called on tired Queensland and the rest of the
world to build his cathedral as a memorial. When
the Duke of York visited Australia he induced
the young man to lay a stone, and again shouted
to tired Queensland. Responses were slow and
small. The Bishop is a bachelor —which may
partly account for the monomania of the cathe-
dral.

The Sydney Sunday Retired Grocer .con-
tinues to bray about the exodus from Australia
owing to Labor legislation.. That there is an
exodus of landless farmers from Australia is l true
—they can’t get land worth having until the big
estates are cut up. But the Sunday rag professes
to believe that Australian farmers are flying' from
Australia because they can’t afford to pay l the
remuneration demanded by the Minimum Wage
Act and Arbitration Courts. The Sunday organ
apparently doesn’t know that neither Wages
Board in Victoria nor Arbitration Court in
N.S.W. has ever interfered with the wages paid
to agricultural laborers. Recently, when a squad
of Victorian farmers went to look for land in. Ta-
smania another paper, very like the Sydney
Retired Grocer (Launceston Examiner), gravely
assured the community that they were dri' >- out
by the minimum wage and the land-tax. Th are
never was a legal agricultural minimum wage,
and no Victorian with less than (40 acres, pays
any land-tax, but the good old - erviceable lie
came in just the same.

“R.S.A.” : Had a bad attack of diphtheria
once. The doctor gave me but small hopes of
recovering, and prescribed an evil-smelling gargle,
to be used frequently to dislodge the black phlegm
in the throat. This gargle smelt worse than a
decayed snake. To save argument I poured a
little of the vile stuff away regularly, and, in
place of it, used ginger ale, which always loosened
the phlegm and gave me great relief. The doctor
said the gargle saved my life.

THE KIDNEYS ARE THE CLEANSERS
For a person to enjoy good health it is essential that the blood be kept free from all contamina-
tion. It must be purified by the kidneys and liver. As the blood flows through the veins it becomes
laden with certain matter which has been used up by the tissues. When the blood, in the course of
its circulation, reaches the kidneys, the kidneys extract this waste matter and cause its removal from
the system. If the kidneys, from weakness or disease, are working feebly, a portion of this waste
matter remains in the blood and becomes actively poisonous. The kidneys of the average individual
filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine in twenty-four hours. In this quantity of
urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid, together
with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. These
are all solid substances, but they pass from the body in complete solution if the kidneys are working
efficiently. It is easy to realise the important functions which the kidneys perform. Unless the solid
substances are removed suffering must ensue. In fact, to the retention of these urinary poisons in the
system nine-tenths of all pain and sickness is due. Sometimes minute crystals of uric acid form
in the blood and are deposited in the joints and tissues of the body, thus causing the acute pa.n felt
when we have
RHEUMATISM, GOUT,
Lumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, &c. Sometimes the uric acid crystals, aggregating, together
in the kidneys, form Gravel and Stones, and cause Kidney Colic, and Irritation, and
Inflammation of the Bladder. When the kidneys are working perfectly we experience none
of these pains and disorders, as the causative poisons are then duly removed in a natural manner.
WARNER’S SAFE CURE
is an unfailing specific for all disorders of the kidneys and liver. It acts directly on those vital organ
in such a manner as to always restore them to vigorous health. So intimate is the relation between
the functions of the kidneys and those of the liver that it is essential, in order to cure any disorder of
either, that an agent should be employed which possesses an equally curative action, upon each. In
other words, when the kidneys are sick the liver is sick also, and the contrary is likewise true. The
supreme power possessed by Warner’s Safe Cure in the treatment of diseases of so, apparently,
diverse nature as Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, Gravel. Stone,
Bladder Troubles, Blood Disorders, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Head-
ache, Anaemia and Debility, is entirely due to its ability to restore to health and robust activity
both the kidneys and the liver, when all urinary and biliary poisons are duly expelled from the system
as required by nature. The blood is then clean and we cannot suffer, as the cause of suffering is
removed.
The most common symptom of kidney disease is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A surer test is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and
let it stand until the next morning. If it is then cloudy or there is a brick-dust like sediment, if
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural colour, then the kidneys are not healthy and
Warner’s Safe Cure should be instantly resorted to or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes or some less
serious but more painful illness will surely result.
Mr. F. Elliott, of 52 Surrey Street, Darlinghurst, N.S.W., says:—

to all the principal doctors in Sydney, Melbourne and New Zealand, and they all gave my case up as
hopeless. I got thin and lost my appetite. I kept getting worse every day, and often wished that I
could die. One day I read one of your books, and decided to try the effect of Warner’s Safe Cure in
my own case. I began to feel better after taking the first bottle. My appetite came back, and I
began to put on flesh. I took six bottles in all, and am now quite well.”

On Our Selection.” By “Steele Rudd
Price, 6a., Postage Bd., from The Bulletin Newspaper Co. Ltd., Sydney.
Most of the Tory papers complain that the Labor
party has never attempted to show how reduction
of members (of the Assembly) could possibly, as
the party alleges, be opposed to Labor representa-
tion. With one man one vote, these papers
allege, the party would have just as large a
representation, proportionately, as ever. The
Tory press knows well, though, that if the
Assembly were reduced to 60 members, there
would be a far poorer chance of abolishing the
now unnecessary Second Chamber. The gag
would be that, in view of the reduced membership
of the People’s House, we couldn’t be so dead-
sure that the House really represented the people;
also, as the quorum would be reduced, that a
mere handful of members might commit the
country to some extravagance. That is one
reason why the Labor party doesn’t howl with
the Telegraph and Joseph Carruthers, and
George Pile, and H. 8. Badgery, and the other
conspicuous “Reformers.” The Labor party
reckons that the number of members of Parlia-
ment should be reduced by reducing those who
don t represent anybody or anything—the mem-
bers who are mostly in Parliament because they
happened to be friendly with some dead-and-gone
politician, who himself would not now be able to
get into Parliament if he were alive and wanted
to get there. The Labor party wants to get rid
of those members first; and in so doing, it is
more of a Reduction party than its opponents.
4.
A North Queensland correspondent:—
Two of the central sugar mills erected under the Q.
Sugar Works Act were taken over by the Government
under the provisions of this Act. Notwithstanding the
sympathy of the Government staff appointed to manage
these concerns towards the cane-suppliers of the mills,
this is how the pro-nigger Queensland Government
penalises white labor at Proserpine (near Bowen). It is
necessary to keep the price of cane somewhat below
current rates in order to make good some of the financial
leeway that this mill company lost prior to Government
control, and consequently the assistance of the Federal
rebate on white-labor cane was hailed with delight by
the majority of the cane-suppliers. But those who used
white labor incurred the displeasure of the Philp Minis-
try. Assistance hitherto given was withheld, and those
who were obliged to finance on their crops to do
necessary replanting work were compelled to deal
with a local bank and pay 8 per cent, interest for accom-
modation. This is in marked contrast to the treatment
of colored labor cane-growers, who are encouraged to
employ black labor in this fashion. A colored gang is
sent to clear these people’s lands, fence them, burn off
the timber, plant the cane, weed it, and harvest the crop
when mature, the settlers receiving the net proceeds of
these colored labor speculations without doing an ounce
of work themselves. Furthermore, no interest has been
charged on the sums disbursed for these speculations,
although the amounts have, in some cases, stood for nearly
two years. About £lO,OOO has already been advanced
from the Queensland Treasury for bolstering up black
labor. These are the sort of tactics behind much of the
paraded antipathy of certain N.Q. cane growers towards
white labor under the conditions of the rebate system,
and further developments are worth watching.
♦
Said that King Ned’s visit to Portugal was to
arrange some sort of a British protectorate over
the little country, which is hopelessly in debt to
the London money-lenders, and probably must
soon pawn its nationhood. Why Portugal has
reached that state is explained by a writer in a
London daily :
Little, if anything, in Portugal that means anything is
Portuguese. The country produces little of what it
needs, and its natural sources of revenue have been sold,
as have its colonies, to foreign concessionaires. I am
living in a typical Portuguese hotel, where the only
Portuguese thing is the language. The cutlery is from
Sheffield or Solingen. The crockery is French. The
chromos on the walls are American. The bedsteads are
German. The curtains are Nottingham lace. The oil-
cloth is Yankee. The wooden toothpicks, which every-
one uses assiduously and obtrusively after each course,
are obviously Yankee, too. The pillar-box in which I shall
post this was made in Derby. The street car in which I
shall ride home was made in St. Louis ; and the fare I
Eay will go mostly to the English company that runs
isbon’s admirable street-car system. The country buys
everything, and has nothing to sell. And having long
since paid out all its capital of natural resources, it is
naturally heavily in debt to everyone.
A dreadful warning to Australia, whose George
Reid-Pumpkin-Smith party would urge it on that
same road to ruin.
