A Maoriland correspondent:—
It grows more and more plain that politics in this
State will ere long degenerate into a protracted squabble
between the labor organisations and the alleged Farmers’
Union. At the various conferences of the latter body
held recently, resolutions were passed declaring uncom-
promising war on Skddon’s proposal to grant legislative
preference to unionists under the Conciliation Act, and
the sacred right to acquire the freehold was declared to
be the inalienable privilege of every Britisher. One of
the conferences opened with a resolution declaring belief
in the existence of God, and a bucolic orator—a Captain
Something — urged farmers to trust in God and fight for
the freehold. These antediluvian reactionaries know
well that preference to union laborers means the more
rapid multiplication of labor organisations, and hence
the greater will be the opportunity for the dull agricultural
laborer to become infected with a knowledge of his rights
as a human being—the very thing the Tory gang doesn’t
want him to do. By “ freehold,” of course, is meant the
“fee-simple”—in other words, the right of the man
with money to buy up all available space, leaving the poor
wretch who depends on his hands alone the helpless tool
of the arrogant “owners” of that earth which some
people call “the Lord’s,” but which, according to the
system in vogue, belongs to the man with the greatest
capacity to grab. The landowner is mostly pious-
in a certain way—and says the earth is the Lord’s,
but all the same his hunger after a deed setting forth
that the Lord’s earth belongs to Hon. John Smith is
amazing. And when he gets the Lord’s earth the
promptitude with which he puts up a notice that that
which was made in the Lord’s image will be prosecuted
if it doesn’t keep off the grass is amazing also.
♦
Melbourne and Sydney pay their town clerks
£lOOO a year each or thereabouts ; Glasgow pays
its clerk £3500, but then Glasgow is a ‘ city,’ not
merely the central block of one. Glasgow’s
Council began its progressive career in ’66, and
to-day employs over 10,000 people. It owns its
tramways, ferries, steamboat services, and
hydraulic power system ; runs a People’s Palace,
winter garden, concert hall, besides museums,
libraries and art galleries. Owns its own Botanic
Gardens, 11 large parks and numerous smaller
gardens—and doesn’t cadge Government grants
to keep them up. Last year its Parks Depart-
ment spent £76,356, levying its usual 3fd rate
for that special purpose. Also Glasgow’s
citizens own lodging-houses, artisans’ dwel-
lings, and the systems of electric lighting,
gas, telephones and water-supply. Its town clerk,
with nearly twice George Turner’s salary,
looks after the spending of nearly a million, and
has, besides the usual assistants, a corporation
chemist, a bacteriologist, managers of tramways,
gas, &c., a superintendent of the cleansing depart-
ment, a manager of the City Improvement Trust,
and a superintendent of the labor bureau. Mel-
bourne and other Australian cities worry along in
disorganised, happy-go-lucky fashion with cheap
men—just as Australia pays its Treasurer-in-Chief
a paltry £l9OO a year, and expects to get served
capably. The Commonwealth is lucky in its
Treasurer just now, but luck like that won’t
always last.

THE BULLETIN STORY BOOR:
Price 4s fid, postage fid.
Ivanhoe (W.A.) ia hard at work on improve-
ments, which should result in a very material reduction
in costs. All the hauling will be done through one shaft.
It is claimed that the present plant extracts 85 per cent,
of the gold contents, and that it would cost more than it
is worth to extract the balance. The diamond drill
boring from the 1600 ft. level of the Great Boulder has
been withdrawn, and it may be taken for granted that it
has been a complete failure. Of the six lodes found in
the upper levels only one is reported as having been
found in the bore, and that showed only lldwt-. ore. Had
the bore been a success, the result would have been
trumpeted far and wide, and the secrecy now displayed
will have a very damaging effect on the market. The
mine is now earning at the rate of about 245. a share
yearly, and, in future, commencing with July, divs. of
ss. quarterly will be paid, with a probable bonus at the
year’s end. With such a prospect, were it not for the
failure of the diamond drill at 1600 ft., the shares would
look tempting.
. * * * *
; ’ Associated Financial Corporation, run by that
wonderful wizard, Horatio Bottomley, has been recon-
structed, with a capital of £1,000,000 in ss. shares. Co.
had previously undergone some half-a-dozen reconstruc-
tions, and has always been a complete failure; but
Bottomley appears, and the public comes to his cry
df “ pay, pay, pay.” Bottomley still reckons that he
can get divs. from that 10-year-old wild cat, Associated
Sit. Jackson, Southern Cross, and the equally hopeless
Trafalgar, Ealgoorlie.
* • * •
North Kalgurli (W.A.) will shortly start crush-
ing with a new 10-head mill ; manager Nutman hopes by
the end of July. The mine has been more or less opened
up by eleven levels, and there is enough 15dwt. ore in
Sight to keep a 10-head mill going for at least two years.
The manager is very favorably known on the field for his
economical-handling of the Mt. Charlotte, and, with a
much more up-to-date plant, his working costs should be
low. The great drawback is that the lodes are much
disturbed and patchy. The final call of 6d. was payable
22nd inst. Co. is in 350,000 shares of 10s., so it will have
to show very good results to justify any very high quote.
**» ‘ *
Princess Royal (Norseman, W.A.) continues its
successful career, its June div. of Is. bringing up the
total distributed to £BB,OOO, or 110 per cent, on its
nominal capital. Mine continues to open up satisfac-
torily, but, unfortunately, the lode underlays into the
adjoining Princess Royal Central at 390 ft., so that, when
tpe three levels now being worked are depleted, the mine
will be played out. Under the circumstances, the best
thing to be done is to amalgamate, as the Bull syndicate,
which was trying to acquire both, intended doing.

£87,500, Oroya - Brown Hill £40,500, Sons of Gwaiia
£31,800, and Princess Royal £4OOO.

m Briseis: The statement by Messrs. Lake and
Qprrie, English,engineers sent out to report on the pro-
perty, fell like a bombshell in the camp, and fully
accounts for the tremendous slump in the value of shares
within the past three months. It also fully confirms
Bulletin’s consistent representation that the property
his always been grossly overvalued. The report is ail
the more significant from the fact that Mr. Currie, who
reported on the mine in November, 1899, has been com-
pelled to reduce his original estimate of 4,500,000 cubic
yards of wash to 2,700,000 ; and, further, he admits that
his former estimate of the tin contents of the drift of
frthn f per cent, to 1 per cent, per cubic yard is “ greatly
in excess of what wili be actually produced,” but he de-
clines to commit himself to a new estimate of the
quantity of tinin.the mine. A qualifying sentence in
the report' is still more significant: “If the above scheme
cstn be carried out the future working of the mines will
bp considerably simplified. That it is possible to carry out
the scheme has been proved by previous work, but it will
b'4 a most anxious time for the management, and the
position will require to be carefully considered from
ifionth to month.” Shareholders who read between the
lines of this report will find very poor consolation in it.
4- * » ■ * *
, Two ’Murkan oil people, K. R. Moure and C.
Fair, are reporting on the Coorong (S.A.) oil country for
the Melb. Co. which has secured 240 Square miles under
■license. Moure represents Douglas Lacey, a still bigger
Massachusetts oil-man. The supposed oil country is the
low-lying swamp land south-east of the Murray mouth.
The outward sign of the possible inward presence of oil
is an inflammable, elastic substance which has oozed
from the swamps, and filled the holes. A bore was put
down 332 ft. 10 years ago, but nothing was got beyond the
usual “indications,” and the appliances were not then
available to go any further. The ’Murkans report that,
on the presence of the inflammable ooze alone, deep
boring is worth while.
* » • *
A British syndicate has started to work the old
North Cornish of Daylesford (Vic.) with an up-to-date
mining plant'and, it is said, plenty of capital. The mine
has been shut down for years, and was always a stringer
in the matter of dividends. The North Cornish was the
last big mine to work the Daylesford leads, and great
things are expected from the old ground.
■L * * * •
Bendigo established a new record last week in
a highly payable crushing from 3310 ft.—the deepest
point at which payable gold has been won on the field.
The crushing—3o tons for 21oz.—came from Lansell's
180 mine. New Chum Victoria, adjoining, also had a
payable crushing from nearly the same depth. New
Chum Railway, about a miie to the south of these
mines, is passing through spurry country at a
depth of over 3800 ft., and in some of the spurs coarse
gold is showing. This is quite an unexpected dis-
covery ; it appears to be an entirely distinct formation
from any previously worked in the mine. : Altogether
Bendigo’s prospects in the deep ground are better than
they ever were before, and it looks almost like a certainty
that it will not be long before the New Chum line, will
again assert itself as the premier producing formation of
the district.
* * * *
jubilee mine, Scarsdale, near Ballarat, during
past 2J- years has produced 24,0000 z. of gold, paid £36,000
in dividends and spent £lO,OOO on machinery. The
original outlay was £9OOO, and the profit on this has been
£46,000. This is the mine which the Mines Selection Co.
ighominiously rejected, whereupon its Melbourne repre-
sentative, H. J. -Daley, took it in hand personally, and is
to be credited largely with the success which has been
achieved. Prospects look hopeful for a long continuance
of satisfactory results.

Berry Consols Extended, Smeaton (Vic.), made
a profit of £8748 in its half-year to May 31. Of this £BOOO
has been paid in divs., and, with the balance brought
forward, there is £1699 left. The total paid by this co. in
divs. to date is £49,000 ;in wages £180,926. Present
prospects of the mine are unusually good. Costs have
also been reduced, partly through the recent introduc-
tion of a system of electric haulage.
Horatio Bottomley recently told the share-
holders in a Loddon (Vic.) Deep Lead co. that it was re-
ported on the authority of a man “well-known out
there,” that there were 965,000 fathoms of washdirt
available, worth loz. of gold per fathom, which, added
the great financier, “means loz. jer ton.” An area
6x 6 x 6ft. represents a mighty big ton. Divs. amount-
ing to over £1,000,000 were promised, the first to be de-
clared in August next. Writer will wager that August,
1904, will come before the first div. is paidr“
* * * *
Paid-up Queen Centrals (Charters Towers) have
lately sold briskly at 265; and over, on the strength of the
probability of the straight shaft cutting the lode worked
in the Queen Cross. Less than three years ago shares'
stood at about 35., and the mine was among the despised
shows known locally as “tripe,” and nothing in the
Central ground has had anything to do with lifting the
stock into its present position—A good instance of a -
number of mines working on each other’s boundaries, as
in The Towers, where a good development in one means
renewed vigor and enhanced values for perhaps a dozen
others.
‘ i.#- » * *
jLL. West Coast stilj has hopes of tapping a
petroleum supply. The Kotuka Oil Co., boring outside
Greymouth, lately tapped a flow of gas, which was
followed by a rush of sludge. For some days the flow
spurted 60ft. high: it “resembled ground pumice
soaked in kerosene and mixed with water."
Ex-Govt. Geologist Murray, of Vic., has made
another inspection of the silver-lead and copper country
120 miles or so south of Burketown, in the Queensland
Gulf country. Most of the shows are held by a Sydney
co., which doesn’t seem to have done anything except
try to sell them to J. Bull. As, however, Bull would be
required to put up enough money to build 126 miles
of railway, besides doing some harbor work at the port
of shipment, he has taken two years to think over it, and
it still thinking. Mr. Murray describes large bodies
containing ore, mostly galena ; the weak point is that he
apparently did not take samples, and gives no idea of the
value of the stuff. Of one show he says that 400,000 tons
of ore may be said to be proved ; but as to quality all he
says is—“ From what I can learn there have been less
than 1000 tons of ore altogether sent from this mine for
treatment, the results varying from 60 per cent, to 80 per
cent, lead and 28oz. to 50oz. silver.” The investor will
probably want something much more definite than “ from
what I can learn.
* * * »
The latest Westralian bubble has been pricked
more suddenly than usual. The alleged find was made
about 50 miles south-east of Kalgoorlie ; “ gold-studded
stone, the richest ever found in the State,” was reported,
the place was christened Randell’s (Dr. Randell, M.L.G.,
is one of the backers of Pleyel, who pegged the first
blocks), and a rush started. Mines-inspector Jenkins,
sent off at once, found that no alluvial had been got, that
the country had been well overhauled by dry-blowers and
deserted, and that “ the place is fit for prospecting only.”
♦ * * *
A new game has sprung up in Victoria since
the Factories Act compelled dressmakers to pay their
apprentices at least half-a-crown a week. They now
advertise “dressmaking classes,” and the independent
young lady goes to learn at nothing a week, and so keeps
her poorer sister out of the running.
* * » *
The present sugar season on the Tweed River
(N.S.W.) promises to be a record one. The growth of
cane throughout the district is described as something
the like of which has never been seen there since the first
farm was hewn out of the dense jungle known as the
“Big Scrub.” Between 70,000 and 80,000 tons of cane
will be put through Oondong mill alone, which starts
crushing in July.
* * * *
There is something in the complaint of Labor-
member Nielsen, with regard to N.S.W. mining wardens.
Until a few years ago the Mines Department had a staff
of wardens, who also did police-magistrate’s work. Most
times they didn’t know much about general law, but they
did know the mining law—several of them had helped to
make it. They administered that well, and fined the
drunk at random. But nowadays the Mines Department
hasn’t any such staff ; the P.M.’s act as Wardens, and
while they fine the drunk in a strictly legal style, the
shockingly intricate mining law is administered in a very
casual way.
* * * »
“T.D.”: The best accountant, probably, in
Victoria, who happens to be in the Iceberg service, was
once visiting a farmer down Gippsland way, and was
made use of by the man on the land to fix up his income-
tax schedule, “ because it was so difficult to understand.”
The figures staggered him, especially when the “ poor
farmer,” during the evening, stai ted the characteristic
whine about the woe and unprofitableness of his lot.
“Why,” he remonstrated, “you made £lOOO profit;
Surely that was good enough ? ” The farmer objected
that £6OO had been spent on new machinery and new
implements, which left him only £400.. “Of course
you’ve deducted the £600,” he remarked. The public
servant said he had done nothing of the sort, carefully
explained to the poor farmer that his £6OO had been clear
profit, however he chose to spend it—that it was still an
asset ; and that a whining agricultural hypocrite, who
no doubt complained bitterly about the extravagant
treatment of leading public servants, who didn’t average
one-fifth of his income, was a curse to hi» '•ountry, any-
way. That year one farmer’s schedule at least was made
up properly.
Brisbane Courier shows signs of coming
back to its old love and the flesh-pots ; but the
Boodle wraith is going to run its paper next July,
fearing, perhaps, that the morning journal might
throw another somersault at an awkward moment.
Cardinal Moran’s South Sea Islands tour
has been abandoned—ostensibly because of an
outbreak of measles at the Islands, really because
there was no marked rush of excursionists to join
in the trip, for which it was proposed to charter a
special steamer. The difficulties of blending a
convivial whisky-and-water holiday with a solemn
holy-water enterprise were too great.
Dr. M‘Carthy’s “rain-making” para-
phernalia, with which he is undertaking to flood
some of the droughty inland parts for a trifling
consideration, consists of a machine to send up,
“from three centres, a column of gas made by
certain chemicals.” The rain-fakir says that “ the
gas causes in the different strata of the atmosphere
a vacuum, which is the centre of a heat storm and
is filled by the dense clouds that contain the rain.”
But in a great many parts of the interior dense
clouds to fill a vacuum with are almost as rare as
the conversion of the devil.
The N.S.W. Government proposes to put
its referendum as to reduction of members of the
Assembly somewhat in this way:
Are you in favor of
125 members
100
90 „
80 „
70
Indicate by the figure 1 your first choice, by the figure
2 your second, and so on.
Then the votes will be calculated on the Hare
preferential system. Those who wish to vote
against reduction will do so by voting for 125
members.

Matter arousing intense dissatisfaction here.

State. Again strongly urge permission to land.
Received your wire this morning.
The shadow of Cohen over our politics.
The Bulletin receives a printed docu-
ment signed by Senator Zeal of Victoria, in which
that gentleman grieves over the proposal to
establish a High Court on account of the expense,
and compares the cost of our judicial system with
the cheap Canadian methods. The comparison
is somewhat erratic as it doesn’t take full account
of the Canadian provincial expenditure, but that
may pass. Australia can’t do better than follow
Canada’s example—by concentrating most of the
business in the Federal High Court and cutting
down the State establishments, by abolishing the
State Agents-General, by wiping out the Upper
Houses of the State Parliaments, and by sticking
on a 50 per cent. Protective tariff.
Few men have a better knowledge of the
working of the Lands Acts than has Land Agent
W. N. Willis, of 21 Bligh-street. Payment by
results is not only reasonable but also novel.*

pared by two of Sydney's leading Accountants Mr
William Robertson and Mr. Wilfrid Johnson, of Yaralla

which is generally foisted upon the public.*
At Poverty Point.
«* Women are all actresses. What makes actresses so
fascinating and attractive to men is that they are women
twice over.” —Max O’Rell.
The statements sown broadcast by strong man Sandow,
athlete and advertiser, that he was a puny, sickly school-
boy at a continental college, and owed his marvellous
development solely to his system, created considerable
amusement among some of his fellow-countrymen in
Melbourne. They aver that he spent his youth and early
manhood as a brawny individual, earning an honest
livelihood as a lumper on the Hamburg wharves, hence
these thews. They go into raptures over his strength,
but over his system they don’t seem to rapture to any
extent worth mentioning.
“ Spring ” : What a number of sterling actors end their
days at Poverty Point! The drought-stricken sheep in
the “ Breaking of the Drought ” were bought by a butcher
when the Sydney season was over, and found so poor as to be
fit only for sausage. Perhaps they had been poor all along :
perhaps listening night after night to the torturing
blither of the hero, the moans of the heroine, and the
curses of the villain had told on them. Anyhow, it was a
melancholy ending for a flock Of actors who had played
their parts well. The man-actors wind up at Poverty
Point; the sheep-actors comes to an end that, after all,
is not more tragic.
A contributor writes at considerable length to state
that as Mark Hambourg was leaving Melbourne his spec-
tacles fell into the Yarra, but the coating of scum and
dead things on the surface of the alleged water was so
thick that they didn’t sink, and a laborer presently
rescued them.
Napoleon Buonaparte as a stage subject is played down
about as low as he will go in a recent drama called
“ The Exile.” This three-act atrocity shows how the
weary, ailing Boney puts in his time at St. Helena. He
has some mild birthday festivities, and the principal
item on the programme is a review of 10 small kiddies,
armed with toy swords and drums. When he has patted
the heads of the 10 infants they march up the stage and
leave him, and the busted Emperor, striking his favorite
attitude with his chin on his chest, murmurs, “My last
review!” “The Exile,” produced in London, was
rather disrespectfully greeted on the first night. A
voice from the gallery interrupted the simple pathos of
the review episode by calling upon Nap. for a hornpipe.
R. S. Smythe, the much-travelled, began his showman’s
career in ’62, when he left the Melb. Herald to
take a small concert co. to South Africa, India, and the
Chinese ports. He possessed at the time £5OO and a
piano. He once said to present writer : “ I got back
with nix ; but I had learnt the business, which is a diffi-
cult one to learn. I was well satisfied with the trip.”
“ Moose ” : The awful falling-in of a Melbourne paper
re Leon Caron’s scoring of “My Lady Molly ” was a
worse thing than your par. would suggest. When
“ Molly ” was put on, Jones’s scorings had not arrived, so
Caron scored the music himself at the last moment,
assisted by Conolly—of course in excellent style. Caron’s
scoring was hurriedly rehearsed, found to go all right,
and played for the first few nights. By-and-bye a
belated mail brought along Jones’s scoring. It was
played over and found to be a good way behind the
substitute. So, taking out the overture (which,
of course, he had Dot provided), Caron put
aside Jones’s music and went on with his own—
to the joy of all but the daily paper critic.
On 22nd May that thrilling young party stated that,
“ owing to an accident . . . the instrumental score for
the first performances had to be supplied by Mr. Leon
Caron, and very ably indeed they were vamped (HI) by
that sound musician.” (“Sound musician” hereisgood.)
This was bad enough, but the unhappy young man went
on to say that—“ naturally the management desired to
give the composer’s work in its entirety.” And three days
later he went out of his way to remark that it was
“now being performed with all the orchestral effects
of the composer, Mr. Sidney Jones; and the rich
harmonies, which a hasty scoring failed to bring
out, greatly enhance the value of the work” (HI), etc.
Then Leon Caron went out in force and sought for blood.
Caron still “bucks” at the horrid word, “vamped.”
Fancy our only Leon vamping I It is not so many months
back that there was a similar falling-in over the pro-
gramme of a first-rate visiting violinist (Max Schluter),
who, finding himself “slated,” set a trap. He altered
his printed programme in a drastic way one night, but
next day out came the usual remarks as to the “defi-
ciencies ” of the performer in certain pieces (on the
printed programme), which pieces had never been played
at all! Then the kettle boiled over, and great was the
boil thereof.
Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis recited before the Austral Salon
(Melb.), on the anniversary of the Battle of Waterloo,
Disraelffs speech in the House of Commons on the death
of Wellington. No one mentioned that this speech was
a deliberate crib from the address of a French orator, de-
livered a few years previously in connection with the
death of a famous French general. It was Ben. Hayward
who discovered the plagiarism. Dizzy said he had for-
gotten having read the oration in French.
“Harry Musgrove and Co., Musical, Dramatic and
General Agency,” of Argent Chambers, Hunter-street,
is one of the newest shingles hung out in the over-
shingled city of Sydney. Harry Musgrove, long business
manager with J. C. Williamson, now undertakes to book
tours (alas, how often the tour doesn’t pay ex’s, after it
is “ booked” !), to organise concerts (what a pathetic lot
of girls there are who want to get a concert organised !)
to arrange engagements (“ Poverty Point ” is lined three
deep with people who are trying to catch a glimpse of an
arranged engagement on the far horizon !), also to supply
artists for Mrs. Cohen’s “ at home ” and to do all kinds of
theatrical biz. And if all this biz isn’t done to the com-
plete satisfaction of the applicants it may fairly be
assumed that the times are responsible—not Harry
Musgrove. ' J

but, before half the lectures had been delivered Geach
found the gaunt one running to fat on his’hands
and losing caste as one who had suffered much
and starved on locusts and wild honey. Later
Geach with Willoughby, took over the Arnold show
and before any pelf had accumulated the plague
closed up Sydney Criterion and carried off two members
of the co. This blotted out that particular season and
cost Geach over four figures. The plague-driven and
much-disinfected co., after a season in Melbourne, made
foi Maoriland Geach was in advance. The first indivi-
dual to greet him at the Bluff was the income-tax a-'ent
and the last at Auckland to farewell the departing
manager was the income-tax collector! Return! mrtn
Sydney the G.-W Co caught the back-wash of the
drought. Finally Geach was struck by Bent’s railwav

** The Brotherhood of the Good Shepherd”
has taken root inDubbo(N.S. W.) Bishop Camidge
has'given a site for the Home.' The institu 1 ion is
Church of England, and “'brothers ” may be
clergymen or laymen. They must remain un-
married while in the Order. The duties of the
“ brothers ” will be to scour country up to the
Queensland border, gain entrance to shearing
sheds and miners’ camps, and, as opportunity
serves, lay spiritual bait about in their tracks.
Darley, Beauchamp, Hampden, Brassey, and even
warrior Hutton, are all .patrons or something qf
the Order; and, it need hardly be added, the
Order is endeavoring to raise money in England
to shove the enterprise along.
A party of Jap. naval officers were taken
down to the Bellambi coal mine the other day and
the manager of the works spread himself at great
length with a view to impressing the Heathen.
He was somewhat disappointed as the spreading
process went on, to note that the Heathen were
not being impressed to any great extent. When
he had pumped himself dry one of the Chrysan-
themum men smiled sweetly and explained in
tolerable English that the mine required to be
brought up to date, and fluently advised that
manager to go in for certain kinds of new machinery
“that we use in Japan.” Then the party
adjourned for “light.refreshments,” but a flabber-
gasted manager drank his light refreshment neat.
He wanted u to keep him up. t

AN AUTHORITY SAYS.
p
EOPLE cannot understand why Diamonds do not come down in nrice
Unless some new Diamond fields are discovered they will never come
down, and I think that proportionately they will go as much InXr
. ... “ the next ten I™™ a f ‘hey have in the last ten years. The reason
is this: Ten years ago it took less than one-third of the effort to produce
Diamonds as it does to-day. In other words, the strata of blue groundTn the
Kimberley Fields that were then worked were so prolific that Diamonds
were found practically on the top of the earth. After the very prolific strata
were worked out and it was necessary to go down a little deeper into the
earth Diamonds were not quite so plentiful, and to-day it takes at least three
times the effort to produce the same amount of stones it did ten years ago
Pearls are also going up in value at a rapid rate.
A
UNIQUE
INVESTMENT.
AS an investment, Diamonds afford advantages offered by nothing else.
f\ For years they have increased in value at about the rate of 10 per
cent, a year, which makes them as an investment about three times as
good as any bank. When you put your money in the bank someone
else has the use of it; when you put it in Diamonds you yourself have the use
of it, and the pleasure and satisfaction of wearing Diamonds are worth as
much as the returns from the investment. This is no idle statement, written
only with the view of selling our Diamond goods, but actual facts which you
can easily prove to be correct.
We are the onlj* Australian firm having a Warehouse in Hatton Garden,
which place, as everyone knows, is the Diamond Mart of the world. Our
long and careful study of the Stone Market has placed us in the unique posi-
tion of being able to buy more favorably than almost any firm. These
advantages are shared with our customers, while others, if they could buy as
cheaply, would probably retain them as extra profit.

The blundering Bent was forced to admit
the folly of his own administration lately. His
remarkable “weeding” contracts were to save
the Railway Department so many thousands, but
the few who took them, finding they could only
make 2s. a day, promptly threw them up. To a
deputation Bent admitted that, though the weeds
were in many places growing over the lines,
causing the engines to slip, it was nevertheless the
wrong time of year to weed ; and that “it seems
to me we are only wasting our money in doing
this weeding at all.” The trouble is that the
weeds are there : if they are allowed to grow up
the engines crush them into pulp and the effect is
much the same as if the line were greased ; and
whether Bent thinks weeding is waste of money
or not his blessed engine won’t get along with-
out it.

Last Year’s Girl.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wonder where she’s got to —the girl of twelve
months gone ?
Her mouth was sweet and hot, too; her eyes like
beacons shone!
I wonder does she go still per ferry to the Beach ;
I wonder does she glow still to someone’s warm
love-speech !
I guess some fellow’s grabbed her (I’ve got another
girl!)
Ah me ! I often cabbed her with heart and soul
a-whirl.
I wonder does she talk on to him as once to me —
I ponder if they walk on the stars in ecstasy ?
It is a sweet sensation to stride upon the stars ;
To gaze down on creation through Space’s slip-rail
bars !
She used to make me think so; she made the
planets whirl
Before I took to drink so, and lost dear Last
Year’s Girl !
She used to make me fancy I stood aloft in Mars—
What sights a lover can see among the twinkling
stars !
Her hand in mine we rambled across the Milky
Way,
Or lip to lip we scrambled past Jupiter’s bright
ray.
We often sat on Saturn, her dear soft hand in mine
(And oh, her stocking pattern was something
superfine!)
One night our star lurched over and spilt us into
Space;
Such carelessness, Jehovah, amounts to a dis-
grace !
We quarrelled o’er some trifle —I lost my joy and
pearl;
And now I sit me sighful and muse on Last Year’s
Girl.
Heigho ! I’m sad and sorry we parted then—but
no !
There’s Next Year’s Girl for quarry to chase with
heart aglow. •
W.A.
GRANT HERVEY.
On the Tram.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ How long ago it seems,” said Nell, banding me
my ticket, “ yet it can’t be very long.”
“Asa matter of fact,” I replied brutally, for I
still bore her a grudge, “it’s precisely ten years.”
She laughed merrily.
“ How ungallant you are—Jim ! ”
The name came hesitatingly from her lips, and
then softly:
“ What children we were then ! ”
“ In worldly knowledge ! ” I agreed.
Her brows knitted ever so little.
“Of course,” I added hastily, “I am speaking
of my own accomplishments at that interesting
but distant period.”
Perhaps it was my own conceit, but I could
have sworn that her lips trembled.
“Why are you so cruel?” she murmured.
“ You might be a little more kind ! ”
“ If my memory serves me,” I answered, “ that
is precisely what I used to tell you.”
“You forget,” said Nell, reprovingly, “that
my position was slightly different then. At that
time people might have talked.”
“And now ?”
“How absurd you are. How could they—now
that I am married ? ”
“I must apologise for my stupidity,” I said
obstinately ; “but I fail to see that that fact can
possibly have deprived your acquaintances of the
power of speech.”
She looked severely at me, but I was absorbed
in the contemplation of an exceedingly neat shoe.
Nell is, by the way, rather proud of her foot.
“You are,” she said slowly, “either an utter
fool or a perfect actor.”
I bowed my head.
“ The umpire’s decision,” I remarked, “is in all
cases final.”
“Please try to be sensible for one moment,”
said Nell, pityingly. “ What am Ito think ? ”
“You must, on no account,” I remonstrated,
“allow your thoughts to be influenced by me.
Still, I have never previously been accused of
genius.”
“But why?”
“ I will tell you. When a man is disappointed
in love ”
Nell’s smile was distinctly encouraging. ‘ ‘ Were
you ever ? ”
“ I haven’t said so,” I retorted.
“No,” she admitted; “you haven’t said so;
but—er —what was she like ? ”
“I am afraid,” I said sulkily, “that I cannot
describe her. It was so long ago, and I’ve not
seen her since till quite recently.”
There was comparative silence for a moment;
silence, save for the rattle of the tram.
“Do you think,” she asked, tearing her ticket
to shreds as she spoke, “that she still cares?”
“ Who ? ” I asked, absently.
“ The —er —the lady we were discussing.”
“Oh!—well, you see, that is precisely what I
wish to know.” '
She flushed rosily.
“ If she is a woman of taste ” —she began.
“She is ! ” I interrupted with decision.
“Then you may make your mind easy,” said
Nell. “ But I’m afraid I must get off at the next
Stop. By the way, when are you coming out to
see us?.”
“I am content,” I said, resignedly, “to leave
the matter entirely in your hands.”
“ Well —I was thinking,” she said, musingly, as
the tram slowed down; “Jack will be going
sailing on Wednesday—and I hate sailing.”
“ Then I may ?”
“ If you particularly wish it,” she whispered
softly, “you may!”
SPINIFEX.
M.L. Premier Seddon, speaking from
bitter experience:—
He never would be a party to placing the interior
means of communication in the hands of private com-
panies. The Manawatu district had suffered from that,
and he also instanced the Midland railway. There was
no railway constructed by private capital that had not in
the end been inimical to the best interests of the State.
The Bulletin was reading in a Canadian
paper of a handsome new bridge to connect Rich-
mond and South Melbourne, and thought it must
refer to an Australian work until it saw that
“ the municipalities interested had paid the whole
cost.” Then it looked hurriedly for further
details, and found that the bridge is in Quebec
province, Canada.
Now that Russian methods are so popular
with the Tory papers, they may like to know that
in Russia a paper is liable to be suppressed and
have its type broken up and its right of publi-
cation taken away for a certain period, perhaps
forever, “if convicted three times of spreading
information which it knew to be false.” If such a
law were in force here, nearly every daily paper in
Sydney and Melbourne would have gone out of
business by now.
“ Jaybell,” the misanthrope :
Now, that the women of Australia have the franchise,
what are they going to do with the thing ? It won’t cut
up into dress lengths or scald cockroaches, or get the
old man's tea ready, or anything else to make it
worth the average woman’s while keeping it on the
premises. If, by a twist here, a gather there, and the
addition of a ribbon somewhere else, it could inveigle
last year’s millinery into false pretences, the franchise
would have a biased historian in every petticoat, and a
mafficker in every skirt. The kind of franchise woman
really needs is one that will catch mice, answer the front
door, eject the sewing-machine canvasser, look after the
sponge cake in the oven, serve as a hairpin, and keep
her skirt from blowing around on a windy day, and, if
the Constitution can’t supply that, the Constitution had
better be altered pretty sudden.
Dr. Morrison, London Times Pekin cor-
respondent, reports that Britain is getting left in
Northern China, and that the French, Russians,
Germans, and Belgians are snapping up all
the concessions. This is about the fourth time
within the last few years that China has been
partitioned industrially on paper; but no one
seems to get much forrarder. And all the time
the Chow goes on building modern arsenals and
learning to make modern artillery (a recent cable
reported that 80 odd arsenals were working day
and night turning out equipment for some army
or other), and to use magazine rifles. If only the
right kind of leader arises, the cosmopolitan
foreign devil in China will one day leave without
even waiting to pack his carpet bag, let alone
dispose of his “concession.”
Belfast Flax Spinners’ Association has
started to systematically prosecute for fraud those
spinners who supply alleged linen that is really
mostly cotton, and thereby destroy British export
trade by bringing British goods into disrepute.
On this subject the Age remarks :
If Victoria possessed a properly constituted Chamber
of Commerce an appreciation of its duties to the public to
protect them against frauds of this character might be
expected. In Great Britain, India and other centres of
English trade these chambers are peculiarly active in
hunting down frauds of this character. In Melbourne
the hunt is for subscriptions, and, as long as these are
forthcoming, no questions are asked as to whether the
business of the subscribers is conducted on honest lines
or the reverse. The bitter resentment expressed against
the Customs because this department tries to protect the
revenue and the public is an apt illustration of our com-
mercial morality. It is but fair in the interests of the
community that the Federal Government should at an
early date place a Merchandise Marks Act on the statute
book. Such a law at present exists in Victoria, but its
administration is carried out so perfunctorily that it is
practically a dead letter. The exposure of such frauds
on the public is being actively followed up in England at
present, and it is time something was done in Australia
to protect the public against the rapacity of “ firms of
the highest respectability” which are profiting so largely
from textile and other adulterations.
The S.A. Commonwealth representatives
have already sorted themselves out to suit the
seven districts into which Sheriff Boothby has
divided the State. The choice is as follows:
Adelaide, Customs Minister Kingston ; Port Ade-
laide, Labor Member Batchelor; East Torrens
(the third city seat), Solomon; Chamberlain (Mid
North), Speaker Holder; Angas (Lower North),
Glynn ; Flinders (the biggest constituency m Aus-
tralia, comprising the whole of the We3t and fax
North, including the Norther n Terntory,
Boynton; Barker (the South and South-East),
Bonython.
Vie. Women’s Political Association, the
other day, agreed on the following platform in
Federal politics:—
Social.—Equal marriage and divorce laws, equal paren-
tal rights over children, Federal Food Adulteration Act,
Federal Old Age Pensions Act.
Industrial.—Equal pay for equal work, Federal Fac-
tories Aot, Federal Conciliation and Arbitration Act,
Federal Patents, Copyrights, and Inventions Act.
Financial.— Federalising railways, federalising State
debts, Federal Life Assurance Department.
If the women had added “No more pawning of
our country to Cohen ” it would have been a fine,
sensible polioy.

“ on the soul-saving industry :
Was stopped near Alexandria Park, Sydney, last night
at 10 o’clock by a lanky, consumptive-looking man who
asked me'to attend a mission tent on the Park, where an
“ after service prayer-meeting” was held from 10 p.m.
till midnight. I pointed out that it was very cold and
breezy, and the struggle for a crust forced me to leave my
bed at 5 a.m., so I naturally required a few hours’ rest.
He assured me with madness glaring out of his eyes that
he had not had rest or food for the past five davs and would
neither eat nor sleep till he had gathered for the Lord a
dozen pure white souls which would ensure him a seat
amongst the blessed. I jumped off the footpath and ran
and refused to stop when a policeman hailed me a little
farther up the street. I think he grabbed that policeman
for I heard a shriek come down on the wind A police-
man’s soul is sure to be a nice clean white one
One Buckeridge, who returned with the
ship Morning .after a spell with the Antarctic
exploring expedition, has been lecturing in Maori-
land after this fashion;
Speaking of the streng th of blizzards in that region, he
said the first that struck the ship after reaching winter
quarters, besides doing a lot of other damage, blew away
a five ton anchor through the ship’s rails. They found in
making tests of the effect of the cold that a bucket of
water would more quickly become solid ice than a bucket
of ice on a big fire would melt. A little kitten they had
2° tu 6 / or ’ w h en placed on the bulwarks, was frozen to
death in 2min. 7sec. It was dangerous to take quick
breaths during the extreme cold. A lieutenant of the
company had done so, and his wind-pipe became frozen,
then turning his head he had cracked it, and it had not
healed since.
The Wayside Pub.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Good ! here’s a wayside pub.—the Horse and
Jockey—
A bench before it and the woods behind.
Come, comrades, let’s exchange life’s roadway
rocky >
For wheresoe’er the cup may lead mankind.
Here a glad space, my masters, will we halt
And blithe anticipate the miles with malt.
For life should—like our literature—begin
With the suggestion of the sunlit sign ;
And was not Chaucer’s Tabard but an inn
Whereof the memory doth the heart incline
To recollect how all the goosequill tribe
Hath ever pubs, delighted to describe ?
Shakspeare’s great self was moved to maddest
mirth
Where Falstaff’s fooling shook the old Boar’s
Head,
And Scott, from a Black Bear in “ Kenilworth,”
His living legions to adventure led ;
From the Pied Merlin did Doyle’s Company
trudge,
And what without its Maypole is “ B. Rudge” ?
Fielding is but of inns a wild procession,
And the Three Pigeons well Noll Golds uithknew,
And gave not Thackeray, in lonely session,
A treasured tribute to the tavern, too—
It s in the Ballad of the Bouillabaisse
Born of that inn “in comfortable case.”
There s Shenstone’s verse which Dr. Johnson
quoted,
Who made the little fishes talk like whales ;
And at a Wayside Inn it was that noted
Down Longfellow, those pleasant Travellers’Tales;
And Lawson’s thirsty page is nowise scanty
In tribute to Australia’s b.tckblox shanty.
I know not if the inn’s been sung by Homer,
Unless, indeed, the Lotos Land was one ;
But everybody’s well aware that Omar
With praise of Persian pubs is never done.
And now, for sure enough, my memory’s lent,
To vindicate my verse with precedent,
Enough to make my tankard sparkle somewhat,
More than without these names my tankard might:
Shame on those sourly-slow whose pulses drum not
Swifter at mention of the men who write—
These mighty men of learning and of worth,
Who from so many pubs did issue forth !
N.S.W.
MOUSQUETAIRE.
Hawley’s Part.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Chisholm affair began in the most trivial
way. There were three at the table —Chisholm,
his wife Ella, and Hawley, their friend. Con-
versation had drooped, and in one of the casual
silences, Chisholm pulled out of his pocket a
tobacco-pouch of unique design—just the sort of
thing to appeal to the vanity of a man of
Chisholm’s temperament.
“ What do you think of this?” he asked. “ A
present from "Crane last week —not so bad, eh ? ”
And Chisholm passed it across to Hawley, who
examined it critically and appraisingly before
giving his opinion.
“Oh —so-so !” observed Hawley, returning the
pouch to its owner, who placed it in his pocket
again, quite satisfied with Hawley’s “ faint
praise”—coming from Hawley. Then the wife
broke in
“Now, that is too bad of you, Jack! You
have had that present all this time, and this is
the first I have heaid of it.” Turning to Hawley,
she continued, and there was keen resentment in.
her voice, “That is just my husband all
Mr. Hawley. He never shows me anything, or
tells me anything. I find things out by chance,
when his friends come to see him. I am just a
nobody in his estimation. Would you treat a
wife in that way ? ”
Chisholm stared across the table in blank amaze-,
ment. Not the reproach, which he thought
childish ; but the tone in which it was uttered,
the feeling, as it were, of malice, and the disre-
gard of the presence of a third person, to say
nothing of the appeal to that third person, caused
the husband to gaze in a wondering, uncompre-
hending way at his wife. It was a new and quite
startling experience in their domestic relations.
He waited to hear what Hawley would say, and
not without concern.
One could never be sure of Hawley. He would
be flippant and irrelevant when, in all conscience,
he ought to be serious, or he would be as grave as
a mummy when you expected nonsense from him.
With Ella Chisholm’s eyes still interrogating
him, Hawley hesitated, and critically regarded
the pattern of the tablecloth.
“ Why don’t-you answer me ? ” demanded Mrs.
Chisholm, impatiently. “ Don’t you think it
wrong of my husband ? ”
“I was thinking,” said Hawley, slowly and
without raising his eyes, “of a Russian proverb—
I think it’s Russian —about the risk run by the
third dog that interferes in a fight between two
other dogs. Do you know,” he inquired, meeting
Mrs. Chisholm’s steady gaze, “why I have
remained a bachelor ? ”
“But what has that to do with the way my
husband treats me?” she interrupted, and that
new tone in her voice, still persistent, gave the
husband a curiously disagreeable sensation.
“It has this to do with it; the dread of a
reproach like yours, to old Jack, here”—and
Hawley nodded towards his friend. Chisholm
felt inclined to reach over and slap Hawley on
the back, whilst his wife considered him a brute.
Hawley should . now have stopped. That,
indeed, was his intention, but Ella Chisholm’s
eyes were fixedly and staringly interrogative
still, and compelled him, in spite of himself, to go
on. He blundered along, and only sheer good
luck, and not his wit, preserved him from the
fate of the third and interfering “dog.”
Chisholm, silent all the while, stared hard at the
table, wondering if Hawley had taken too much.
Finally, the latter wound up sundry promiscuous
observations by saying—
“ Speaking as a bachelor, I hold matrimony to
be. an eternal blunder- well, not exactly that—
it’s a clumsy way of putting it ”
Chisholm looked puzzled and angry. Ella,
persismut and petulant, broke in—“ MiyTiawley,
I want to know whether you think Jack’s treat-
ment of me is right or not.”
“I am coming to that,” answered Hawley,
beginning to feel uncomfortable ; “I was going
to say something that would amuse you ; but you
want me to give my opinion of Chisholm’s
“That will do, Hawley. Drop it. My wife
had a row with the servant this morning, and got
the worst of it. That’s the real trouble. Ella,
sing that new song of yours. It will suit
Hawley.”
The te:: ion relaxed, and the situation, for the
present, was saved.
In their bedroom that night Chisholm reverted
to the subject..
“That was a foolish remark of yours this even-
ing, Ella. .What has Hawley to do with your
feelings, and my conduct as a husband ? ”
“So it went home, then?” came the prompt
retort, and there was a note of triumph in the
voice.
“No, it did not go home —in the sense you
mean.” He spoke quietly and gently. “But I
was thinking of your self-respect.”
“ Mr. Hawley agrees with me, and sympathises
with me,” came the reply. “I could read it in
his eyes.”
“ I want an early breakfast in the morning. It
will be ready, I suppose?” He spoke now with
sharp peremptoriness.
“Yes, of course—if Jane knows.”
“She will know if you tell her.”
Ella turned a quick glance upon her husband,
but his face was averted in taking down a book
to read.
“I want to be away by 8 o’clock,” he added,
quickly.
* * * »
When Chisholm returned to his home the next
day he brought with him from the city a bunch
of violets. He had purchased also a briar-wood
pipe. He gave the violets to his wife, who
smiled on the gift, and was on the point of
kissing her husband when he took the new pipe
from his pocket. “I bought this to-day, Ella,;’,
he said. “I thought you might like to know!”
The violets were instantly forgotten. Ella grew
red with indignation. So her feeling about
Crane’s present had been construed into mere,
disappointed curiosity. Her husband was a fool.
He had no understanding '
“ Show it to Mr. Hawley. "He will know how
to praise it,” she said, quttingly, and marched out
of the room, leaving the violets on the table.
Chisholm looked perplexed and hurt for a
moment, then his glance fell ori the pipe, lying on
the table. He picked it up, and turned it over in
his hand. A placid smile broke over his features.
“I’ll tell Hawley I got it for half-a-crown .”
A few days later Hawley was again at
Chisholm’s house. During the evening, when
Chisholm had left the room to go and chain up
his dog, Hawley said to Mrs. Chisholm, “I
hardly knew what to say the other night, but you
know my sympathies were entirely with you.
Chisholm is naturally cold and undemonstrative
—he is my friend, and all that—but that doesn’t
prevent me from seeing that he is of a very selfish
nature, only concerned about ”
Chisholm just then returned to the room.
Later, the two men being left together smoking,
Chisholm broke a long silence to remark, “My
wife can’t get over that little affair of the tobacco-
pouch last week . . . you know ? She is hurt
that I didn’t tell her about it before showing it to
you. She has sulked ever since. Extraordinary,
isn’t it ? ”
“Just a woman’s way—she’ll turn round in a
day or two. I wouldn’t fret about it,” answered
Hawley carelessly.
“I don’t want to fret about it. Of course I
thought it would pass off next day. I tried to
make it up, but it was no go. I have hurt her
feelings seriously in some way that I don’t under-
stand. As you say, I suppose, it will pass off, and
This is a capital briar-root.”
“ Wilson has sent up to ask if you will see him
for a minute or two,” said Mrs. Chisholm, coming
into the room.
Hawley was particularly struck by the hard
voice, and the omission of the customary “ Jack,”
and whistled long and low under his breath.
Left alone with Hawley, the frowning expression
on Ella Chisholm’s countenance changed to a
winning smile.
‘‘ I knew ycu understood and sympathised
with me, Mr. Hawley,” she began. “Jack
doesn’t; he understands his dog better than he
does me.” The smile went, as the husband’s
returning step was heard.
“Come and have a look at the dog, Hawley,
before you go. I’m going to enter her for the
show.”
Hawley gave Ella Chisholm an understanding
and sympathetic glance as he passed out. Rather
to Hawley’s surprise when saying good-night,
Chisholm offered his company for a short
distance. t
The two men proceeded in the darkness for
some paces, in silence. Then Chisholm took hold
of Hawley’s arm—an unusual thing, and Hawley
involuntarily shrank from the touch, but the grip
tightened.
“Look here, Tom,” (“Tom” also was unusual)
“going back to what I was saying inside. You
seem to have a good deal of influence with Ella.
Try and brighten her up a bit for me. She is
generally in good spirits when you are abobt. I
confess she puzzles me —this sympathy business
she harps upon so much. Every look she gives
me now is a stab. What she “wants I really don’t
know. I would make her happy if I. could —you
know that, Tom,/ I feel quite beaten over it.”
Chisholm paused.
“There was a time when I could please her and
make her happy-*-1 thought so, at any rate.”
Hawley felt the twitch of the fingers on his arm.
There was another and longer pause.
“I sometimes wonder, old man, whether Ella
did not make a mistake in marrying me.”
Hawley received a shock ; freeing his arm from
the other’s gx - asp, he began rapidly : “ What non-
sense you’re talking, man—your wife would not
exchange you for any man living, take my word
for it. And, look here, don’t talk to any other
man in the way you’ve been speaking to me.
Good night.” Tapping Chisholm on the shoulder,
he hurried across the street.
“Hawley—Tom, don’t go like that.” But
Hawley only waved his hand, and kept on his
way. “ I suppose I have made a fool of myself,
and lie’s gone off rather than tell me so,” Chisholm
exclaimed, shamefacedly, to himself.
Hawley had been in the habit of dropping in at
Chisholm’s about once a week. His visits now
became more freauent, and Chisholm—cold, un-
demonstrative Cnisholm—was grateful to his
friend for bringing back to his wife’s face the
smiles and gaiety that properly belonged to her.
* * * *
Chisholm, returning home one afternoon earlier
than usual, found Hawley taking tea with his
wife. He did not notice that she seemed more
glad than usual to see him, and that her eyes
sought his with an eager yearning, nor that a
softness filled them when he looked in her direc
tion. The evening passed very quietly. Once
w hen Chisholm turned to her, he found his wife’t

Molly greeted her master with a noisy welcome

“ To Jack.”

For an hour he sat there, with his hands in his

carpet. The dog was nestled up at his feet.
Rousing at last, Chisholm took the dog on to his
knees,
“Well, Molly, there’s only you and I now;
she hadn’t much regard for you; but there’s no
one to interfere now. We shall have it all to
ourselves.” The look of irresolution was gone.
He looked like a man who had been stricken
badly in a fight, but was determined to go on.
The next morning, as he was having breakfast,
he said to the servant: “ Your mistress won’t be
back for some time. I suppose she told you
before going.”
“No, sir, she didn’t say nothing,” answered
Jane.
Chisholm went to his office as usual, and every
day thereafter proceeded to the city, transacted
his business, and returned in the evening, reading
late into the night, the dog always at his feet.
On the third day, he heard casually that Hawley
had gone to Europe. He put his wife entirely out
of his thoughts. On the seventh evening—it was
Saturday—Chisholm had been in his bedroom
for some time, reading, when suddenly the
spaniel, who was on his knee, gave a low growl,
Chisholm looked up. In the doorway his wife
was standing. For a moment his heart stood
still, as though he were looking at a ghost.
Recovering himself quickly, he rose, and said
quietly, “What is it, Ella? What do you want
here?”
“I have come home, Jack. I want you to hear
me, and, if you believe my story, Jack, I
want to ask your forgiveness. If you don’t
believe me, you—you—’’(Ella looked at a point
above the mantelpiece where it had always hung)

As the sound died away, Ella look up at her
husband.
“Do you believe me, Jack?” she asked,
faintly.
“ Do you believe me, Jack ? ” she repeated.
Chisholm reflected that he had been very lonely
the last few evenings. He knew, too, in his
heart, that his wife was speaking the truth.
“ Yes,” he said ; “I believe you !”
SPHINX.
The Cow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The base ingratitude of the cow, and its progeny,
is notorious amongst cattlemen. I don’t think
there is any animal in creation so ready to return
evil for good as the bull family in general. I see
in Bulletin (23/5/’O3) where some wharf-lumpers
rescued a bullock from a flood, and four of them
were promptly knocked into the water by the
ungrateful beast. Now this sort of thing happens
every time, and if a man ever does a kindness to a
cow, the best thing he can do is to climb a tree
immediately afterwards, for a cow can’t climb a
tree, if it’s moderately upright in all its dealings
(the tree, I mean—not the cow ; it couldn’t be
upright if it tried).
When I was on the Towers in the early days I
was camped near the Talisman reef, on the bank
of a gully, that had been worked for alluvial.
About 25 per cent, of our time was devoted to
helping people to pull up horses that had fallen
down old shafts, or tilling up the holes that had
dead horses in them. Then a man started a dairy
in the vicinity, and, about the second day, he
came round wanting us to give him a hand to pull
a calf out of a hole. The four of us went, without
a murmur, although the thing was becoming a
trifle monotonous. He called at several other
camps on the way until we mustered about 16.
Then he wanted to go to another camp to
get some more help, but we protested that
there were enough of us there to eat any
ordinary-sized calf, so we went on to the hole,
where he had shear-legs erected and tackle ready.
He went down and made the rope fast and we
hauled for all we were worth. At last a four-
year-old bullock hove in sight and was safely
landed. No sooner did it feel the solid ground
under its feet than jt gave a bellow and charged
straight at us, where we were all in a string. It
would have scooped the whole lot, I believe, if
there had not been another shaft, between us and
it, down which it pitched head first and broke its
neck.
On another occasion I saw a bullock, on the
opposite side of the river to my house, lying on its
back in the water, where it had rolled down the
bank. It was unable to get round on to its feet.
I got a rope, went over in a boat, and after a lot
of exertion managed to pull it over so that it could
get up. As soon as it got on its feet it bellowed
and charged straight at me. I sprinted for the
boat and just managed to push off as the beast
was trying to gather me up on its horns It
plunged into the water after me and swain for
about 300yds. up the stream in pursuit of the boat
bellowing and snorting as if it thirsted for mv
life’s blood. If any reader knows anything that
excels the cow in base ingratitude I would like to
hear the particulars.
N.S.F.
My Letters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Each night when I go posting
Warm letters to my Love,
Behold ! a royal hosting
Of stars goes too, by Jove !
With one young star a-gleaming
Upon my arm to port,
And one to starboard beaming,
We caper and cavort.
Nine billion stars go sailing
All vanguardwise ahead ;
With ropes of song a-traihng,
We march moon-chapletted.
A band of gods plays gaily
On lip-sweet harps above,
And strong young stars beat daily
Blows on the drums of Love.
On sun-wheeled bikes a-coasting
Come rear-guard stars a-whirl
When I (hooray !) go posting
Sweet letters to my girl.
The “Office” gleameth golden
With pearly lamps unspent,
In envelope close-folden
I cast Love’s implement.
We lift a postal chorus
Against the quaking sky,
And shake the cud of Taurus
Erom out his jaw, forbye.
One jovial star a-clasping
Each arm o’ mine we tread,
A thyrsus-dance, a-rasping
The tomb-rust off the dead.
With clank and clink and jingle,
With whips of salty song,
We make the earth’s hide tingle,
The sparkling hours along.
Then home —0, home we travel,
My young white stars and me—
O ho ! we scratch the gravel
And part in ecstasy.
Then lo ! the stars go ghosting
Through far-olf glen and grove
Till next I go a-posting
Big letters to my Love !

“ Pefcramos: Was once behind when the Woods-
Williamson Co. was playing “ Hands Across the Sea” at
Adelaide. “ Hands Across ” is a fine drama for Woods
as there is “an escape from prison ” scene, wherein he
has a chance to knock down 14 French bobbies with a
single biff. The author, however, rules that he must
get arrested towards the end of the scene, but on this
particular night, Woods, having got over the prison wall,
and the lightning change to the “ exterior of the prison ”
having taken place, no one seemed particularly anxious
to come and arrest him. Seeing him severely punching
the air and rushing round, in a circle instead of making
off down the street, as any ordinary prison breaker
would have done, someone let down the rag. Another
someone promptly hoisted it again. After it had gone up
and down some six times Woods was at last discovered
surrounded by a small army of gendarmes—arrested at
last. The stage manager and not the law was to blame.
The poor sups. Couldn’t get to Woods because there was
no entrance left after the lightning change scene had
been effected. When the minions of the law did make
an entrance it was probably through the gaol ventilators.
“ G.” : Saw a peripatetic sword-swallower performing
in the street t’other day. His “ manager ” took up a
collection at each stand, and gathered a crowd by the
furipus ringing of a spavined dinner-bell and the lifting
up of a voice like the cries that come out of purgatory :
“ Now then, boys, come along, sixpence more an’ ’e’ll
swallow the lot. Come along, gents ; who’ll give a tanner
to see ’im swallow six swords four feet long ? Fourpence
more an’’e’ll swallow the lot,” &c., &c. The fourpence
wasn’t forthcoming, and the show dragged. A small boy
asked the manager if the performer wouldn’t swallow
the swords unless he got the fourpence. “No, gents;
must ’ave fourpence before ’e can perform.” Then up
rose a burly contributor with beer and blood in his eye :
“Then give me back my dashed tuppence.” Those
swords were swallowed right there.
“ Sounding Board ” : I want to say a word about
musical critics, one or two of whom I know by sight.
Was at a concert in Sydney Town Hall. At the interval
these critics trooped in behind tha scenes to personally
interview the artists, whose musical capabilities are
supposed to be their whole concern. Later on, some of
the singers re-appeared andjclu tered round one or two
of these “ critics’’—the prettier the artiste the more she
seemed to cluster. I have been told, possibly t>y persons
of dubious reliability, that X of this paper and Z of that
one have their favorite artistes, not always of striking
ability, and that concert managers do well to engage
these artistes, in order to put X and Z in good humor,
the difference in the notice when the favorite artiste is
engaged being quite visible—according to my dubious
informant. Anyhow a critic who wishes to be considered
fair should avoid acquaintance with the people he
criticises. If this is not possible, he need not let them
cluster in public. Clustering is much pleasanter, too,
when done in comparative seclusion.
The loves of two leading members of a certain theatri-
cal company provide lots of entertainment for other
people behind the scenes. The frequent quarrels of the
amorous pair are dramatic in their intensity, and then
the reconciliations O, the reconciliations ! Middle-
aged Strephon and youthful Phyllis were on mutual
“ strike,” the other week, when friend Snapshot, of the
illustrated press, came along with his camera to get
photos of the company—dressing-room studies, green-
room groups, and so forth. He found Phyllis m low
spirits. Shp had no heart for being photographed unless
they could be taken together. Snapshot, the ambas-
sador of love, went to Streph’s dressing-room and ex-
plained the situation, and, after a faint pretence of
indifference, the happy man consented to reconcile “the
damsel would come and adore him in his den. Then
Snapshot took the message to Phyllis, vvho flew to the
arms of her one and only, and as they glued their ups
together in a lingering kiss, Snapshot photographed
them.
A very old performance of “ Othello ” was that given
by an actor, or alleged actor, named Kemble at Windsor
(N.S.W.) in the early fifties. Kemble had no company,
but he advertised his show vigorously, and some kind of
an audience rolled up at the old court-house, where the
bench did duty as a stage. Kemble emerged from the
magisterial room with one side of his face black ana
commenced a dialogue between Othello and lago. hue
reciting Othello he turned the dark sl^ e .f f hls
ance to the audience. The public stood it fo !! ab b u J- h''
minutes; then without waiting to see what Desdemona
would be like, it threw almost everything portable at the
show. The co. left by the back door, and fled with One-
half of its face black, across the South Creek bridge,
and vanished into the bush, Kemble dropped out of
stage life after that, and ended his days selling matches
in Sydney streets. .

“T.D.”: In his “Breaking of the Drought” melo
drama, Blandolt gives an excellent picture of Paddy’s
Market, Sydney, from the scene-painter’s point of view*
Buildings, sleepy horses in stationary vegetable carts,
yawning old lady at a coffee-stall, the early morning
drunk, and the ravenous assemblage of kids, are all as
they should be—yet there is one thing wanting. Bland-
olt has forgotten the Chinamen. Paddy’s Market
without the all-important Chow bargaining over his
cabbagee is Waterloo without Napoleon. In the days
when I used to take an early morning stroll through
Patrick’s bazaar, there used to be a quaint old Chow with
a fat front and a beautiful long silken beard, who acted
as a sort of father confessor and adviser-in-chief to all his
compatriots, and gave himself the airs of an emperor.
Now, if Blandolt could only have engaged that highly-
respected Confucian.
Who was the first fiend to bring in that Hall horror,
the “turn” done by two alleged male comedians?
The brothers Buncombe —Bob and Bill—enter and stand
facing each other. They are togged out in an insane
fashion and lean on heavy crutch-handled sticks. Brother
Bob bangs his stick on the floor, and apropos to nothing
at all observes, “T was a-comih* erlong th’ street.” Here
Brother Bill chips in, like a Greek chorus, and says, “ Oh,
you vas a-comin’ arlong th‘ street.” Bob replies, “ Yes, I
was a-comin’ erlong George-street.” Bill: “Erlong
George-street. Well?” Bob: “When all at wonst I
met a man.” Bill: “You met a man?” Bob:
“Yes, sir, a man cornin’ in the opposite direction.” Bill
(with the air of a man who scents a State secret) “Oh!
you met a man cornin’ in the hopposite direction. Well,
wot then?” Bob: “I was carryin’ a new-laid hegg.”
Bill: “A new-laid hegg.” Bob: “I said a new-laid
hegg.” Bil': “ And 1 said a new-laid hegg.” Bob : “ Sir,
I said I said a new-laid hegg.” Bill : “ Yes ; I said I said I
said a new-laid hegg. Well?” Bob: “I said to him
‘ You’re a fool !’ " Bill : “ What! You said to him that I
was a fool, did yer? Take that!” (Here Bill bashes
Bob’s hat over his eyes.) Bob (from the depths of the
hat): “ No, no ; I honly said to him ‘ You’re a fool, that’s
all. Just you’re a fool.” Bill: “Say it again, will yer?
Take that!” (Subjects Bob to gross personal ill-usage
amid shrieks of laughter from an audience that would
sit out the “ Comedy of Errors” without a smile.)
Pinero’s earliest problem play was “The Profligate,”
which differed from “ Tanqueray” in its bearings on the
duty of the sexes. The Profligate’s giddy bachelor past
accounted for all the trouble in his married life, but
Pinero couldn’t make a convincing character of the
respectable penitent sinner. The play had merely a
success of novelty in England, nor was it any good to
Brough and Boucicault in Australia.
